Having completed for you, O Sons, this book, which is the last of medical science with its complement, and having reached the end thereof from its expositions and declarations, it seemed to me that I should complete it for you with this Treatise which is a part of operation, that is surgery with the hand.
Since operation with the hand is prostrated in our region and time utterly private, so that perhaps the knowledge of it has perished, and its trace cut off; and there remain from it no descriptions except small ones in the books of the ancients, which hands have changed: and error and hesitation have happened to them until its intentions were closed and its force elongated. It seemed to me that I should revive it with the arrangement of this Treatise therein according to the way of exposition, declaration and abbreviation; and that I should come with forms of instruments of the Cautery and the other working instruments, with what is done from the addition of declarations and from its hastening which is necessary.
But the cause for which a good craftsman of it with his hand is not found in our time is because the art of medicine is long, and the author thereof must exercise himself before that in the knowledge of anatomy as Galen narrated until he knows the joints of the members and their forms, and their conjunction and separation, and has knowledge of bones and nerves and sinews and their number and their egress, and arteries pulsatile and quiet, and places of their exit.
And on account of that, Hippocrates said that a name with a name is much, and with work it is little, and especially in the art of the hand.
We indeed have already spoken of him at the beginning of this book.
For he who is not knowing what we said of anatomy is not purged, but rather falls into error by which men are killed: just as I have seen many of those who are formed in this knowledge and boast themselves thereof without knowledge and without experiment.
For this reason happened to me to see a stupid doctor cut open a scrofulous abscess on the neck of a woman and he cut into some arteries in her neck, so that blood flowed from the woman until she fell dead between his hands.
And I saw another doctor who presumed to extract a stone from a man who was advanced in age, and the stone was large. He therefore came upon him and extracted it along with a piece of the bladder. Therefore the man died on the third day.
And I myself had already been summoned to extract it and I saw from the size of the stone and the condition of the sick man that he would die.
And I saw another doctor who was receiving a fee from a certain Duke in our land for medicine: and it happened that a black eunuch who was with him suffered a fracture in his leg near the heel: and the doctor hastily, with his ignorance, bound the fracture over the wound with bandages and strong binding straps and did not allow the wound to breathe.
Then he discharged him according to his wishes. Afterwards he dismissed him after several days and ordered him not to loosen the bandage: and it remained so until his leg and foot became abscessed and he came to ruin.
I was therefore summoned to him and I hurried to loosen the bandage and there was relief and he escaped from his pains.
Nevertheless the corruption had already been confirmed in the limb and I could not restrain it.
Put therefore the corruption ceased to spread in the limb until it died.
And I saw another doctor who perforated a cancerous abscess and it became ulcerated afterwards until the malice of it was magnified with its master.
For this reason: when cancer is pure from melancholic humor then it is not necessary to approach it with iron at all unless it is in a limb which can tolerate it so that the whole of it may be cut off.
And for that reason O sons, it is necessary for you to know that operation with the hand is divided into two divisions: namely, operation which is associated with health; and operation with which there is danger in many dispositions.
And I have already aroused in every place of this book, in which comes operation, in which there is terror and fear.
Therefore it is necessary for you to be cautious of that and moderate it so that fools do not find a way to speak and dishonor.
Therefore receive the measure with solicitude and protection, and your infirm ones with ease and firmness, and use the better way leading by God's grace to salvation and praiseworthy succession; and dismiss terrible illnesses of difficult healing; and lift your souls from that which makes you fear so that hesitation may enter upon you in your faith and well-being.
For it is more lasting for your glory and more sublime in this world, and finally for your quantities.
For Galen already said in some of his admonitions. Do not treat a bad illness lest you be called a bad doctor.
Now however I have divided this Treatise according to three Chapters.
Chapter One.
On Cautery with fire, and Cautery with sharp medicine, chaptered and ordered from head to foot, and the forms of cautery instruments and tools; and all that is necessary in the operation.
Chapter Two.
On incision and perforation, phlebotomy and venesection, and wounds: and the extraction of arrows and similar things; and wholly chaptered and ordered according to the forms of instruments.
Chapter Three.
On restoration and dislocation and the cure of torsion and similar things; chaptered and ordered from head to foot, and according to the forms of instruments.
But before I recall the operations performed with it, it is necessary to say what are the qualities of its cauterizations and their harms; and in what constitution they manifest.
I say, therefore, that speech concerning the quality of cautery's cauterization and its harm is a long and subtle science and hidden secret.
Indeed, many wise men have spoken on this subject, and they have disagreed; and it is sufficient for me to say a little from their words, fearing prolixity.
I say, therefore, that cautery generally suits every constitution which is with matter and without matter; except for two constitutions, which are a hot constitution without matter, and a dry constitution without matter.
However, in the hot and dry constitution with matter they disagreed. For some said that cautery is beneficial in it. And others said the opposite of that, namely, that cautery is not suitable in a disease which is from heat and dryness; because the nature of fire is heat; and it is inconvenient for something hot and dry to heal a disease which is hot and dry.
And he who said the opposite of that said: that through cautery benefit is done in every hot and dry disease which occurs in the bodies of men; because when you compare the body of a man and its humors to the complexion of fire, you find the body of a man cold.
And I indeed say through his speech: because experience has already revealed that to me many times.
However, it is not fitting that he should come upon this unless he has already been proven and trained in the last chapter of the practice of cautery, and has learned the diversity of human temperaments, and the disposition of diseases within themselves, and their causes, and their incidents, and the time period of them.
But there is no fear over you from the remaining complexions; and especially in cold and moist illnesses. For all physicians have now agreed on them, and they have not disagreed on the benefit of cautery in them.
And know, sons, that from the secret of healing by cautery with fire, and its goodness over cautery with a burning medicine, is because fire is simple, whose action does not reach except to the limb which you are cauterizing, and it does not harm another limb joined with it, except for a small injury.
But the action of burning medicine reaches that which extends from the limbs, and perhaps causes a difficulty in healing to occur in the limb, and perhaps destroys it.
But fire, because of its subtlety and goodness of its substance, does not do that unless it overflows.
Now indeed it has become clear to us through experiment, because of the length of service and study with art, and steadfastness upon the truth of things, and because of that I excuse myself from the length of speech.
And if it were not so, because it is not permitted for this book of mine, I would bring to you the hidden secret in fire, and the quality of its operation in bodies through its expulsion, by which it expels illnesses with demonstrative philosophical speech, which would agree with your minds.
And know, sons, that they themselves disagree on the time in which it is fitting for cautery to be done, and they posit a better time according to truth.
And I say that cautery is fitting at all times; therefore the harm which occurs because of time is submerged in the benefit, which is attracted with cautery. And especially if the cautery is for pains from necessary pains, strong, prompt, which do not tolerate delay: because it is feared that from them, a harm may happen, which is greater than a small injury incurred through time.
And let it not fall into your minds, O sons, that which the common and foolish physicians do not fear, namely that to the sickness which is healed by cautery, there is not always a return; and they posit that as a consequent, and the thing is not as they think: because cautery is nothing but a medicine in its place, which alters the complexion and dries up the humors which are the causes why pains happen.
Truly however cautery surpasses medicine through the swiftness of its health and the power of its operation and the vehemence of its dominion: and it is possible that sickness may return at some hour according to the complexion of the weak person, and the location of his sickness, and its power; and what is prepared in his body from an aggregation of superfluities therein; and what comes upon him in acquisition from foods and similar things from those causes unless sickness arises in which cautery is administered.
Sickness subtle in a limb of few superfluities and humors, such as cautery of a tooth, because of pain and similar things to it, for it is possible that the pain may not return in it. But that happens in few cases.
But truly the common saying again, that cautery is the last of medicine, is a right statement: not to that which they intend in it; because they believe that there is no cure which agrees with medicine nor with any other after the setting of the cautery.
The matter is however contrary to that and the intention, that cautery is the last of medicine, is not except when we administer modes of cures in some sicknesses, and those medicines do not agree: then we administer cautery at the end of the matter, and it agrees; then from there it falls, that cautery is the last of medicine, not according to the intention which the common people and many foolish physicians understand.
The ancients said that a cautery with gold is better than a cautery with iron, and they did not say this except because of the equality of gold and the subtlety of its substance; and they said that it does not make pus the place of the cautery. But that is not absolutely true: for I have now proved it and found that it does not do so in some bodies without others.
And a cautery with it is good and better than with iron, as they said. However, when you ignite a cautery in fire made of gold, it is not declared to you when it is ignited according to the quantity which you wish, because of the redness of the gold; and when cold rushes towards it, and if you add ignition upon it, it melts in the fire and turns into sheets.
Therefore the craftsman falls from it into occupation; and because of that a cautery with iron is made among us faster and closer, from straightness to the operation.
And I myself have now arranged this chapter on the cautery according to chapters; and I have arranged them from the head down to the feet so that it may relieve one seeking that which he wants from it.
Chapter II.
Cauterization here is directed at the dominion of humidity and coldness over the brain; which are two causes of headache, and abundance of flow of humors from the head to the parts of the eyes and ears, and abundance of sleep, and toothache, and sore throat: and universally every sickness which happens from coldness, such as paralysis, and headache, epilepsy, and apoplexy, and those similar to these sicknesses.
The way of cauterization is that you order the sick person to be evacuated first with a medicinal solution purging the head for three nights or four, according to what the strength of the sick person requires, and his age and his habit.
Then order him to shave his head with a razor. Then make him sit between your hands on a stool, so that he has already placed his hands upon his chest.
Then place the root of your palm upon the root of his nose between his eyes. Wherever therefore your middle finger arrives, mark that spot with a cautery.
Then with an Olive-shaped Cautery: whose form is this.
Then place it upon the spot marked with the cautery, with descent, whereby your hand is gradually constricted, and you revolve it. Then quickly lift your hand and look at the place.
If you see that a quantity of the head equal to a small almond, or a quantity of rue, has already been exposed from the bone, then raise your hand. And if not, repeat your hand with the iron itself, or with another from it if it becomes cold: until the bones which I told you appear.
Then take some salt and dissolve it in water, and soak the cotton wool in it, and place it upon the spot; and leave it for three days.
Then place cotton wool soaked in butter upon it, and leave it upon the spot until the cautery burn is destroyed. Then treat it with Tetrapharmakon ointment until it heals.
They have said that the more a wound remains open, the more it emits pus; therefore it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them have said that the skin should be cauterized down to the bone; and the cautery should be held until something of the thickness of bone is hardened.
Then after that which has been burned is scraped away from the bone, then it is treated.
And others say that it is necessary with the cautery to reach the final point until it makes an impression on the bone so deep that the bone falls out; this is done like the shape of a kirate, or an instrument which women put in infused with a small alphabet: and they think that vapors from the head exhale from this place, and the wound is left open for a long time.
Then it should be cared for until it becomes solid.
But to me these two kinds of cautery do not seem at all suitable except in certain people according to the way of fear.
And to dismiss him is better with me than his health.
For the head is weakened by the breaking of its natural continuity as we have testified in other members and especially when the head of a weak person is naturally frail; and indeed the first kind of cautery is safer and better for me, and I use it: therefore operate with it in safety.
Chapter III.
When chronic pain occurs throughout the whole head and it is prolonged in a weak person, and they use hot baths, and cooking pots, and head-wraps, and oils, and poultices. And especially if it has already been cauterized with one cautery, which we have described, and none of those things helps them, then consider. For if the head of a weak person is strong by nature and not frail, and they find intense cold then cauterize with another cautery under that one slightly. Then cauterize them over the whole horn of their head with a cautery so that it does not go through the skin and uncover a quantity from the bone, as we have described. And cauterize them with one cautery on the back of their head in the place called "vas," which is the place where a windsock is placed, and elevate your hand in this place, and do not uncover the bone. For the weak person finds because of that intense pain, and different from the pain of all other head cauteries.
And I say this cautery in its place and it is necessary that it be a cautery with which the horns of the head are cauterized, and the back of their head with a more subtle cautery with which the middle of the head is cauterized.
Chapter IV.
When pain and swelling occur in a part of the head, or the pain extends as far as the eye, and the head is cleansed with purifying medicines, and the remaining treatment, which I have mentioned in the divisions of diseases, is administered, and it does not all resolve: then cauterization is appropriate in this case according to two methods: either cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine; or with iron.
Cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine is done as follows: you take one clove of garlic, and remove its outer layers, and cut off its ends from both sides. Then, during a time of bloodletting, split the area of pain until a spot under the skin is made where the tooth will be held.
Therefore insert it into the skin itself until it is concealed; then bind it tightly with a bandage of appropriate constriction and leave it for fifteen hours. Then loosen it and remove the garlic, and let the wound rest for two or three days; afterwards place upon it a poultice steeped in butter until the area produces pus; then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish to do this with certain burning medicines, which I have written about in the twelfth treatise, On Burning Medicines.
Cauterization of it with iron is done as follows. Heat a cautery iron, which has this form.
And it is called a "Clauilis" because the shape of its head is like that of a key, which has a certain curvature, and in its middle there is a small prominence.
Then place it on the spot and hold your hand steady; and revolve the iron gradually with speed; and let the quantity that is cauterized be half the thickness of the skin. And raise your hand so that the artery which is below is not injured and bleeding occurs.
Then soak a swab in salt water and place it on the spot, and leave it for three days; then place the swab with butter; afterwards treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize this half-skull with the second point of the cautery. And be careful not to cut an artery in this half-skull, not an old one properly.
Concerning the way of cauterizing an old half-skull.
When you treat a half-skull with what we have said from the preceding treatment and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the treatment does not succeed; and you see from the illness that it is not sufficient for it what we have said from the first cautery with medicine or with fire, then it is necessary that you ignite the knife-shaped cautery until it becomes white after you have marked the place of pain with a line, whose length is half a finger, or so; and place your hand on it once, and squeeze it until you strike the artery and reach the mouth.
However it is necessary for you to be careful from the continuation of the jawbone, which moves at chewing, lest its side is injured or the nerve that moves it, whence a spasm occurs.
And be on guard and cautious about the flow of blood from the artery which has been severed. For there is fear in its severance and especially for him who does not know what he is doing and is not studious or experienced; and he abandons the operation with it at first.
And I will come with a remembrance of the regimen of blood flow occurring from an artery according to its own way and in its place in this book.
If you see from the illness that this cautery is not sufficient for it, and you see a weak body enduring cautery in the middle of the head, as we have described, care for the wound until it heals.
And if you wish, apply cautery, as we said in the chapter on extracting an artery with cautery having two knives. For this cauterization is better and more effective.
On the method of cauterizing ear pain.
When pain occurs in the ear from cold and is treated with solutions and the remaining care as we said in the division, and the pain is not removed, then cautery by fire: and that is what is called a point, whose form is this.
Then points with it after its ignition around the whole ear, as it revolves: or around both if pain is present in both at the same time. And distance yourself with the cautery from the root of the ear a little, after you have marked the places with cautery paste: and let the quantity of the cautery be ten points in each ear, and like that.
Then care for the places until they heal.
On the method of cautery for a fistula.
Chapter Seven.
A fistula that is treated with cautery is not except from the kinds which arise from phlegm, according to what we said in the divisions of illnesses.
And avoid cautery for the kinds that arise from dryness and nerve spasm.
When you treat this kind of fistula with solutions and poultices and gargles, and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary that you cauterize the weak person with three cauteries: one at the root of the ear, and a second a little below its temple, and a third at the junction of the two lips.
And place your cautery opposite the weak part. For softening does not happen except in the part which appears healthy.
And the form of the cautery is that you cauterize it itself with the cautery in the direct extremity of the upper part of the ear under the horn of the head a little, and at another time: its length being according to the length of the thumb. And place your hand with the cautery until a quantity equal to half the thickness of the skin is burned.
And this is the form of the cautery and it is a species from the kind of knife, its shape having been presented previously: however, it is not thinner than that by a little, as you see: and it must be such that the knife in it has a little superfluous thickness.
Then care for the places with what preceded until they are healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in old apoplexy.
When apoplexy becomes old and you treat it with what we said, and your medication does not take effect, and the sick person does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries over all of his horn of the head, and a cautery in the middle of the head as we said, and a cautery on the back of the head according to what preceded: and let the manner of the cautery be according to what preceded.
Also, it is cauterized with a cautery over the mouth of the stomach and it is the last resort.
Then care for it with what preceded.
Chapter Nine.
It is necessary that first strong hyssop be given to the sick person in a drink, and pills which purify the brain.
Then shave the entire head of the sick person and place on the back of his head a plaster of synop as described in the treatise of plasters, and apply it several times.
For this is the method of cautery; and do it according to the same order that I said there.
If therefore it heals thereby, it is good. If not: then make for him three cauteries on the posterior part of his head, which are descending from the upper part of the head down to the lower part of the eye; and put a finger's breadth between each cautery.
Then care for the cautery as was done before.
But if you wish an addition, and the weak person can tolerate it: then make a cautery for him in the middle. But if you wish an addition: then cauterize it above the two horns.
Then care for it until it heals; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form that was given before.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in paralysis, and softening.
Chapter Ten.
It is necessary that you proceed in the cleansing of the head with hyssop, and with what we said.
Then shave the head of the sick person; afterwards cauterize it with a cautery in the middle of the head, and with a cautery above each horn of the head, and with a cautery above the back part of it, and with three above the vertebrae of the neck.
But if you are occupied in the illness with softening of the body by many of those things and the sick person is able to tolerate it and the illness is strong: then cauterize him with four cauteries above the vertebrae of the back, and last of all with a cautery until most of it is burned from his skin, and lift up your hand. Then care for it until it heals; and thus the olive-shaped cautery.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in epilepsy.
An epileptic is not cauterized unless he whose epilepsy is from phlegm.
Therefore it is necessary that you cleanse his brain first with vinegar, and with the remaining treatment which we mentioned in the division; when he is a large invalid, or tolerant in the reception of medicines.
But if he is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then a gargle and cleaning chews are administered for the brain, before that for many days with improvement of food.
Then shave his head, afterwards cauterize it with one cautery in the middle of the head, according to what preceded in the manner; and with another cautery on the back of his head, and with a cautery over all the horn of his head. But if the invalid is strong and tolerant, then cauterize him with the cauteries which I said in the one having paralysis, and softening of the body over the vertebrae of the neck, and the vertebrae of the back; and let the cautery be olive according to the manner which preceded.
But if the invalid is a child, then place a subtle cautery according to this manner.
On the manner of cauterizing in melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humors and thick phlegm, then cauterize him with the cauteries which we said in the one having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is a superfluity inclined towards melancholy, and the body of the invalid is moist, then give him a drink that will cleanse his brain, according to what preceded in the division; then shave the head of the invalid, and afterwards make a well-made tourniquet from linen, like a circle.
Then place it in the middle of the head; and the invalid sitting upright must be held from every side.
Then present the liniment. And it is more fitting to make it from old sheep's butter. And heat it over a fire with temperate heating, which the finger can tolerate when immersed in it. Then evacuate it in the middle of its head in a circle, and leave it alone until it becomes cold.
Do that to the weak one once a week, with the rest of its good regimen, until it is healed.
Or if you wish, cauterize it by making many small points on it, except that which you hold with the cautery: let there be a smell.
For this kind of cautery moistens the brain with quality.
Then put a swab soaked in butter, or chicken fat on top of it.
On the method of cauterizing descending to the eye in water.
Chapter Thirteen.
When the beginning of water is declared to you in the eye by the signs which I have said in the division, then begin and make the weak one drink, with that which cleanses its head.
And keep it from all dampness, and make it sit in a bath, fasting for several days.
Then order it to shave its head, and cauterize it in the middle of its head. Then cauterize it above the two temples with two cauteries, if there is a beginning of the descent of water in both eyes at the same time: or from one side, if there is a beginning of water in one eye: and cut off with the cautery all the veins and arteries that are under the skin: and let the cautery be long in width to the temples: and beware of the flow of blood.
For if you see anything from it, then cut it off immediately with whatever medicine is possible: and I will come with wisdom, which is in the extraction of arteries, and their cutting, and caution against the flow of blood.
And sometimes it is cauterized on the neck, under the two bones, with two cauteries lastly produced.
On the method of cauterizing old tears.
When tears of the eyes are old, persistent, and are upon the veins and arteries which are on the exterior of the head manifest, it is verified that this is from cold, phlegmatic superfluities; then cauterize with the same cautery as I have described in the beginning of descending water, a cautery in the middle of the head, and two cauteries above the two temples, and two cauteries on the neck beneath the two bones. And if it does not yield with addition, then cauterize it on every side cautiously above the eye, upon the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Concerning the way of cauterizing in a foul smell of the nose.
When you treat it with what we said in the division and medication does not work; then hurry, and give in a drink three nights of infirmocochias; then shave its head and cauterize it with a medium cautery, with an olive oil cautery. Then cauterize it with a clove cautery two cauteries, above the two eyebrows a little under the hairs.
And guard from the artery so that you do not strike it.
And this is the form of a clove cautery.
Concerning the way of cauterizing in relaxation of the eyelid.
When the eyelid of the eye is relaxed from illness or humidity; then cauterize the eyelid with one cautery, with this lunar cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize it above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries on every side; and they are elongated from the two temples; and let the length of the whole cautery be according to the lengths of the eyebrow; and do not stop your hand with the cautery, but rather according to the amount that is cauterized as a third eyelid.
On the method of cauterizing an eyelid when the ingrown hairs are stinging the eye.
A cautery is done in two ways: either with fire or with a burning medicine.
But in a cautery with fire, instruct the patient beforehand to let his hairs grow out, if he is one of those who removes them, until they are lengthened and even. But if they sting him at their origin, then bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow out.
When they have therefore grown out and are even, then place the patient's head in your lap; then mark on the eyelid of his eyes with a cautery, a mark which is according to the shape of a myrtle leaf: and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
Then place a swab soaked in albumin, or in psyllium mucilage on the eye.
Then apply the cautery with fire, which has this shape.
Then cauterize over the figure which you have marked gradually in many applications until the surface of the skin which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf, appears entirely and properly burned.
And the sign of the success of your work is this: that if you see the eyelid of the eye already contracting and the hairs rising at the puncture of the eye.
Therefore raise your hand then, and let him go for three days.
Then place a swab with butter on it until the eschar is removed. Then care for him with ointment until he is healed.
If any of the hairs return after an hour and the eyelid softens, then repeat the cautery on that place as you did at first.
If, however, the hairs are below: then cauterize it until it rises to its natural place and becomes equal so that the hairs do not sting the eye.
It is as if you should direct the sick person to let out the hairs until they are lengthened and equalized. Make from paper or cloth a shape of a myrtle leaf.
Then take from known soap and from lime not soaked an ounce one or so from each; then grind them together with good friction and do it quickly so that it does not congeal. Then from the pan put it onto the paper which you made like the shape of a myrtle leaf and place it on the eyelid of one or both eyes. And place under the eye a swab soaked in albuminous material; and let the head of the sick person be in your lap.
And place your index finger on top of the medicine and press it for a short time. And you will move it whenever the sick person feels the bite of the medicine; because he finds its bite like fire.
While therefore he perseveres in this that he finds a bite, then let go the medicine and move it with your finger. When therefore the bite rests, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And consider then if you see the eyelid rise as it rises with fire or with an incision, it is good; and if not, then repeat upon it from the medicine upon the place in which the medicine does not imprint and does not blacken until your operation is equal and the eye contracts.
Then put on it a swab with butter until the skin of the burn is eradicated. Then care for it with diaphinicon ointment or another until it heals.
And it is necessary for you, during the operation, to keep the closest watch, so that nothing from the medicine may fall into the eye.
And if the eyelid softens after a few days, and something from the hairs fights its way into the eye, then repeat the medicine upon that place which softens from the eyelid itself, just as you did at first. Then take care of it until it is healed.
And this is the form of a myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified in size and equality. Therefore let your consideration be according to that, and let the way of righteousness not be hidden from him who has a desire for this art.
On the manner of cauterizing in a tear fistula.
When you treat a fistula, as we said in the divisions of diseases, and your treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary for you to cauterize it according to this method.
Instruct the sick person to place his head in your lap and let a minister hold his head between his hands so that it may not be moved nor shaken with his head.
Then place cotton infused in alum or in the mucilage of flaxseed over his eye; then with fire a cautery, whose shape is this.
Let it be concave, like the shape of a hawk's feather quill from one end, with which the cautery is made; and if you wish it to penetrate up to the other extremity, and if you wish it to go upward then try this way.
However this concave shape is better for your purpose.
Then express the fistula, if it is open, and draw out the pus from it and dry it; and if it is not open, then open it and draw out its pus.
Then place upon it the cautery which has been ignited very hot.
Hold your hand for a short time with it near the cautery by the eye, towards the side of the nose, so that your hand does not stray, or the weak person become restless; and place the cautery in the fat of the eye, and burn it.
Therefore if you reach at the beginning of your cautery up to the mouth, it will be well. And if not: then repeat the cautery once, or twice, if it is necessary with that: and leave it for three days.
Then apply a compress with butter, and care for it with a drying ointment until it is healed.
And if forty days pass and it does not heal, then put upon it a sharp corrosive medicine until the mouth is exposed; and scrape it according to what comes in its chapter.
And there is another way of cautery for a fistula, which some of the first have said. Attend to the place of the fistula and find it; then place in the fissure a thin cane, that is, a thin cannula, whose form is this. And pour into it a quantity of a drachma of melted lead, and hold your hand with the cane in a good grip, and let the weak person not move at all, so that the melted lead does not run or reach their eye.
And it is necessary that you place upon the eye of the weak person a compress soaked in albumin, or in water.
For the lead burns the place of the fistula and heals it with a marvelous healing.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said from cautery and treatment, it is good. And if not, then it is necessary to administer a nasal foramen and reduce the fistula back into the course of the nose, according to what will come in its proper place.
Concerning the way of cauterizing fissures of the lips.
Very often fissures occur in the lip, which are called pili, and especially in the lips of children.
Therefore, when you treat these fissures as we said in the division of diseases, and the treatment does not work, then a small knife-shaped branding iron should be applied with fire according to this form.
And its concavity should be according to the subtlety of the knife.
Then quickly place the heated branding iron itself into the fissures themselves until the branding iron reaches the depth of the fissures.
Then treat it with cerate until it heals.
On the method of branding a fistula of the mouth.
When an abscess occurs at the root of the gum, or in the palate, or in the roots of the teeth; then it makes pus and bursts: afterwards it becomes chronic, and pus runs out, and a fistula forms: then you treat it, and treatment does not work, then it is necessary that you apply a branding iron with fire according to the size that fits in the fistula. Then insert the heated branding iron into the fistula opening and hold your hand there until the heated iron reaches its depth and end; do this once or twice.
Then treat it afterward with what we said about treatment until it is cured.
If therefore the discharge is cut off and it heals, it will be good. And if not, then it is necessary to expose the place and remove the corrupt bone according to what will come in its chapter.
On the method of branding gums and softened teeth.
When the gum softens due to humidity and the teeth loosen, and you treat it with medicine but it does not work, then place the head of the weak person in your lap.
Then a cautery iron, the shape of which will come after this, so that you place the cannula over the tooth; and insert into it the ignited cautery quickly: and hold your hand for a short time until the weak one feels the heat of the fire has already reached the root of the tooth.
And raise your hand; then repeat the cautery several times, according to your wish.
Then let the weak one fill his mouth with salt water and hold it for one hour; and spit it out.
For the tooth motion is strengthened, and the softened gum is tightened, and the corrupted moisture is dried.
Concerning the way of cauterizing in toothache.
When toothache is due to coldness, or there is cold itself in it, or a worm, and treatment with medicines does not help; then the cautery is used on it according to two methods: either a cautery with butter, or a cautery with fire.
But the cautery of it with butter is, that you take vaccinated butter, and boil it in an iron spoon, or in a crucible. Then take a wick, and wrap it over the end of the tent. Then dip it into the boiling butter; and place it over the painful tooth quickly, and hold it until it cools; then repeat it several times, until the power of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool, or a wick in cold butter, and place it over the aching tooth; and place ignited iron over it until the fire reaches the depth of the cavity of the tooth.
But cauterization with fire is, that you apply a tube of air, or a tube of iron. And let something of thickness be in its body so that the sharpness of the fire does not reach the mouth of the weak one.
Then a cautery by fire, whose shape will come; and place it upon that tooth; and hold your hand until the cautery becomes cold.
Do this several times, for the pain will be removed, either on that same day or another day.
And it is necessary in the impression of that cautery, that the sick person fill his mouth with good butter, and hold it for an hour; then he spits it out.
And this is the form of the cautery.
Cauterize with whichever extremity you wish according to what is possible; and this is the form of the cannula.
On the manner of cauterizing in fistulas.
When fistulas are from phlegm, and from cold humidities, and are not obedient to digestion by medicines, and you wish to digest them quickly; then a concave cautery by fire, whose form is this.
Having two open extremities, so that the smoke may exit at the cautery from one extremity; and place it ignited upon the abscess once, and a second time, if necessary with it, until it reaches the depth of the abscess.
Which if the abscess is small; then place the cautery according to the size of the abscess.
Then leave it for three days, and place upon it cotton infused in butter, until what the fire burned is removed.
Then care for it with tooth salve until the shape of the cannula is healed.
On the way of cauterizing in hoarseness of voice and constriction of breathing.
When humidities overcome the channel of the lung and especially when it is with coldness of complexion then it is necessary that the weak one be evacuated first with solutions.
Then cauterize it at night at the root of the throat in a depressed place and be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery nor the skin unless a little of it.
Then cauterize it with another cautery at the juncture of the neck at the last vertebra of its end and let the cautery be gentle according to the way which preceded.
Then care for it with that which preceded until it is healed.
On the way of cauterizing in illnesses of the Lung and Cough.
When Cough and illness of the Lung are from cold humidities and the weak one does not have a fever nor phthisis and the illness is old then cauterize it with two cauteries above the fork in depressed, gentle, empty places. And with another cautery in the middle of the chest between the two nipples and let the cautery be of a cannula according to the form which preceded.
And if your cauterization wishes to be by pricking with a cautery which is called a Point, whose form preceded in the cauterization of ear pain and let there be points from thirty points up to almost others.
Then care for it with that which preceded until it is healed.
And let the cautery have three branches according to this form. Therefore cauterization is relieved with it when you do it with it with one three cauteries.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
When the head of the humerus is dislocated due to humidity and does not return to its place within an hour, until it becomes accustomed to this displacement, then it dislocates from a slight movement, which happens as we have already testified, then it is necessary that you first reduce the dislocation. Then the weak person is placed on their back or on their sound side. Then lift the skin which is inside the shoulder joint upwards with the fingers of your left hand; and if the joint dislocates inwards.
Then, having a cautery with two points. Whose shape is this.
Then cauterize the skin until it penetrates to the other side, and a cauterization mark will appear with four points.
And sometimes the cauterization is done with a cautery having three points, so that the cauterization mark then has six points; and the points are chosen according to subtlety.
And this is the shape of a cautery having three points.
And sometimes one more is added in this way, so there are eight cauteries.
Then place pounded leeks with salt on top of the cautery. Then make the weak person adhere to tranquility so that the limb is not moved for a while until it is strengthened.
And if the dislocation is upwards, and this rarely happens, then cauterize it above the shoulder with a clauile cautery or by pricking with many cauteries.
For the joint is strengthened and the humidity is removed; and the weak person is healed.
On the method of cauterizing the stomach.
When cold and much humidity happen in the stomach, until they draw it from its complexion, and catarrhs are multiplied to it, and assiduous modes of treatment are applied, and they do not help: then it is necessary that the sick person be put on his back, and extend his legs and arms. Then cauterize with three cauteries, a cautery under the size of a spoon's handle on the breastbone, a cautery on the clavicle, and two cauteries below from both sides of the one cautery, until a triangular figure of the cauteries comes. And space them apart so they do not join together when they make pus.
And let the depth of the cautery be the quantity of two-thirds the thickness of the skin.
And let the figure of the cauteries be according to this form without addition: and if you wish, cauterize with a large cautery in the middle of the stomach according to this form: and let the cautery by which this cautery is made be one according to the figure of a circle according to this form.
And the stomach is cauterized by pricking it for him who is terrified from this cautery: and it is that you sign upon the stomach a point according to the quantity which you wish with encausto; then cauterize it with a point cautery; then treat it with the treatment that preceded until it is healed.
Concerning the manner of cauterizing a cold epar.
When pain happens in the epar from coldness and humidity, or from thick windiness, until superfluity departs from its natural complexion by egress, and the sick person is treated with what we have said, and does not make progress in it: then it is necessary that the sick person be put on his neck, and three cauteries be signed with encausto according to this figure, and the same quantity above the epar below the hypochondria, where a man's elbow reaches: and let the space which is between every cautery be according to the thickness of a finger: and let the cautery be straight according to the length of the body: and do not press your hand much with the cautery.
And let the quantity thereof which is cauterized be from the thickness of the skin, the quantity of half of it not added; and let the weak man stand upon his own feet.
But if he is not standing, then let him lie down, who has already extended his legs, and raised his arms.
This is the form of a cautery.
And it is indeed possible for you to cauterize with this cautery using a knife cautery, when you have subtlety and prudence in the art.
And guard yourself so that you do not proceed too far in the cauterization, and the thickness of all the skin is cauterized, wherefore the whole body is cauterized, and it reaches the intestines: because the skin there is subtle, know therefore that.
Concerning the manner of cauterizing liver abscesses.
When it happens that you wish to know if that abscess is in the flesh of the liver, or in its tunic: then if it is in the flesh of the liver, it assails the sick person with pain and gravity without sharpness; and if it is in the tunic of the liver, with pain there is vehement sharpness: and you see now that it has fatigued the physicians who treated him.
Therefore, it is necessary for the sick person to recline on his neck. Then figure the place of the abscess with a heated cautery; then ignite the cautery which resembles a ray. And this is its form. And cauterize with that one cautery until all the skin is burned and it reaches the siphac, until all the pus comes out; then treat it with the treatment of exit wounds until it is healed.
And it is not necessary for one to use this kind of cautery except for someone whose study in the art of medicine is prolonged; and through whose hands these kinds of illnesses run with frequent experiment; then let him dare to perform this type of operation.
As for dismissing her, I prefer to do so myself.
On the Method of Cauterizing in Pleuresy.
The ancients said that a cautery with the roots of aristolochia should be applied to cold pleurisy in this way. And it is that you should take a long dry root of aristolochia and choose the longest one, which is found among them, and let it be as thick as a finger. Then submerge it in oil and ignite it in fire. Then cauterize with one cautery in that which is between the continuation of the collarbone and the neck, and with two small cauteries under the shoulder blades a little lower towards the part which is under the beard.
And also with two cauteries in that which is between the fifth and sixth ribs a little lower towards the back; and with another cautery in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach. And with three cauteries from the back: one in that which is between the two shoulder blades; and two cauteries from the two sides of the chest, lower than the cautery which is between the two shoulder blades.
And it is not necessary that you press your hand with the cautery, rather let it be manifest on the skin while smelling.
And now some ancients have said that there are men who use a cautery of iron similar to a rod; and they ignite it and insert it into that which is between the ribs, until it reaches the abscess itself, and pus comes out; as we have said in the abscess of the liver.
But in this perforation with a cautery there is fear, that either the weak one dies immediately, or a fistula occurs in the place, which has no healing.
On the Method of Cauterizing the Spleen Chapter Thirty-Two.
When you treat a disease of the spleen, as we said in the treatment of divisions of diseases, and your treatment does not succeed, then there is cautery on it according to three ways, which are all right.
One of them is to make three or four cauteries by cutting along the length of the spleen according to the shape of liver cauteries, whose figure precedes it.
And let there be a quantity equal to the thickness of a finger, or more or less, between every cautery.
And let the mode of the cautery be equal as we said in liver cautery.
And do not press your hand with the cautery, and let the patient be lying on his back.
And another way of cauterization is: to have a cautery with two points, as we said in the chapter on dislocation of the shoulder; and lift the skin which is directly over the spleen where the left elbow of the patient reaches; and let your elevation be such that you lift the skin according to the width of the body so that the cauteries may fall according to the length of the body.
Then insert the two burning points deeply until you penetrate the skin on the other side; then withdraw the cautery; thus making four cauteries.
And if you wish, cauterize with another cautery having three points.
Then care for the cautery after you have let it discharge pus for many days; for it is more effective than any other treatment which precedes it.
On the method of cauterizing in dropsy.
Cautery does not apply to dropsy unless it is specifically ascites.
When you treat dropsy with the methods of treatment which we mentioned in the division, and the treatment does not succeed, then it is necessary that you cauterize it with four cauteries around the navel, and one cautery above the stomach, and another above the liver, and another on the spleen, and two cauteries behind the back between the vertebrae, and one directly on the chest, and another directly on the stomach; and let the depth of the cautery be near the thickness of the skin.
Then let the open cautery be dismissed, so that it emits pus over a long time.
And do not discharge the weak one from treatment after the cautery with it, for two intentions must gather for him, and healing will hasten to him with God's help.
The shape of the cautery with which the belly is cauterized should be clauilis, according to its aforementioned shape; and that with which the back is cauterized should be olive-shaped, according to its aforementioned shape.
On the method of cauterizing the feet and legs.
When abscesses form in a dropsy and are filled with clear water, you should cauterize on the back of the foot in the nocrati region, which is between the auricular and annular regions; raise your hand with the cautery, and do not twist it; then raise your hand and do not move it at all.
For the clear water will be reddish, and the cautery should be of this shape.
Then make two cauteries on each leg, and let the cautery be made with the sharp end of the cautery; and let the cautery be made according to the length of the leg, one below the knee and the other below it, about the middle of the leg; and two cauteries on the whole thigh; and let the open cautery be dismissed without treatment for a long time so that some water may exude from it; then treat it with the rest that we have mentioned.
On the method of cauterizing in abdominal rupture.
Chapter XXXV.
When there is an abdominal rupture from cold and humidity until the retentive and digestive powers which are in the stomach and intestines weaken, and it is treated with treatments but does not heal; and you see the weak patient tolerating a cautery of abundant power, then cauterize him with a large cautery on his stomach according to what preceded regarding cauterization of the stomach with a circular cautery, and four slender cauteries in a circle around the navel with a slender clauilis cautery; and a cautery above the groin with a large one, or two cauteries.
And if you see abundant moistures, and the patient weak enduring that, then cauterize it with a cautery over the comb, and with a cautery over all the groin; and perhaps you will add two small cauteries over the stomach near the large one.
For it is a progressing cure whose benefit does not err.
On the manner of cauterizing hemorrhoids.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
When there are many old hemorrhoids in the anus, or one, and they are from thick, cold humors, or corrupted humors, and they are cured with what we said in the division, and the cure does not help; then cauterize with three cauteries over the lower vertebra of the back under the scapula a little triangularly, and a cautery under the navel like two fingers.
And if you think that his stomach is chilled, and his food is not digested, and you see his face inflamed, then cauterize him over the stomach with a large cautery, according to what preceded; and with a cautery over his liver, and another over his spleen, with a clauili cautery: and leave the cautery open for some time; then care for him until he is healed.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
When you excise atheromas, then heat a cautery that resembles a ray. Then insert the ignited one into the excised atheroma; and keep your hand until the cautery reaches the orifice of the vein from which blood flows; do that once or twice.
Therefore, if there are many atheromas, then cauterize each one with a cautery according to what we have narrated. Then care for them with what helps them from ointments until they are healed.
And if you cauterize it with a large cautery iron above the ligature, it is best for healing.
On the method of cauterizing a fistula in the anus.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
When the sick person does not obey the incision and work which we have described in its place, and is terrified by it, perhaps he is healed with a cautery.
Therefore when it happens to someone that a fistula becomes old, pus runs from it, and corrupt humors. It is therefore fitting to do what is necessary, so that you examine it with a delicate probe. Then ascertain the depth of it with a probe.
Then ignite a cautery iron which is similar to a spoke. Then insert the ignited iron into the fistula itself and mark the amount that it entered.
And repeat the cautery upon it until all those corrupt substances are burned, once, or twice, or thrice, according to the necessity of your judgment.
And be careful of burning a nerve if it is there, or a large vein; or if the fistula goes towards the bladder or towards the intestines, then beware of all those places.
And do not do this unless when the fistula is in a fleshy place and you consider that it is not penetrating.
Then care for the place until it heals.
But if the place is sealed and materials are cut away from it, and it remains so for a long time, then know that it has now been healed completely.
And if materials are not cut away from it, then know that it is penetrating, or deep within it there is a corrupt opening, or something like that, of which the remembrance will come in its place.
On the method of cauterizing the kidneys.
When pain occurs in the kidneys from cold, or from thick windiness, and it diminishes because of that weakness of his, then it is necessary to cauterize it over the almethenem over the kidneys themselves, with a cautery on all the kidney with a clauili cautery, of which remembrance preceded.
And perhaps we cauterize it a second time in the very same almeidat. Therefore three lines will come. Therefore they will be better for relief.
Concerning the manner of cauterizing the bladder.
When weakness and softening occur in the bladder from cold and humidities, until the sick person does not retain urine, then cauterize it on the lower part of the pubis over the bladder, where the hairs of the pubic region begin, and with a cautery from the right side of the pubis, and another from the left side of it: and let the length of that cauterization be according to the size of a thumb-joint: and cauterize it with a lower back cautery, or two cauteries if necessary for that: and let it be according to what preceded, a clauili cautery.
Concerning the manner of cauterizing the uterus.
When cold and humidities occur in the uterus, and it is forbidden by that from impregnation, and its menses are corrupted, and their provocation is delayed, and pain occurs to her at their arrival, then it is necessary that it be cauterized with three cauteries around the navel, as we said in the cauterization of the bladder, and with a lower back cautery, or two cauteries: and let the cautery be clauile.
Concerning the manner of cauterizing the shoulder in dislocation.
When mucosal humidities are poured into the socket of the shoulder, and they become the cause of its exit from its place: and the sign of it is that one leg is extended over the other, when they are compared to each other: and a vacant place is found in it.
Therefore, it is necessary that you cauterize the weak cautery upon the pitch of Anchae itself with a circle similar to the cautery; after you have marked with encaustic a circle around the pitch, as it revolves: and so that the pitch itself may fall in the middle of the circle.
But if you do not have this cautery present, then cauterize it with three cauteries, with a large olive cautery; and let the depth of the cautery be according to the amount of the thickness of the skin throughout.
Then care for it until it is healed.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in sciatica.
When pain occurs in the pitch of Anchae, and its cause is cold and humidity; and the weak one is cured with that which we said in the division, and the cure does not avail, and it becomes old: then it is necessary to dissolve the weak one from thick humors with fetid pills, or with artificial pills, and the like. Then cauterize him.
And the method of cautery in this is according to two methods: either cautery with burning medicines, or cautery with fire.
Cautery with fire is according to many methods.
One of which is that you cauterize upon the pitch of Anchae itself with three triangular cauteries, according to this form. And make their depth good, or bad. And let the space which is between every cautery be of the thickness of a finger: and let the cautery be an olive one.
Sometimes, however, cautery is done in the middle above the head of Anchae itself. Therefore there are four cauteries.
And if you wish to cauterize with a circle, whose shape precedes in the cauterization of a wide dislocation, make it one: so that it is a circle containing the whole dislocation.
And burn the thickness of all the skin.
This is the method of cautery in sciatica, when the pain is adherent to the wide area itself, and does not extend up to the thigh and leg.
But if it extends up to the thigh and leg, then cauterize it with two cauteries above the thigh, above the place to which the sick person points there, in which there is pain, and your cautery above the calf four fingers towards the wooded side a little: and let the cautery be a knife-like one.
And let the depth of the cautery be according to the depth of the thickness of the skin.
But if the sick person points out that the pain extends almost to the toes, then cauterize it there where he points with a point-like cautery, three, or four, or more, if it needs it.
And if he points out the pain to be below the knee towards the leg, then cauterize it there with one knife-like cautery.
And be careful in all your cauterizations, that you do not finish with the cautery so as to burn a nerve, or a large artery, and thereby bring upon the sick person an evil harm, or contraction.
And I myself have indeed already seen one and another, of those who cauterized above the calf, and finished in the cautery, and the leg swelled up, until a tumor reached the foot, and it was perforated all through, and the whole foot was corrupted: then there occurred a rupture of the belly, and death after that.
But if the pain is in two parts at the same time, cauterize both according to this method.
And certain wise men have said in the cauterization of a wide area, whose mode of cautery this is. Make a knife of iron, its diameter being half a palm; and let there be at its opening according to the thickness of the knuckle, or a little less: and within that knife let there be a third knife.
And let the space that is between all the knives be by the amount of a knuckle; and let the knives be open on two sides; and let their height be about a knuckle, or two knuckles; and make for them a handle of iron conveniently fitted to the knives.
Then heat it in fire until it glows and projects sparks; then place it on a pillow with the patient lying weakly on their healthy side. Then cauterize him with three round cauteries at once.
Then dismiss him for three days. Afterwards plaster with butter; and dismiss the open wound for many days. Then care for it with ointment until it is healed.
Says Albucasis, we have rarely used this kind of cautery, my son, on account of the terror of its appearance; and therefore because we have found few who will endure it.
Nevertheless it is among the good cauteries for him who endures it and finds its place with it.
But the cautery with healing medicines is for you to make two knives having a pierced handle into which descends the hinge pin of its gate from brass or iron, the length of a span; whose height shall be two knuckles, or as they are themselves.
And let the space that is between each one be the thickness of a finger; and let them be open below and above; and let what contains them to each other be according to this form.
Then place it on a broad pillow, the patient lying on their healthy side. And press your hand hard upon the broad pillow. Then pour between two circles hot water, which has been heated with fire for a short time, and hold it for the space of an hour: and let the sick person endure its bite and its burning, for he will find the bite like fire until the bite is quieted.
Then raise your hand with the instrument and wipe away the hot water from the whole wound with fresh water; then leave it for three days.
And put butter upon it until the blackness of the burning is removed; then leave it for several days and let pus run from it. For this is the last resort in healing.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
But if the pain extends as far as the hip or leg: make him a bath containing water according to what you did for the wound equally.
And this is the method of hot water: and I have already written it in the treatise on the rectification of medicines. Take alkali and quicklime each a part: grind each and put them in a new pot, whose lower part is pierced with one small hole according to the quantity which enters the radius itself; and place under the bottom of the pot another glazed pot; and pour over the alkali and lime fresh water, which submerges both by a finger; after which you press them with your hand with good pressure; and let the pot stand until the sharp water descends into the lower part of the glazed pot.
Then add all that water and pour it over other lime; and strain until it is renewed.
For then it will be very strong and sharp, which is administered in many medical operations, and in the cauterization of other members. Because it effects the operation of fire itself.
And among medicines are those with which wounds are cauterized, also like turpentine and cashew nut honey, and lime with soap, so that both are thoroughly mixed.
And Galen indeed related the cure of a certain man from ancient times, by which he was cured of pain in the ankle and sciatic vein; and he praised it greatly. And he said that he needed no other treatment and that he was healed from one draught until he related that perhaps he had entered a bath carried in and came out of it already well.
And it is to be taken from the leaves of a green fig; and if green is not found, let it be taken dry, fresh; and it is mixed much with a little fat and placed on the ankle where there is pain, or the leg, or on the hip; and it is tied on and left for three hours, or by the amount which the sick person feels the heat subside. Then introduce him into a bath.
When therefore his body becomes hot, introduce him onto a couch. For the pain goes away, and he is healed.
And know that it is not necessary for anyone to use a cautery with these medicines unless after evacuation of the body.
And Dioscorides who also said that when goat dung is cauterized with it, it applies it to the sciatic vein; and a cautery is made in this way. Take wool and soak it in old oil and place it on the deep spot which is between the thumb of the foot and the sole, and which is nearer to the sole.
Then take dry goat dung and inflame it in fire until it becomes charcoal.
Then place it on the wool and leave it until it goes out. Then repeat that. Do not therefore cease to do this until the feeling reaches through the middle up to the ankle and the pain subsides.
And this method of cautery is called the Vaccinum cautery.
On the Cauterization of Back Pain.
Sometimes pain occurs in the back from many causes, either from a fall, or a blow, or excessive evacuation, and similar things, from infusion of cold and humid matter; and cautery indeed does not apply except in this one kind, which is caused by infusion of cold matter.
Therefore it is necessary after evacuation of the sick person with fetid pills, and similar things, so that they are made on the back, where there is pain, three lines according to the width of the knife itself; after signs are made with a hot iron in every line five cauteries, or more according to the amount which comes from the endurance of the sick person's strength; and let the cauterization be done with a pointed cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize with three cauteries, or four, with a middle-sized pointed cautery, according to this form.
On the Method of Cauterizing in the Beginning of a Hunchback.
Very often this illness happens to small infants.
And the sign of its beginning in children is: that a constriction occurs upon him in breathing near elevation, and motion; and in youths at the last vertebra of the back and its nodes, which has already begun to protrude above the other vertebrae.
When therefore you see that, and wish to do it, then cauterize it with a cautery which is a circle according to this form.
And let the cautery cover the whole part of the vertebra equally; and if you wish cauterize around the vertebra with a pointed cautery two lines or three, and let the points be close to each other.
Then care for the place until it heals with what we have said.
And beware, lest you administer cautery in a hunchback which is caused by spasm of a nerve.
On the method of cauterizing gout and joint pains.
When joint pains are from cold humors, which are poured out into any part of the body, then when they happen in the feet, it is customary for physicians to name that Podagra properly.
Therefore, when Podagra of a cold cause is treated with treatment methods and the pains are not removed, then cautery removes them.
And it is to cauterize him after evacuation around the joint of the feet with many cauteries; and let the cautery be olive wood.
And if it is necessary to prick on the surface of the foot, then do so with a pointed cautery. But if the pains rise up to the knees or other joints and this often happens then cauterize him over the whole knee with three or four cauteries from every side with the same olive wood cautery.
But if you need more with this cautery then cauterize him. And do not submerge your hand with the cautery but let it be approximately the thickness of the skin only.
But if pains rise to the knees and back then administer what we said from the cautery in its own chapter.
But if the pains are only in the hands then prick around the knuckles like two lines revolve.
But if any pain remains in the fingers then prick them on every knuckle and on the palm of the hand.
But if pains rise after days to the elbows or shoulders then cauterize both from every side.
And do not remove the weak person from a good regimen, from taking medicines. For if a good regimen is made and phlegm is evacuated he will be healed with the help of God with this cautery.
On the manner of cauterizing ruptures.
When a rupture occurs in the groin and the intestines or omentum descends to the testicle and it is beginning nearby, then it is necessary that you advise the sick man to abstain from food on his day. And let him use laxatives so that the feces may descend from his abdomen.
Then let him sit between your hands on his back and instruct him to hold his breath until it emerges or the omentum or intestines appear.
Then reduce it with your fingers and mark with a cautery under the rupture above the pubic bone, with a mark whose end is like half of a circle toward the upper part of the body.
Then apply the hot cautery, which has this shape, until it becomes white and projects sparks. Then reduce the intestines or omentum to its cavity. Then let the assistant place his hand on the spot so that the intestines do not come out and you have already opened between the sick man's thighs and placed a cushion underneath him, and one servant above his thighs and another above his chest who holds his hands.
Then place the cautery on the mark itself with your hand holding the cautery standing straight. And hold it until you have reached a bone with it or repeat another application if you did not reach a bone with the first one.
And carefully observe from the exit of the intestines during your cauterization so that you do not burn it and there comes harm upon the sick man or death or great injury.
And know that when you do not reach a bone with the cautery your operation is of no avail.
And it is necessary that the cautery be delicate like that for children according to their size.
Then care with the remaining ointments until it heals.
And let the weak one lie on his back for forty days until the wound is sealed.
And it is necessary that you provide him with nourishment for the length of his healing time, which soothes his belly so that the intestines do not protrude during straining and defecation.
Then when he wants to rise after forty days, then administer a bandage well made, and let him hold it for another forty days.
And he should abstain from labor, and the fullness of food and drink, and loud voices.
For when he uses this regimen, thus he heals with complete healing.
And I will speak of the treatment of ruptures with incision in its own chapter.
But when ruptures occur in the rest of the belly and are beginning and you wish that they not be added to, then cauterize his rupture with a round cautery according to its size, and let that which is burned be one-third of it from then on. Then care for him as we have said, and it will not be added to.
And now some first ones have said that the rupture should be cauterized with a triangular cautery according to this form.
After it is signed with an encaustic and a line is put which is across the width on the upper side of the rupture, and another line below. And place your hand in the middle with the cautery held tight.
But the first cautery is easier and better.
On the way of cauterizing for pains because of a fall or blow.
Chapter 48
When pain occurs in certain limbs because of a fall or blow and that pain persists for a long time, and healing with medicine does not come upon it: then it is necessary that it be cauterized with a point cautery.
And let the cautery be for every member according to its size, and its suitability, and its weakness, and its power, and the placement of the twist, and the pain from it.
Then if it heals from the first cautery, it is well. If not, then repeat the cautery upon it.
For it is customary for these pains to change from one member to that which is next to it. Therefore it is necessary for you to pursue them with the cautery until the weak one is healed.
Concerning the manner of cauterizing leprosy.
Those with leprosy are sometimes greatly benefited by cautery. And especially the type of leprosy which occurs due to the putrefaction of phlegm and melancholy: therefore when you wish to cauterize it consider.
Therefore if the leprosy is beginning, and you have treated it with that which we said in the division, and it does not descend nor remain stationary, and you fear for the weak one that corruption may spread over their whole complexion: then cauterize them on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle of the head, and a second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hair, and two above the two temples, and one behind above the nape of the neck.
And with the last cautery press your hand upon it a little while until it makes a small impression on the bone, and the crusts are loosened from it: so that respiration of coarse vapors may be relieved from it, and also cauterize the spleen with a cautery according to what preceded.
But if the leprosy is already spread over the weak one and appears manifest then it is necessary for you to cauterize with these cauteries which we said upon the head, and a cautery upon the end of the nose, and two cauteries upon the two maxillae, and two cauteries upon the vertebrae of the neck, and six upon the vertebrae of the back, and one large one upon the alhaossos at the base of the tail and one above it, upon the very almeidati: and two upon the anchas: that is to say, upon every ancha with one, and two upon the knees: one upon every knee, and two upon the shoulders, and two upon the elbows, and two upon the soft parts of his chest: and cauterize him upon every joint, from the joints of the fingers of his hands and feet: and cauterize every heel of his feet and the focilla of his hands.
And beware of the nerves that are above the heel bone, so that you do not burn them.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery over the bone of the pectoral muscle, and with another over the bone of the stomach, and with another over the liver.
And know that the more you apply the cautery, the more beneficial it is and the more binding.
And know that a weak person does not find pain in the cautery, as healthy people do, because his body is already stunned.
And it is necessary that the cauteries be of magnitude and size according to the limbs and joints, as previously described from the types of instruments.
Then care for the cautery with flour of herbs mixed with honey, and other care, until it is healed.
On the manner of cauterizing in a stupor.
When a limb is in a stupor and is treated with medicines, oils, and plasters, and does not heal, then cauterize the numb limb with cauteries according to the magnitude or smallness of the limb.
And let your cautery penetrate into the thickness of the skin a little. Then care for it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes it is cauterized for a certain stupor which occurs in the hand and foot, in the vertebrae of the back near the exit of the nerves that move the limb, and the stupor is removed.
But let no one presume to do this unless he is wise in the anatomy of the limbs and the exit of the nerves that move the body.
Chapter LI.
When an albare becomes old and nothing from medical arts is effective in it: then cauterize over it with a deep cautery for a short time, according to the thickness of the skin, until the whiteness is removed and its color is changed.
Then care for it with herb flour, and lentils with rose oil, and arnoglossa leaves, and dove blood, and swallow blood: let there be one part of each; mix all these things together, and spread them on a cloth, and apply them to the place, until it is healed.
On the Method of Cauterizing Cancer.
When a cancer is beginning and you wish to stop it, then cauterize it with a circular cautery all around it, as it revolves.
But the wise have said that it should be cauterized with a final cautery in the middle.
However, it does not seem right to me; for I fear that it will ulcerate; and I have already seen this happen many times.
And it is better that it be cauterized around it with a circle, as we said, or with many cauteries.
On the Method of Cauterizing Adenitis.
When someone has adenitis and it is delayed in ripening, either because of excess causing it, or because of the infirmity of age, when they are old and have little blood, or because of time, and you wish to hasten the ripening of the adenitis, then cauterize around it with many small cauteries, puncturing. Then leave it alone. For it will hasten with ripening.
But if you wish to perforate it with a cautery, then a hot iron cautery, which has this shape.
And place it in the middle of the adenitis until it penetrates the skin; and let the cautery be weak after that which follows so as to alleviate the course of pus.
Then care for it until it is healed.
On the Method of Cauterizing an Ulcerating Condition.
Chapter LIV.
A corrosion is nothing but a corruption walking in a member and corroding it; just as fire eats dry wood.
If therefore you see a corrosion in a place which can bear cautery with fire, then apply much cautery with fire, small and large clauiae, according to what suits the place where the corrosion is.
Then cauterize it from every side until you eradicate the entire corruption and nothing of it remains at all.
Then let it alone for three days, and put powdered sulfur mixed with oil on the cauterized places until all the eschars and all corruption are eradicated.
Then treat it with ointments that generate flesh.
If therefore you see that after three days healthy flesh is born in which there is no corruption, it is good.
And if not: then repeat the cautery on that which remains from the corrupt places.
And a corrosion is sometimes treated with an acute medicine. For it arises in the place of a cautery.
However, cautery with fire is of quicker healing; and I have already said its cauterization with an acute medicine in a division: therefore take that there when it is necessary.
On the Method of Cauterizing Warts and Corns.
Chapter LV.
Very often this ailment occurs in the lower parts of the feet; and it is a thick, filtered thing which injures the foot.
And indeed cautery in them is according to two methods: cautery with fire and cautery with hot water.
But as for that with fire, it should be a concave cautery similar to a vulture's claw, made of iron according to the size which contains the wart from every side; and let its margins be subtle.
Then lay it ignited upon the ulcer. Then turn your hand with the cautery around the ulcer until the cautery reaches the depth of the ulcer; and leave it for three days until it begins to discharge pus. Then plaster it with mullein leaves, crushed with salt; and leave the plaster upon it at night. For it will be eradicated from its roots.
Then treat the place with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
But if they are not inverted and often occur on the surface of the body, especially on the hands and feet; then apply to them a cautery with hot water: and it is such that you should take a cannula from bronze, or iron, or from a vulture's quill; and lay it upon the ulcer, or althelul.
