Then pour into the cannula a small amount of hot water; hold your hand and turn it with the pressure of your hand for a short time so that the edge of the cannula is impressed upon the root of the ulcer, and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the ulcer: and let the patient wait a little above the bite of the hot water for an hour.
Then leave it alone, for the ulcer will be eradicated with its roots.
Do this to them one by one until you come upon all of them that are in the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the Method of Cauterizing in Tremor
When it happens to someone that Tremor arises from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or another cause; then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries, or five upon the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra a cautery, and one upon his chest, and one upon his stomach.
For tremor indeed ceases with an olive-wood cautery, and healing hastens for illnesses of cold nature.
On the Method of Cauterizing by Albatir.
Chapter Fifty-Seven.
Sometimes foul things, that is, pustules, are expelled from the body by Albatir, which are caused by corrupted thick humors.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary to cauterize them over the whole head of the pustule with a delicate cautery made of myrtle wood, whose end is ignited with fire. Or with a long aristolochia root. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the very beginning of their expulsion in this way; and they do not grow larger; and the excess is broken up, making them, and they are healed from them.
However, it must be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
On the Method of Cauterizing in a Flow of Blood from an Artery in a Wound.
Very often it happens that there is a flow of blood from an artery which has been cut at a wound, which happens externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or a cautery of a limb, and similar things to these; and it becomes difficult to stop it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then hasten with your hand to the orifice of the artery, and place your index finger upon it; and press it very much until the blood is constricted beneath your finger, and nothing goes out from it.
Then place small olive-wood cauteries into the fire, and a great many of them; and blow upon them until they become thoroughly ignited.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place in which the artery has been ruptured; and place the cautery upon it, after which you quickly remove your finger; and hold the cautery until the blood is cut off.
If it is expelled when you raise your finger from the opening of the artery and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery, from those prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease to do that with one after another until the blood is severed.
And be careful not to burn the nerve which is there, and lest other harm befall the weak patient.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to sever it: especially when the artery is large, except with one of four ways. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities are contracted and the blood is severed. Or it is tied with a strong ligature thread. Or medicines of a property which severs blood are placed upon it and it is constricted with a decent poultice constriction.
But he who performs its severance with a ligature and constriction with cloth or positioning of burning things and similar things does not quite bring it to a close except rarely.
But if it should happen to someone and there is neither a physician present nor medicine: then let him begin with the position of the index finger over the opening of the vein itself, as we have narrated, and a very strong constriction thereof until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed, but always with cold water of vehement coldness placed upon it until the blood freezes and thickens, and is severed.
And meanwhile he should consider what he needs from a cautery or medicine.
On Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in Wounds and Similar Things.
We have already said in the First Chapter that every disease in which it is fitting to use fire with a cautery and burning medicine, its occasions, causes and instruments, and forms of cauteries.
And I have placed chapters there from the head down to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter the same way so that it may be relieved for the seeker who seeks his question.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you learn, O sons, that in this chapter is from the fear that which is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And for that reason it is necessary that there be more caution in it, because in the operation which is in this chapter, very often evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision over an abscess, or opening of an exit, or treatment of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and those similar to them: from all of which trembling and fear are associated, and death falls upon many of them.
And I warn you, lest that which is hesitation falls upon you, for sometimes there fall upon you in this art ways of men with ways of illnesses. For from them is he who becomes angry with his illness, and death is light to him because of the vehemence of his which he finds from his sickness and length of its malice. And with illness is from fear which signifies death.
And from them is he who gives his flute player and enriches you with him, hoping for healing, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore it is not necessary that you consent to him who comes to you from them, whose way is such. And let your caution be more vehement than your greed and avarice. And do not presume upon anything from him unless after certain knowledge which verifies with you that to which praiseworthy succession arrives.
And use in the treatment of all your patients, prognosis and indication as to what is conveniently for their health.
For in that there is help to you on the acquisition of fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire you His intellect, and not take away from you direction and inspiration: for that is in his hand.
And I myself ordained this chapter distinguishing by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter of Cautery, from the neck down to the feet: so that it may be eased for you what you desire from it, and what you need.
On the treatment of Water which gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter I.
Know that this infirmity often happens to children at birth: or when the midwife tightens the head of the child without ease.
And whenever it also happens from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I myself have not seen this illness in others than in children: and to all of them whom I saw from them, death hastened: therefore for that reason it seemed to me to let go of the operation on it.
And I myself saw from them a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger each day, until it could not sit upon itself because of the size of its head: and the moisture increased until he died.
And this indeed moisture, either gathers between the skin and bone: or it gathers under the bone above the suture.
And the operation in that case, if the moisture is between the skin and bone, and it is a small abscess, then it is necessary for you to make an incision in the middle of the head one finger wide: and let the length of the incision be as much as two knuckles, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of a spatula.
If therefore the moisture is more increased and the abscess is larger, then make those two fissures, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open on every side; and the water is depressed when you press it with your hand to its interior, and it is not concealed from you: then it is necessary for you to cut in the middle of the head with three fissures, according to this manner.
And after the incision extract all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and lint. Then embrocate it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and care for the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not let go binding the head with equality. And feed the weak person with all dry food of little humidity, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another manner of incision is to consider where the magnitude of the abscess appears, and the collection of water.
For sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the vertex, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore ascend with the incision where you see the abscess and the filling of water; and split it according to what is possible for you.
And observe lest you fall upon an artery and there should occur a flow of blood, and the weak person should die from that flow with the evacuation of the moisture.
Concerning the Incision of Arteries Which are Behind the Ears.
Chapter II.
When it happens to someone that there are catarrs to the eyes, or to the chest, and it grows old, and healing is not brought by medicines; then the last cure in that case is the incision of those two arteries.
Therefore it is necessary when you wish to cut them, for the weak person to shave his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries may appear. Then let the weak man tie his neck with any of his cloths. Then consider where a vein pulses. And the places of both are the depressed places behind the ears: and they are rarely concealed, except in some men.
Then mark with a cautery above each vein; and cut through each with a sharp bistoury on both sides, the incision reaching to the mouth; and let this be done across the breadth of the head.
And if you wish, insert the bistoury under the artery and cut it upwards with an incision.
Let the length of the incision be like two fingers joined together.
For when a vein is cut, blood flows out in a throbbing stream, which leaps to the insides with frequent jumps.
But if the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear a space of three fingers. Then mark with a cautery and cut down to the mouth.
And what is necessary to let out from the blood is six ounces; and perhaps you may let out more or less from it. All of this according to the quantity which appears to you from the weakness of the man and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains at the mouth of the wound from the cutting, cut that off so that a hot abscess may not occur; then bind the wound with bandages from linen cloth.
Then care for it with ointment until it heals.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from an old headache or sharp catarrh due to acrid humors and heat in the temples' muscles, or an old, vehement soda and similar ailments: and he is treated with methods of medical care and it does not work; then we have found experience in these illnesses of extracting arteries from the temples, or cauterizing them as we have described.
The manner of operating in the extraction of these is that the sick person should shave the hairs which are on the temples and stretch towards an apparent artery in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you from its pulse and is rarely concealed except in a few men or by the violence of cold.
But if it is concealed from you, then let the sick person bind their head with any cloth of theirs; then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with hot water until it appears to you manifestly. Then take the speculum, which I fashioned a little before. Then with it scrape away the skin easily until you reach the artery. Then thrust a hook into it and draw it upwards until you draw it out of the skin and make it come out from all the tunics that are under it.
But if the artery is slender: then enfold it with the extremity of the hook. Then cut from it a part according to the amount by which its ends are lengthened and contracted so that bleeding does not occur. For if it is not cut and not severed, the blood does not ascend thoroughly.
Then evacuate from the blood six ounces up to three.
But if the artery is large: then it is necessary that you tie it in two places with a strong double silk thread or with threads of aloe, so that putrefaction does not hasten to it before the consolidation of the wound and bleeding occurs.
Then cut away the excess which is between the two ligatures. Do this in that hour or another hour afterward.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone, with a knife cautery: until its ends are incised and it stands in place of the same operation.
And it is better work unless he who is weak has a fever or an overheated complexion. For cautery is one of those things which help to finish humidities. Therefore it is a better remedy.
And it is necessary after the extraction of arteries that the place be filled with old cooked lint and poultices made loosely be placed upon it.
And after the loosening it should be treated with dry medicines which cause flesh to grow and with ointments until it is healed.
But if while you are operating it should happen that there is a flow of blood from the artery then hurry to cut it off with a cautery or to fill the place with wax and press your hand upon it until the bleeding stops. But if none of those things are present to you then put your finger upon the place until the blood congeals. And embrocate the place with water of strong cold until the sharpness is calmed and it is bound as needed.
And because it is lighter and easier for the extraction of a vein it is to cauterize a vein with this cautery having two knives and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to a razor. However they are of much less sharpness than two knives. For if they are sharp like two knives the cold rushes to both and they do not strike the flesh with speed. But since there is a little thickness in them the heat of the fire is retained in them and the flesh is cut quickly and by this means the work is better than all other works and lighter and easier.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the thickness of a finger after you mark two places with plaster and put both ignited strongly until they reach the mouth and strike the vein in two places so that what is between its ends is lengthened. For they are not thoroughly consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the treatment of sharp, persistent courses of tears from the eyes.
When tears are persistent, and their course is from the veins which are above the outer part of the head; and no cure with medicine is effective against them; and you see the face of the sick person already reddening, and he feels a movement in his forehead like the movement of ants: and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that his eyelashes are already corroded with erosion; and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tears: then the ancients used this operation for their cure, and they called it the Cure with the Sword.
And it is that you order the sick person to shave the hairs which are on his forehead. Then make three incisions in the forehead, equally spaced, according to the length of the forehead; and let the length of the incision be like two fingers, and one incision be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second a little after the length according to the movement of the temple muscles, and the third from the other side; and be careful not to cut the arteries which are on both sides; and extend your hand away from the continuation of the two mandibles, and let the distance of the whole incision be the size of three joined fingers; and have a piece of sponge prepared for you, or many dry cloths, with which you dry the blood.
Then introduce a speculum with two pointed ends, whose following form is soon: From the incision which is from the side of the temple, up to the second incision; and scrape out with it all the skin which is between the two incisions, with a knife, which is above the mouth. Then do that also from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then extract this speculum, and introduce into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp curette, blunt on one side and not sharp on the other. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the attached flesh with the skin, and its blunt side towards the opening; and press it until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut off with it all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes; except that the incision should continue up to what appears from the skin.
Then make that in the middle fissure up to another fissure.
And after an equal quantity runs out from the blood, press out the places from clotted blood fragments.
Then place in every fissure a pledget of old baked cotton, and put on them poultices soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And see that hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day dissolve the ligature, and administer a large infusion with warm water. Then care for it with oil of roses, and the rest by which wounds are healed: until it is healed.
Chapter Five.
Concerning the treatment of sharp tears and cataracts in the eyes from within the head.
When sharp, severe, persistent cataracts happen to someone; and you see his eyes weakened, small, and their appearance already debilitated; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is a sharp, painful pain deep in the head, and consequent sneezing: then take from these occurrences, that those matters, and cataracts do not come, except from many veins.
And the better cure for them is this. And it is, that you order the sick person to shave his forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of his forehead, or another a little further over by its width; and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be up to the bone; and extend your hand away from the muscles of the temples, which move at mastication: until when the bone is revealed; and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with an old knife blade or with linen threads. Then bind over it with lint; and moisten the lint with wine and oil so that a hot abscess may not occur.
And when you loosen it and see that the hot abscess has already subsided; then it is necessary that you cauterize the mouth until flesh begins to grow there.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that promotes the growth of flesh; such as if you take two parts from wheat flour and four parts from colophony; and prepare a plaster from them, and apply it to the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a flowing thing, such as water and vinegar, and things like them; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone or a kind of stone falls into the ear from those which are not added to and do not grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun; if you then see the stone, then distill something into the ear from oil of violets or oil of sesame. Then perform its extraction and make it sneeze with a powder, and plug the nostrils upon the approach of sneezing, after you place around the ear wads from cloth or wool, and extend the ear upward.
Very often it comes out with this treatment.
But if it does not come out, then perform its extraction with a Gestis, that is, fine Pizigaroli; whose shape is this.
If therefore it comes out with the Gestis, it is good.
And if not, then perform its extraction with a thin blind hook of few windings.
If therefore it does not come out through that, then make a tube out of metal and insert the end of the tube into the ear opening far in, and seal what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil so that there is no way for air except through the tube: then draw it out with your own strong suction.
Very often indeed it comes out with what we have described. But if it does not come out this way: then take a small amount from hellebore, or from birdlime, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are caught; and put it on the end of the probe after you have wrapped upon it a bandage decently placed: then insert it into the ear opening with ease, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have described, then hurry to cutting, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the manner of incision is, that the phlebotomist should first make a small cut on the head: and draw out blood from him according to his strength. Then make the patient sit between your hands and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near his fat in a hollow place: and let the incision be moon-shaped until you reach the stone; then remove it with what is possible for you from the instruments.
Then sew up the incision immediately with speed and take care of it until it heals.
But if the thing falling into the ear is from one of the grains which grow and swell: then apply its extraction.
But if it does not yield to your exit, then take a thin, subtle spatula according to this form.
And apply it with an incision of that type from the grains which fall into the ear and do not do this when you think that grain has already been moistened by vapor from the ear until you put many splinters on it: then draw them out with hooks or with a subtle instrument, or with suction as we said for its extraction is alleviated.
For the water which enters the ear, it is necessary that a weak person should use sneezing when it is first placed: and now he has filled his ear with some old cotton; and he is lying upon that ear, in which there is water. Therefore if it goes out through that, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them with fire for a little while, and let the weak person insert one of them into the hole of his ear: and he should jump on one of his feet from that side, and strike with another stone upon the stone which is in the ear. Therefore let him not cease to do this with stone and stone until all the water comes out.
And now the water comes out so that you can take one from a tinderbox or from a reed and insert one end of it into his ear; and ignite the other end with fire until most of it is burned. Then repeat with another reed and do this many times until all the water comes out; and extract it with a tube as was previously stated for a stone.
But in the extraction of an animal entering into it, if it is small-bodied, like a flea, and the like, take care of it as I said in the division.
But if it is large-bodied and appears to the senses; then administer its extraction with gestures and hooks; and its matter is easier for all that are held in the ear.
But in the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you care for it with that which we said in the division and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not succeed; then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. Therefore if something from the worms appears there, then extract it with gestures or with fine hooks.
If nothing appears there, then take a cannula, which has this shape, narrow below, wide above; and introduce the slender extremity into the ear according to the amount that the weak person can tolerate: then suck with it strongly. Do that many times until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to come out, then wrap the cannula's circumference with wax, as I said regarding the stone.
But if they do not come out with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the destruction of worms, and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your infusion with which you pour oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, of which the form is depicted here. As you see, make it yourself from silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there should be a small hole, wide above.
Therefore let what is in the cavity of the cannula, be made of bronze appropriately. And if you wish, take a ray and wrap its extremity with cotton in a decent winding: then pour oil or juice or what you wish from these medicines into the cannula, and let the cannula be in the ear; then introduce the ray with the cotton on top and press it with your hand in a moderate compression until the oil is driven into the cavity of the hearing and the weak person feels it inside: and what is poured into the ear should already be warmed a little with fire: and beware that nothing which is distilled into it is very cold: for medicines do not tolerate it.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the care of accidental hair removal from the Ear.
Sometimes certain infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it also sometimes happens that hair removal occurs in the ear canal from a wound or flesh born there.
And this incision is sometimes made deep within the hole of the ear, which does not follow sight; and sometimes it occurs near the outer hole a little, and it follows sight.
That which occurs deep within the ear, and which sight does not grasp, is difficult to heal in many conditions.
And in that which sight follows, it is necessary that you place the sick person's ear in the sun, and look at it: then if you see an apparent incision, open it with a subtle speculum, whose form is as follows, let its extremity be in which there is a small width, of which a part is sharp; and let the remaining speculum be of two smooth sides: so that it does not injure the ear.
But if the incision is flesh-born in the ear canal: then hold it with a subtle hook, and cut into it with vehement ease, until all the flesh is removed.
But if the incision is deep within the ear: then take a subtle, gentle ray, and warm it slightly with fire; then send it into the ear canal.
If therefore you feel the incision with it, which the sick person finds with difficulty of hearing, try to open it with ease; and be careful not to wound the nerves of the ear.
Then place a licorice stick in the ear according to the quantity of the amplitude of the hole, which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days, until you are sure of the consolidation of the wound.
Or take licorice, and infuse it in rose water, and sprinkle ground zig over it, and use that.
But if you see the ear already inflamed: then it is necessary that you remove the licorice, and allow it with another licorice, which you wrap in cerate made with rose oil, until the hot inflammation subsides; then care for it until it heals.
If blood flow should occur, then immerse a sponge or cloths in cold water and place them on the ear; and use the remaining treatment which separates the blood flow as described in the treatise on distillations.
On the Treatment of Cataracts.
Chapter Eight.
These cataracts which occur in the eyelids are sometimes humid and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary to hold them with tweezers or with a hook and to cut into them at their roots with a scalpel. Then place pounded zedoary on the spots.
But if blood should flow from them, then cauterize them with a fine lentil-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For very often they return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and they do not return.
On the Treatment of Granules of the Eyelids.
Chapter Nine.
Very often something occurs in the eyelids which resembles a grain in its firmness and hardness, and on account of this it is called a Granule; it is an aggregation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore the operation on them is to consider then if it is a Granule proceeding from within, which appears from the eyelid and moves in all directions and its matter is easy.
Then make an incision over it across its width. Then scrape it away from all sides with ease until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
But if the incision does not prepare for you its separation unless after you penetrate the eyelid with a slight incision, then it harms the weak person somewhat.
If the fissure is large: then bring it together with suture; and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and makes it flesh.
And if the hailstone is sloping towards the inner parts of the ear towards the inner surface, then invert the eyelid and suspend the hailstone with a hook: besides which you will need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, it does not harm anything.
Then bathe the eye after the incision of the hailstone with salt water and treat the place with that which makes flesh until it heals.
Concerning the care of the eyelids.
Chapter Ten.
Axirnath is a fat which generates in the coats of the upper eyelid and it most often happens to children. It weighs down their eyes and they get catarrhs in the mornings and cannot look at the light of the sun because tears rush to them, and therefore you see them lying on their faces always and sleeping on them and their eyelids are wet under their eyebrows, which has already been covered by inflation and the eminence of Axirnath is visible to the eye and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the manner of its operation is that he who has it should put his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare lye in it and make from that lye a circle according to the amount which contains the Axirnath on every side. Then put it on top of it and press with your fingers from every side so that the Axirnath gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with a scalpel making incisions across its breadth and let there not be an incision larger than that which is made in phlebotomy in depth.
Either you must cut through the whole skin until you reach the Axirnath, for in many dispositions it arises from a fissure according to the shape of a white fat wedge.
Therefore extract it with the cloth which you inserted between your fingers to the outside, and you will turn your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed; and beware lest you add anything in the fissure so that you do not reach the eye with the spatula and injure it.
But if the Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the fissure, you will add a little into the fissure with ease until the Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it as we said.
Then soak cloths in vinegar and water and place them over the area and compress it with bandages.
And there are people who rub salt and place it in the cavity of the fissure so that it liquefies what remains from that moisture.
Then care for it until it is healed.
But if a hot abscess should happen in the place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it is healed.
Concerning the ways of treating Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid softens and it becomes wrinkled as it should not.
Chapter XI.
When hairs in addition to the course of natural hairs are born on the eyelids and become inflamed, then they harm the eye and cause illnesses such as constant tears, softening of the eyelids, and white spots until it becomes a cause for destruction of the eye.
And indeed, Ataxmir of the eye is treated according to four methods. Either by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what was previously stated in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is treated by incision and suture. Or through reeds, as I shall say.
You must place the head of the sick person in your lap. Then turn back the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If then it is turned back, it is good. And if not, introduce a needle into that part of it where the thread is located from the lower part of the eyelid and make the needle with the thread penetrate from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid in which they are born; and draw the thread upwards together with the eyelid and turn it back with a rod.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid below the added hairs with a speculum inserted from the greater lacrimal duct to the lesser lacrimal duct.
Then extract the thread and place under the eyelid a small ball of cotton or cloth; then mark on the eyelid with an unguent, which is like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However it is necessary that the figure be made according to the quantity and elevation of the eyelid; because it now varies among men.
For some of them need a large amount to be cut from the eyelid according to the amount which is softened by the eyelid.
Then with a speculum find above the two lines which you have marked and begin from the greater lacrimal duct to the lesser lacrimal duct, and let there be one fissure near the natural hairs, as is the thickness of a rod.
Then introduce a hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then flay the entire thing itself. Then join the two lips together with a needle and fine woolen thread; and cleanse the blood, and make what overflows from the threads adhere to the two eyebrows, with certain invisible things if you wish to do it; and if not, do not care.
Then let the suture and threads remain for three or four days; afterwards care for it.
And if you wish, let the wound be without suture and care for it with that which dries and constricts. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing of the wound and its gathering; but it will be better in the future.
This method of healing ancient teachers have taught.
Truly there is anxiety about the sick person and it is from good practice and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, to make a sign on the eyelid in the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three hooks separate or joined, according to this following form; then cut off what is superfluous with small forceps, according to this form, with equality of incision.
But if it is not possible for you to hold the hook and it does not equal it; then take a needle in which there is thread and insert it into the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger tear duct and a second thread near the smaller tear duct; and join the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation; and raise the skin above which is the marked spot as we have narrated.
Then join the two lips of the wound with suture and care for it until it heals.
But if an abscess should occur at your incision or fissure: soothe the abscess with cerate and things like it from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelids also that their hairs are turned.
Therefore, you ought to use what we have said in the incision; but the suture for both is more skillful. On the care of Ataxmir with dogs
And it is, that you turn back the eyelid and split the fissure which is inside, according to what I have said. Then artfully make two reeds, or two slender sticks, the length of which shall be according to the length of the eyelid, and their width less than the width of a spatula; and you have already incised at the extremities of each on both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully place both upon that which overflows from the eyelid of the eye, and tighten the two reeds on both sides with a strong constriction, and leave it for several days. For the tightened skin will die, darken, and rot until it falls off by itself.
If however it is delayed, then cut it with forceps. Then care for it until it heals.
When therefore it firms up, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not sting.
And this is the form of the two reeds, which you will make according to this figure and quantity in length and width.
Chapter XII.
On raising stinging hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation on this is, that you take a fine needle and insert into it a fine, soft silken thread. Then gather its ends and tie both firmly with a delicate knot; and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place another fine thread briefly underneath it in the Alinxotati, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the patient place his head in your lap and be near the sun so that the operation may be illuminated; for it is a delicate or minute operation.
Then insert the needle with the Alinxotati into the root of the eyelid and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you draw out the natural hair from above.
Then draw the hair up above the ligature; and insert into the ligature that hair: if there is one, or two, or three, no more.
Then draw your hand with it until the hair comes out with the ligature into the eyelid with a natural hair.
But if the ligature is drawn and the hair does not come out with it: you draw the ligature down, with the thread which you have put into it, until the ligature comes out of the upper hole, and you see it with your eye; then indeed you draw the ligature and the thread; and extract both, and close the eye; and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
But if the hair is short, then either join to it a long hair from the natural hairs, and elevate it with it; or leave it alone until it lengthens after a time, and then raise it.
Chapter XIII.
When this alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called leporine.
And this alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental therefore arises from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things to those.
And the method of operation on it is that you open that consolidation, and separate its lips, and place in it a ligature from linen, and tie it until it heals.
And it is not necessary for you to use things in its treatment which dry and constrict: for if you do that, the alxatrati returns worse than it was; rather use on it things which soften, such as fenugreek; and embrocate with water in which marsh mallow has been cooked, flax seed: and an unguent called dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some oil; and wrap it with a ligature, and care for it.
And the treasure of your healing is to try with all your ingenuity that it does not solidify according to the form which it had at first.
And this operation which we have said is nothing but a certain rectification of the Alxatrati so that the form of the place does not return according to what it was at all.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid and is a wrinkle or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter Fourteen.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is that which is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural and accidental.
And the accidental kind arises from a wound, or fissure, or cautery, and things similar to these.
And the way of operating on it is to take a needle with a double thread and stain it with flesh color and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upward with the needle and cut it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should be, it is good.
And if it does not [return as it should], take a ray and place it in the fissure and turn it with the eyelid; and make two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and an angle is made from them: so that when they are joined, their shape becomes this shape, which is the letter lambda in Greek.
Then remove that flesh by an amount equal to its lower sharp side from that which follows the eyelid. Then join the separate parts, or sew them with wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said about softening medicines and those that are clean until they are healed.
And if a luxation should occur from a fissure, and suture or cautery: then it is necessary that you split the fissure flat beneath the eyelashes, even according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with lint, according to what we said.
And the sum of the discourse on the care of luxation, when it is above or below, is that the operation should run in it, according to what is prepared from the form of the luxation.
For it itself is of many varieties in form; and a prudent craftsman prepares his ingenuity with whatever way is possible for him, until he may return the figure to its natural form, or near it, and he himself infirm, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid of the eye with the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid consolidates with the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore it is necessary that you insert the extremity of a radius under the eyelid and elevate it upwards; or extend it with a hook: then incise the adhesion with a fine spatula, which should not be with the sharpness of an incision of spatulas, but rather be somewhat blunt: so that the infirm person is not disturbed at the operation, and the spatula is removed and injures the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be as if you were scraping away that adhesion with ease, until when the eyelid returns according to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, with blue water itself; or distill into it from a sieve made, from ziniar dissolved with water.
Then separate between the eyelid and eye with lint of flax; and place wool imbued with egg white on the eye; and after the third day use a sieve, and with dressings, until it is healed.
On the cutting of the nail and the prominence of the flesh of tears.
Chapter Sixteen.
Know that the nail is according to two manners. Either it is nervous, and it is similar to a hard, thin thread. Or it is not nervous, similar to white frozen moisture; which when it touches iron, or you try to take it with a hook, is cut off, and the hook does not stick in it.
And both species do not begin except from the larger lacrimal duct, until it proceeds gradually, until it covers the sight, and prevents light and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operation in its cutting is: that the sick person should place his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and lift his eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of few twirls, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hairs of a horse, ox, or cow; and let the thread be strong, and double the extremity of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail; and pierce it with the needle, and bind the nail with thread, and extend it upwards; and flay the side of the nail which follows the pupil with the hair as if you were to press it with the hair up to its last part.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the lacrimal with a fine scalpel, or with small forceps; and let the natural lacrimal flesh be released so that a constant flow of tears does not occur from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone; or with thread as we have said. Then flay it with a gentle scalpel, which has this form.
And beware that the scalpel does not touch the corneal tunic, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye something ground salt, or made from alum, and let it remain until the next day. Then care for it with what is necessary until it heals.
But if the nail is not nervous and you cannot insert a point into it nor does a hook catch in it, then this type of nail is often cured with medicines and heals as I have described in the division.
But if you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the infirm person and take a delicate spatula, soft, weak in sharpness, according to this form. And scrape the nail from above with subtlety. But if you see that it thins and breaks, and the scraper presses into it, then distill from your hour into the eye alum made water, or red alum water, or ground salt: and bind the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation over it until all of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in your eye: then let it alone and care for the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation over it with a scraper until it heals.
But if the prominence of the tear duct causes great harm to the eye, suspend it with a hook and cut out a portion of it; and do not finish the incision so that the course of tears does not happen.
Then still red alum water or water made from alum into the tear duct until it heals.
Of the incision of a foreign body and flesh born in the eye.
Chapter Seventeen.
Sometimes red flesh grows in the eyes of men, composed or superimposed, until it covers the pupil or approaches and presses upon the eyelids and perhaps turns the eyelids outward; and it is likened to a rose baluster.
And the operation on it is also that the sick person puts his head on your lap. Then open his eyes, and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks: and hold it with one forceps, or hand.
Then cut the flesh first, until all of it is finished with a scalpel. And be careful from the eye, so that you do not injure it during the operation: and let your incision be as you cut it, either with a scalpel, which we have described in the incision of the nail; or with small forceps, with which the asebel is collected, according to its shape after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is complete, and all the chalazion is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt; or distill into it red powder, and the like corrosive medicines from it: and place cotton wool with egg white on the eye outside; so that it is protected from a hot abscess.
But if anything remains from the chalazion, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess: then let go of the eye, and treat it with that which calms the abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it until it is healed.
And do likewise with the added flesh that occurs in the eye from this kind.
However, it is necessary that others treat the cure of all weak eyes from natural or accidental ways by operating with iron: and do not put your hand into any of these operations until you see that the member can tolerate the operation.
On the Gathering of an Asebel from the Eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Asebels are red veins that are woven over the eye, and prevent vision from its operation: and they weaken the eye with the length of days.
Therefore it is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye in which there is an asebel is strong and there is no sickness in it except for the asebel: then gather its asebel.
And direct the sick person to put their head in your lap.
Then suspend those good ones with one hook, or two according to your discretion; and let the hook be a fine one of double thickness, according to this form, or let there be two hooks joined together in one body, according to this form.
Then gather them carefully with fine forceps and wipe away the blood hour by hour until you see those veins receding from the eye and resolving; and be careful not to injure the eye with the points of the speculum.
Let your operation be in the middle of the day in front of the sun and be very firm in your operation so that you do not strike anything except those veins.
And when you have finished, then distill green vitriol or red into the eye so that its sharpness may corrode what remains from the sebum.
But if it is not possible for you to gather it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with something that soothes the eye's pain and leave it for several days until its pain subsides; and guard against a warm abscess; then repeat the process on it in the same way until it heals.
And this is the form of the forceps.
On the reduction of a nasal polyp.
Chapter 19.
Physicians call a nasal polyp Fistula, which you should treat with a cautery or with an acute burning medicine according to its preceding description.
And if it does not heal then there is no skill in it except to cut above the abscess at its origin and extract all the moisture and pus from it until the opening is exposed.
Therefore when the opening is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, then excise it with an instrument of this form.
And it is called Asperi Capitis.
Let it be made of iron indo, and let its head be round like an azir, which should be punctuated with a file punctuation, or a subtle aliskilfer punctuation; and place it upon the corrupted place of the bone.
Then revolve it between your fingers, and press your hand upon it a little while, until you know that the corruption is already smooth; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying astringent medicines.
And if the place is consolidated and flesh arises therein, and the course of blood is cut off; and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, nor does it fester, nor does any accident happen to it: then you will know that it is already healed.
And if not, there is no skill therein except to reduce the fistula to the nostril foramen, according to this manner.
And it is, that you expose the second bone with iron, or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone has been exposed, then take an auger according to this manner, let its iron end be triangular; and let its wood be a pyramid tapered down to the extremity, as you see.
Then place it upon the bone itself; and let it be near the lacrimal; and extend your hand a little from the eye. Then revolve your hand with the auger until it penetrates the bone and feels weak; by this it finds wind escaping from it when holding his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When flesh therefore arises, and the place hardens: then the material which was pouring out to the exterior returns to the nose; it is therefore lighter upon the weak one.
On the Reduction of an Eye Eminence.
Chapter XX.
When an eye protrudes with its totality and neither harm nor diminution occurs to the vision; then it is necessary that you loosen the weak one; then phlebotomize him; afterwards apply cupping glasses to the neck without scarification but with suction.
Then place upon the eye a liniment which is made from acacia, aloe, olibanum, and sarcocol. Then bind the eye above with a plaster and many poultices.
And the manner of the poultices is: that you should take many soft cloths and double each cloth from them four times; make many from them; then place them one upon another, according to the quantity which the eye needs; then bind over them with a hard cloth a strong binding.
And repeat cuppings with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cuppings and let the eye remain bound day and night.
But if the bandage again becomes loosened, then it is necessary that it should be bound tightly; then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we have said.
Therefore if you see the eye has already returned to its former tunic, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster, poultices, binding, and cuppings until it heals.
Concerning the incision of the tunics.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the tunic of the eye, and then it protrudes and proceeds beyond the eyelids like a grain of the eye, and disfigures the form of man because of it, and you wish to excise it; then the operation on this is as I shall relate: it is to insert a needle into the root of the eye from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle into which is a double thread from the lacrimal part and make it penetrate; and let the first needle go according to its own disposition.
Then cut the place of the double thread and tie it with the part of the eye around the needle; and bind it tightly.
Then extract the needle and place upon the eye wool soaked in egg white; and let it remain until the thread falls with the eye.
Then care for the eye with that which strengthens it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes it is bound by a circle of cloth from all sides, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then pierce it with a fine speculum, which reaches the depth of the eye, and let the white humidity flow, and let the eye descend, and enter immediately; then bind it until it is healed.
On the treatment of Alconati.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
This ailment which is called Alconati is nothing but blood which gathers in the eye, similar to descending water, and it is not itself.
And the method of operation on it is that you make the weak person sit upon a prepared seat, or upright.
Then take his head with your hands on both sides and move it until you see the blood arrive at the lower parts of your eyes. Then fix it so that it does not descend and illuminate the light; and let the weak person see things as he used to see; and if it does not descend below, you know that it is water.
But if the descent of the blood is not prepared with what we said, then make the weak person sit between your hands. Then take a fine speculum and pierce with it above the corneal membrane a little at the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic in the crown until the blood comes out.
Therefore, when it comes out, then distill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then care for it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
On the treatment of water, which descends in the eye, or cataract.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already said the kinds of water in division, and which is suitable for piercing by speech declaring and explaining, then take that same one according to health.
Then it is necessary that you make the weak person sit square between your hands, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind his sound eye, and constrict it strongly; then draw down the eyelid of his eye with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then take the lancet in your right hand, if the eye is the left one; or in your left hand, if the eye is the right one.
Then place the point of the lancet near the cornea for the width of a radius in the very whiteness of the eye, appearing near the lesser lacrimal; then thrust the lancet with force; and you will revolve your hand with it until it penetrates into the albuginea of the eye; and feel that the lancet has now reached the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the lancet's journey to its depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of blackness; and this is the cornea of the eye. For you will see these things in the very pupil by the vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the corneal tunic.
Then place the lancet up to the place where the water is. Then press it down below with force, one after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the weak person sees that upon which he opens his sight immediately, and the lancet in his eye.
Then rest a little while. If therefore the water descends, then deposit it a second time, beyond which the lancet goes out.
When therefore it is fixed and does not ascend; then extract the lancet easily; and you will revolve your hand gradually.
Then dissolve something of clear rock salt in water; and wash the eye within it. Then place a whole cotton pad, or wool soaked in rose oil and albumen, outside the eye; and bind it with the sound eye.
We, and those of our time, place ground cumin with egg white over it.
But if the almond does not yield to entry into the eye because of its hardness, since it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then you must take a speculum called an Alberid, which has this form.
Therefore pierce only the conjunctiva with it and do not insert your hand into the opening: and it is only so that you may make a way to the almond's place, which is narrow.
Then introduce the almond as we said.
And when your work is complete, prepare a well-made bedchamber for the weak one in which he may sleep on his back in a dark house; and prohibit him from all movements and coughing; and give him food which soothes his nature; and let him not move his head to the right nor to the left at all.
Let the bandage remain according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then loosen it in that dark house and make him try his sight; and have him look at things. Then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary that he do this during the hour of healing or immediately after the incision; rather, let others do it because water rises quickly through a violent gaze.
But if a hot swelling occurs then you must loosen the eye before the seventh day and correct it with that which soothes the swelling until it is soothed; then release his sight and place an oral remedy on his face by which his vision is regulated for several days, he himself being in that dark house.
Then let him gradually leave the house and engage in his usual activities.
And know that a disciple is not excused from vision several times in surgery; then let him dare to perform the operation.
And indeed, there has come to us a certain one from those who are of Al-Ayraq, who said that it was done in Al-Ayraq a perforated well, by which water is drawn up.
But therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that, nor have I read it in any of the ancient books, and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from a well, so that it stands upon it who does not know them.
Let them be made of bronze properly: and let their ends be with the same delicate triangularity, and pointed end.
Chapter XXIV.
On the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes there are born in the nose various added fleshes, from which there is something that resembles a scorpion of many feet: and from it is cancerous flesh, stony, of a dark color: and from it is that which is soft flesh, not of a dark color.
Therefore, in that which is soft flesh from these fleshes, not deceitful nor cancerous, it is necessary for you to make the infirm person sit between your hands opposite the sun, and open their nostrils, and project a hook into those fleshes: then draw them outward, and cut what you have grasped from them with a thin sharp scalpel on one side, until you know that all the flesh has now been removed.
And if something remained from it of which incision is not possible: then scrape that with one of the subtle instruments easily, until nothing remains from it. But if blood dominates you, or a hot abscess occurs: then oppose it by what is necessary: or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hurry to cauterize it until the blood is severed and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar, and water, or wine should be projected into the nose after the incision. But if the nose is opened and moisture proceeds from it as far as the palate, then you know that it is already healthy.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that there is flesh grown inside it upon the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary that you introduce a linen thread which has some thickness, and knots in it many knots; and place between each knot the quantity of a finger, or less: and contrive, so that it sends one end of the thread into the nose with a rod, or with whatever is possible for him after he has made it like a razor; and draw out his odor, until it reaches as far as the palate, and emerges over his throat.
And indeed very often they do something like this to children in schools; and it is an easy thing for him who wants to do it.
Then place the two ends of the thread, the one end which emerges over the nose and the other which remains inside the nose. Then apply a flesh clamp with the knots that are on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is already held with the knots of the thread.
Then draw out the thread and after removing the blood put a linen cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose; do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Finally, at the end of the procedure, put a lead tube into the nose for several days until it heals.
But if it needs drying treatment, use that.
To make a nasal douche, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it from silver or bronze; which is like a small lamp, open like a shell; and its passageway similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed tube, like a reed.
And the bowl of the instrument for making a head bandage should be full and open, with a handle at its end by which you hold it when heating oil or whatever juice or fluid substances you wish in it.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
Very often a break occurs at the end of the nose, and it swells and increases with each day until its appearance is ugly.
And for this reason it is necessary that you cut into it at the beginning of its appearance and remove all of it. Then place upon the spot either a cautery or a burning medication which will stay in its place.
But if the incision is delayed until it swells, then consider if it is stony, hard, of a dark color, and has little sensation: then do not touch it with iron because it is a cancerous ulcer.
For I have often seen those who cut into such an ulcer and great harm befalls him who has it.
And if the ulcer is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that incision is possible throughout its entirety, then empty the weak spot and cut into it without fear or hesitation: and treat the area with something which dries and constricts it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
On the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a break occurs in the continuity of one of these cartilages, surgery makes little difference in it unless in certain people.
Therefore when this happens to someone, consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood: and then join the two lips of the wound with sutures; then treat it until it heals.
And if the lips of the break are already separated and each part has become healthy, then you must scrape away all the skin from its apparent surface until it bleeds. Then join two lips with suture, and tighten them, and sprinkle dragon's blood and ground frankincense upon it; and place a piece of diaphycon plaster, and one from another of the consolidating plasters, above the powders; and let it be tied for two days, or three. Then untie it, and change the medicine; and let it be so until the threads are cut off by themselves. Then care for it with ointment, until it is healed.
The method of suture, however, is to join the solution of continuity either with needles, as we have described in the suturing of the abdomen; or join the solution with thread, as I made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
On the Extraction of Nodes of the Lips.
Many men indeed have hard boils, similar to grains of herbs, occurring mostly in the inner parts of their lips; and some of these are smaller, and others larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn the lip, and cut over all of its node, and suspend it with a hook, and incise it from every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with powdered zedoary, until the blood is cut off.
Then wash it with vinegar and care for the place with it, in which there is astringency, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the Incision of Flesh Born Upon the Gum.
Very often flesh grows upon the gum, which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore it is necessary that you suspend it with a hook; or hold it with forceps, and incise it at its root, and let the serum run, or blood.
Then place powdered zedoary upon the place, or other astringent, drying powders.
If that flesh returns after healing, for it often does, then cut into it a second time and cauterize it; for it will not return after cautery.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the reason of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, ugly crusts gather on the surfaces of teeth from both the inner and outer sides, and sometimes they become darkened, turning yellow or green, until corruption from that reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul through it.
Therefore it is necessary that you make the weak person sit between your hands and their head in your lap, and grind a millstone and tooth, where crusts appear to you like sand, until nothing remains of them, and similarly do so with blackness, greenness, yellowness, and others, until they are cleansed.
Therefore if what is from the beginning of the reason for them is taken away, it is good; and if not, then repeat the reason over them on another day, the second and third, until at last you achieve what you desire.
And know that a tooth needs razors of various shapes and many figures according to how it is prepared for your operation; therefore the razor with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the razor with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other shapes.
And these shapes of razors should be prepared by you, as you see.
Chapter Thirty.
On the Extraction of Toothache.
You must care for the tooth from its pain with all your ingenuity and delay in its eradication. For there is no substitute for it when it is eradicated, as it is a noble substance: until when there is no excuse for its eradication, then when the weak person is forced to eradicate it, you must strengthen and assure yourself of the aching tooth.
Very often pain deceives the sick person, and they think it is in a sound tooth and extract it; then the pain does not cease until the diseased tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen that from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore when a painful tooth is confirmed with you, it is necessary that you make incisions around the tooth with a scalpel, in which there is some virtue, until the gum is loosened on all sides; then move it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and the head of the diseased person between your knees: which you have now held well so that it does not move; then pull the tooth according to its straightness so as not to break it.
But if it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it under it from all sides with ease, and try to move it as you did at first.
But if the tooth is perforated or corroded, then you must fill that hole with cloth and tie it with the end of a fine ray, so that it does not break in the hour of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And you must act delicately with the incision all around the gum on every side a great deal.
And keep your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the sick person from that greater evil which is his first pain.
And beware, lest you do what ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste and boldness over its eradication, besides administering that which we have narrated.
For they very often cause great infirmities to happen upon men, which are more difficult to break and which leave all or some of its roots remaining; or that they extract it with certain bones of the jawbone: as we have testified many times.
Then let it be washed after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
And if it should happen that there is a flow of blood from the places (for it often happens), then apply something from wool tow and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if you do not have wool tow.
The shapes of the forceps with which a tooth is moved should first be long-shafted, with a short handle, thick so that they do not double when pressed upon the tooth: let them be as you see with thick handles, so that when you squeeze them, they do not give way or double: with short shafts, and made of good iron or steel, well fashioned, straight-shafted: and on their ends are teeth, some which enter others and constrict with a decent force.
And sometimes the ends are made like the shape of the mouth of a bird which is called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
On the removal of teeth, and the extraction of broken jawbones.
When something remains at the removal of a tooth which is broken, then it is necessary that you put upon the place cotton with butter for one or two days until the place softens: then insert into it an algast, or forceps: whose ends are assimilated to the mouth of a bird called a stork. This is the shape of these forceps.
Let their ends be made like a file within, or like a pair of pincers.
But if it does not yield to you towards its removal with these forceps, then it is necessary that you should carve upon the root, and detach all the flesh, and insert an instrument which is called a small Lever, whose shape is this.
Let it be short-shafted, slightly thick: and let it not be imbrued so that it does not break.
Therefore, if the root comes out through that, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to pull with these other instruments whose forms are these.
The shape of the first one is triangular at the end, in which there is a certain thickness.
The shape of the triangular end is slender.
And with other instruments and tools that preceded in the manner of teeth.
Pulling may also be done with this instrument which resembles a large hook, of this shape, with a twisted triangular end, in which there is some thickness so that it does not break; and let it not be soaked.
And know that there are many tooth instruments; and likewise the other instruments are not counted, and a skillful artisan, subtle in his art, finds an instrument for himself according to what the operation and illnesses themselves indicate to him.
For there are illnesses for which the ancients did not mention an instrument because of the diversity of their kinds.
But if the jawbone breaks from the mandible or from other bones of the mouth, or it putrifies: then carefully examine it in its place with what suits it from the instruments and forceps that I have mentioned in the extraction of roots, and pull with a pair of tongs, which has this form.
Let there be a certain gradual thickness in it so that the bone is drawn tight or held with it, and does not flee and wander, until the bone comes out; and restore the place with suitable medicines for that.
But if there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then scrape it from its putrefaction and care for it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
Concerning the Lock of Teeth Projecting Above Others.
When teeth project not according to their natural course, it becomes ugly through that; and especially when it happens in women and girls, namely those of marriageable age.
Therefore, it is necessary for you to consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and is not placed, or its serration is possible, nor its filing: then eradicate it; and if it is joined with another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, which has this shape.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of tempered iron, with a very sharp end.
And let your incision be such that you cut it into many days, and on account of the hardness of the tooth, and so that another is not disturbed from the teeth.
But if it is prominent and exposed to its filing, then file it with a file of tempered iron, which has this shape.
Let it be entirely of iron, and its handle of very small points, like a file with which needles are made; with which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not disturbed, and falls out; then soften it, and in the end eradicate it with certain razors.
But if there is a tooth from which a part breaks off and injures the tongue, then it is necessary for you to file it even until the roughness of that fracture is removed and it becomes equal; and it is made flat so that it does not injure the tongue nor corrupt speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
Concerning the Retention of Teeth with Gold Threads.
When the front teeth suffer a concussion and shock from a blow or fall, and the infirm person cannot bite down on it so that it does not fall out; and you have treated them with astringent medicines, and the cure has not worked; then there is an idea in this, for them to be tied with a gold thread; because silver turns green and rots after days: but gold never changes.
And let the thread be medium in subtlety and thickness according to the amount that it takes between the teeth.
And indeed the manner of retention is to take a thread and insert twice its length between two sound teeth; then you weave with the two ends of the thread among the loose teeth, whether there be one or several, until you reach with the ligature up to a sound tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the part from which you began; and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom until it is not at all moved; and let your constriction where you bind the thread be at the roots of the teeth so that it does not come loose.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the thread with forceps; and join both, and turn them over with a gesture; and hide them between a sound tooth and a loose tooth so that it does not injure the tongue; then let it remain thus tightened until it heals.
But if they become loosened or broken, bind them with another thread and it will hold thus for the entire time.
And this is the form of Teeth, and the figure of retention of two sound teeth and two loose teeth as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after a fall or blow, and are retained as we have described and remain; and this is not done except by a craftsman who is prudent and experienced.
And sometimes a piece of bone is adapted; and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in its place, from which the tooth is fortified and tightened, as we said; and it remains and is used with it for a long time.
Chapter XXXIV.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a person is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already healed.
And the operation on it is to open the mouth of the infirm person in your lap and raise their tongue; then cut that nervous ligament across its width until the tongue is freed from its restraint.
If there is something hard and knotted in it, and if that is from the consolidation of the wound, then thrust a hook into it and probe it across its width until you come upon a ligament and loosen the knot.
And be careful that there is no fissure deep in the flesh so that you might cut into an artery and bleeding occur.
Then wash the area of the incision with rose water or vinegar or cold water.
Then place a linen wick under the tongue, which the patient should hold in his mouth all night so that it does not heal again.
But if bleeding occurs, then put powdered eggshells over the area. But if the bleeding overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a lentil-shaped cautery which is suitable for that; and then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue which resembles a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it grows so large that it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is to open the patient's mouth before the sun and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is of a dark or black color, hard, to which the patient does not feel sensation, then do not touch it because it is cancer.
But if it is inclined toward whiteness and contains moisture, then thrust a hook into it and cut it with a fine scalpel and free it from every side. But if bleeding overcomes you during your operation, then put powdered eggshells on it until the bleeding stops; and then return to your operation until you extract it with all of its contents.
Then it should be washed with vinegar and salt; and let it be cared for with other suitable treatment until it is healed.
On the Treatment of an Abscess in Two Tonsils.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
Sometimes swellings occur between the throat and the palate, that is, glands which resemble almonds, and these are called two tonsils; when you treat them with what we said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
Therefore if it is a firm abscess, dark in color, with little feeling; then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is red in color and its root is thick; then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather let it alone until it matures. And then either pierce it or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is slender; then it is necessary to cut it open.
And the operation on it is such that you should consider before your operation whether its hot abscess has quieted completely with rest or diminishes somewhat; then have the weak person sit facing the sun, and let their head be in your lap; open their mouth, and let a servant hold it between their hands and press their tongue down with an instrument which has this form.
It should be made of silver or bronze, thin like a knife.
Therefore when you press it down with that, and the abscess is shown to you and your sight falls upon it; then take a hook and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outward as much as you can, except that you pull some of the coats out with it.
Then cut it open with an instrument which has this following form. It resembles forceps; nevertheless its tips should be curved so that the opening of each of them is directly in line with the other sharply; therefore take care that it is made of tempered steel or filled with alum.
If this instrument is not present, then cut it with a scalpel, which has this shape. Let it be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you cut one amygdala, cut the other according to this same kind of incision.
Then after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
But if a flow of blood should occur, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled; or myrtle leaves, and things similar to these astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped; then care for it until it heals.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat besides the two amygdalae. Therefore cut them according to what I said in the cutting of the two amygdalae equally.
And indeed I myself have already treated a woman from an abscess that had arisen between her throat, which was drawing it to choking, with few senses, because it had almost closed the throat; and the woman was breathing for me through a narrow passage, and had already prohibited her from eating and drinking water; and she had already come to death if she had remained for one day or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches until they came out above the two nostrils.
Therefore I hurried quickly and fastened a hook into one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion came out from it.
Then I cut it where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did that to her which proceeded from the other nostril.
Then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue; and afterwards I fastened a hook into the abscess itself; then I cut off a portion of it, and there did not flow from it except a little blood; therefore her throat was cleared, and she immediately began to drink water, and then she received food.
