Therefore I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times over a long period, and an abscess would succeed in the place where I cut it until it was prolonged too much and with it that.
Therefore I devised and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
It therefore ceased from adding; then I walked away, and I did not know what God did with it after me.
Concerning the cutting of an abscess of the uvula.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
When a catarrh descends to the uvula and becomes abscessed and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called uvula.
When it is treated with what we said in division, and the treatment does not avail; and you see that the hot abscess has ceased from it, and it is subtle; then it is necessary for you to cut into it.
And whatever from it is a rounded mass, and there is no length to it, and it is bloody, or tawny colored, or black, and there is no sensation to it; then it is necessary for you to avoid cutting into it. For in this there is fear over the weak one.
It is therefore necessary when you see it according to the manner which I said from its whiteness and its length, that you make the weak one sit before the sun; and press his tongue with the instrument of which we spoke previously; then fix a hook in the uvula and draw it downwards; and cut into it with one of the two instruments which I said in the cutting of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you not cut from it except that which is added above the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut from it more, you injure the voice and speech.
Then after the cutting use what we narrated in the cutting of the two tonsils; and care for it until it heals.
If, however, the patient is terrified of the incision: then it is necessary for you to use skill in cauterizing him without fear or trembling.
And the manner of cautery in this is not done except with sharp water; and it is, that you place the head of the patient in your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument which we have mentioned. Then take from the water which I told you in the chapter on cautery and mix it with an unquenched lime, and put it neither thick nor thin; and fill the hollow of this instrument, which has this shape.
Let the extremity in which the medicine is placed be a concavity, like the concavity of a snail's shell.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon his tongue; and let the patient lie on his side so that saliva runs from his mouth through the inward medicine, so that nothing descends from it into his throat and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you from the first place it upon his tongue for half an hour's time until you see it already blackened and the bite of the medicine quieted.
And if you wish to take cotton and wrap it over the extremity of the rod, and infuse the cotton with medicine and insert the rod with the cotton into the cannula from above until the cotton adheres upon the tongue.
Do that many times until you achieve what you desire concerning the cautery of the vein. Then dismiss it; for it will dry up and fall off after three or four days.
And if it is inconvenient for you to repeat the medicine, repeat it; and after cautery wash around the vein with cotton soaked in butter, and cleanse what is around it from the medicine; and gargle with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with embrocations and internally with gargles until it is healed.
Therefore this kind of treatment is healthier by cutting and farther from fear.
And sometimes a wound is healed even with something more subtle than cautery and incision; for it is healed with fumigation, in this way. Take calamint, hyssop, origanum, rue, sage, chamomile, wormwood, and similar herbs; and put them all together, or some of them, in a pot, and submerge them with vinegar, and let them boil; and the pot should be well plastered with clay; and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which an instrument shaped like a bowl is placed.
It should be made of silver or bronze; and the end in which there is a pomegranate seed should be inserted into the sick person's mouth until the vapor rises to the wound above the tube until the wound is darkened greatly.
Then repeat it over the wound many times until it dries up.
And be careful not to do this treatment at the beginning of an abscess's arrival. For it often adds to the abscess; and it is not necessary that you do this unless at the decline of its heat.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a reed and place bark on its end so that the sick person's mouth is not burned; for the bark of the reed prevents the vapor from burning the mouth; and this is a good healing with safety.
On the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone or a fishbone, or something else, sticks in the throat.
Therefore it is necessary that you extract what appears of them upon which sight falls, after you push the tongue with an instrument toward the sun, and it is shown to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary that you cause the sick person's stomach to be empty before food is digested in the stomach. Perhaps indeed it may exit by vomiting.
Or the weak one may swallow a piece of turnip, or a lettuce root, or swallow a morsel of dry bread; or take a piece of soft dried sea sponge: and tie it on a thread, then swallow it. Therefore when it reaches the place where the spines or bone are, and is drawn out, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we said: then use an instrument made of lead according to this form.
Let it be a little thicker than the radius, and at its extremity let there be a curve, which it may introduce into its throat with ease: and let it have its head raised upwards.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, lest he should have a cough, and push with it the mouth, or spine; or the physician may introduce it with his hand.
But what the weak one introduces is better, because of his knowledge of the place where the thing is attached: and he pushes it down, or draws his hand up with the instrument, all of it according to the amount that is prepared for him, until it comes out.
Concerning the Extraction of a Leech.
Chapter XXXIX.
When a leech is medicated with what I said in the division on treatment with medicines, and it does not detach, then consider in the throat of the weak one in front of the sun, or by the sun after you have pressed its tongue with the instrument that I narrated to you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with delicate forceps carefully.
But if it is not possible with them: then take a concave tube and introduce it into the throat of the weak one up to near the throat; then introduce into the concavity of the tube iron heated with fire. Do this many times; and let the weak abstain from all water for the whole day.
Then let him take a bowl full of cold water, and open his mouth into it; and moisten his mouth with it, and swallow nothing from it; and stir the water every hour with his hand. For the leech immediately falls when it feels the water.
But if it does not come out with what we have said, then fumigate the weak person with bedbugs, or with incense with the instrument which I have described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do this many times, for it will fall off itself.
And the manner of operation in fumigation is this: that you take a pot in which there are live plums with fire; and let the pot be covered with a lid, in the middle of which there is an opening; and place in that opening the extremity of the instrument.
Then project the fumigation, and let the weak person place his mouth at the extremity of the tube, and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not go out until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech immediately falls.
But if it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the weak person endure thirst, and eat salt, and garlic, and not drink water.
It is therefore necessary that it comes out with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat when it falls upon the sight.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And the extremity of it is similar to the beak of a bird which is called a stork, in which there is a roughness like a file; when it grasps something, it does not let go of it at all.
On the opening of Abscesses, and their incision, and treatment in general.
Chapter Forty.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of many types, according to the recollection of them, namely one, and one in this book: and they are diverse in their perforation, and incision itself from two ways.
One of which is from the kind of abscess in itself, and by that which it contains from humors.
And the second way is on account of the places, in which it happens in the body.
For an abscess happening in the head, is different from an abscess happening in the anus: and an abscess happening in a fleshy place, is different from an abscess happening in joints: and each of these has its own judgment from operation.
And from abscesses there are which ought not to be perforated, except after the maturation of blood in them, and its completion: and from these there are, which ought to be perforated while they are raw, and do not mature according to completion.
Such as abscesses which are near joints.
For when an abscess happens near a joint, and is prolonged to be its own, until it putrefies what is around it perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: therefore there is a cause for the closer contraction of that limb.
Or there is an abscess at a principal limb: for if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either that or through it the principal limb.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it immature: and it is not necessary that you perforate a raw abscess, not of complete digestion, except that which is near the anus, lest it putrefies deeply, and lengthens to the interiors of the anus, and becomes a fistula, or becomes at its end what does not heal.
It is necessary that you know the hour of perforation of an abscess, which has matured according to completion: and it is at sedation of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness, and throbbing, and sharpness of the head of the abscess: and the remaining signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same perforation in the lower place of the abscess, and of the more prominent eminence; and let the perforation be facing in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and places of tendons, and cords, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflex.
But in those that are reflected, go with the perforation according to that limb.
But when an abscess is in fleshy places, then it is better that you leave its perforation until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to the complement.
For if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus is prolonged from it, and there is much filth and foulness, and perhaps its lips will become hardened, and its depth.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the body when necessary, or according to that which the limb requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and a large perforation in large abscesses, or many incisions according to the likeness of the magnitude of the abscess.
And there are abscesses in which it is necessary that the skin be concave: and it should be incised when it has already been made like cloth, and made at the end, which is already dead: as it happens in many, such as boils, and buboes.
And of these are those which are cut with a section having three angles. And from these is one from which it is incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an abscess of the groin.
And among these are those in which a round section and a lunar section are administered, and similar ones to these sections.
But that which does not have a head, such as soft and superficial abscesses, it is necessary to perforate it with a superficial perforation, or only flat.
And it is necessary, when the abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, that you pierce it, so that you do not hasten and extract all the pus at that time; rather, extract a part of it from it. Then bind up the abscess until another day. Then extract some of the pus again.
Do this many times according to the progression, until it all comes out; especially if the patient is a weak person, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an old man: for the animal spirit is often resolved with the departure of blood from the animal; and perhaps the sick person dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore take heed of this chapter with great caution.
And after your piercing, by which you pierce these abscesses, it is necessary that you wash the wound; and consider. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or there is a flat incision, then apply a linen bandage, or an old tunic. And if the abscess is large, and there are many fissures of the perforation, it is necessary then that you insert linen into every fissure until they reach each other.
And if you have cut off a part of the skin in the abscess, or have hollowed it out around it; then it is necessary that you fill it with an old tunic, or as when cloth is woven, or shorn of linen fluff without moisture; and bind it up until the third day.
Then remove it, and care for it with what is necessary from ointment, until it heals.
But if bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water, and vinegar, after a cloth has been soaked in them; and place it upon the place of bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer regimen, and treatment, and powders which I have described in many places of this our book, and by division.
If it is in the winter time, and the place of the abscess is full of many nerves; then it is necessary that you pour warm broth with wine and oil on it, and place them over the area.
And if it is in the summer time, and the places are fleshy; then it is necessary that you place broth soaked in water and oil, or wine and oil, and all of that cold. Until when it will be the third day, as we said, then it is necessary that you open the abscess and clean it, and minister to its cure what suits it, until it heals.
This is therefore what is necessary from the knowledge of the cure of abscesses universally.
According to the path of distinction, I have now spoken of every abscess, how its way to cure is taken chapter by chapter individually.
Chapter 41.
On the incision of abscesses which occur in the skin of the head.
Small abscesses occur in the skin of the head, and they are of a kind of nodules, which contain sheaths, which are sacs to them: as if they themselves were like milked hens.
And their kinds are many.
Of these, some are fatty; and of these, some contain moisture like slime; and of these, some contain moisture like whey and gruel; and those similar to them; and of these, some are hard and stony.
And in the incision of all, and their extraction there is no fear, as long as an artery does not oppose at the time of their incision.
And the operation in the incision of them is that you try it with an instrument, whose shape will come after this, which is called an Intromissor, until you know what it contains.
For if it contains moisture, then dry it with an incision according to length with a superficial incision; according to this form.
And begin with a section from line b to line g. When therefore the humidity is evacuated, then remove the skin kistin which contains it, and do not leave anything at all within it.
For very often it returns if anything remains in it.
Then immerse the dressing in Egyptian ointment if it is present, and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it and leave it until the next day. For it will corrode what remained from the cyst.
Then repeat upon it a dressing with Egyptian ointment a second time and a third, and if necessary again, until you are confident that nothing remains from the cyst; then treat the wound with ointment until it heals.
But if the abscess contains a fatty node, then dry it according to this form, with a section having the shape of a cross; and insert hooks between the two lips of the section, and remove the skin from every side; and exert your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if an artery opposes you, then do what I will tell you afterwards.
And do the same in a stony abscess, from the incision and treatment, as we have said.
And the incision upon a stony abscess is easier; because there is little blood and humidity. And sometimes there are certain of these abscesses which are on the head in some people, in which there is absolutely no humidity.
For this is the reason: because I cut upon an abscess on the head of a young woman, and found the abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; and if one should strike it with something projecting, it would wound it.
But in other abscesses which occur on the head, besides these abscesses which occur on the heads of children and at the roots of ears, the incision of all of them should be superficial.
Or make a perforation in them always from their lower part, so that the course of blood is relieved towards the lower parts.
Then care for it with what suits them from treatment.
On the Section of Scrofulous Swelling in the Neck.
Chapter Forty-Two.
Very often these abscesses occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and groins: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And concerning those which occur in the neck from them, there are some single, and many: and they are generated from one another: and every scrofula from them is within its own tunic, just as it is in nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have related.
And there are many kinds of these scrofulae. And of them indeed, some are stony: and of them some contain humors; and of them some are malignant, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore in that one which you see of good disposition to the touch, of which it is evident that it is close in color to the skin, and moves in every direction, and is not joined with the nerves of the neck, nor with a sinew, nor with an artery, nor is deep: it is necessary that you cut it with a superficial section from the upper part to the lower part of the body from line b. to line g. and flay it from all sides: and extend two lips of skin with a hook, or with many hooks if it is needed as we said in abscesses of the head, and extract it gradually.
And there is caution to be exercised so that a vein or nerve is not cut into.
And let the scalpel not be very sharp: so that it does not add to your hand with the incision, or disturb the sick person, and you cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or artery, or something prevents you from the operation, then put on the wound litharge pounded, or some powders which staunch bleeding, and compress the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides, and the wound softens: or it begins and wants to putrefy.
For blood is cut off by a tourniquet; therefore return to your operation until you finish it.
Then inquire with your index finger if anything remains from the ligatures or other small ones; therefore cut them and clean them.
But if there is a large vein at the root of the ligature, then it is necessary that you not cut that ligature from its root; rather it is necessary that you tie it with a strong doubled thread and let it go until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old cotton, which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment. Then care for it yourself.
If the whole ligature is cut, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound; and sew it immediately after you see that there is no remaining superfluity at all.
But if you see that there is remaining superfluity because of the size of the ligature, then it is necessary that you direct yourself to cutting the root of it and extracting it; and administer the suture as we said.
And that which from the ligatures contains moisture, pierce it with a superficial perforation so that the place of its maturity appears to you; and put the perforation away from what follows below the body as we said.
Then administer after the perforation lint with ointment and the like so that it corrodes what remains from the decay until when you see the wound already cleansed; then care for it with generative ointment of flesh until it is healed.
Chapter Forty-Third.
On the Section of the Epiglottis from an Abscess Accidentally in the Throat.
The ancients said this section was in the epiglottis and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it; and this is the writing of their speech.
But in those who have synanche, it is necessary that the section of the epiglottis be performed because it does not bring benefit through it; therefore, because all veins and lungs are weak.
In those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no pain in the canal, it is necessary to use the incision of the epiglottis, to avoid the danger that arises from premature cutting.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we must cut the epiglottis under three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across its width, in the space between the two circles, so that the incision is in the mucous membrane, not in the cartilage; and this place is indeed suitable for incision because it is covered with flesh, and blood vessels are distant from it.
But if the surgeon is fearful, then he must stretch the skin of the throat with a hook. Then let him fix the skin until he reaches the canal: divert the blood vessels if he sees anything coming from them.
Then let him cut the mucous membrane as we have described; and it is recognized by the discharge of pus from it, with that which bursts forth, and the interruption of speech.
And leave the wound open for a time.
When therefore, the hour arrives in which premature cutting is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound of the skin and sew it alone without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it heals.
Says Albucasis. The sum of this discourse which we have described is not to be attempted except when they see that one of these abscesses has already choked the throat of the sick person; and he is raised up toward death, and wishes to interrupt his breath: they go to cutting the epiglottis so that the sick person may breathe through the wound with a certain respiration, and be saved from death; and for this reason they prescribed leaving the wound open until the malice of the sickness is consumed, and its malice lasts three days, or thereabouts; then they prescribed sewing up the wound and taking care of it until it heals.
And what I myself have seen is that one maidservant took a knife and sent it into her throat and it fell with it upon part of the pulmonary canal.
And I was called to her care and found her moaning like the moan of the dead.
Therefore I examined the wound and found little blood that had come forth from the wound. Therefore I was puzzled for she had not severed a vein nor a sinew and air was escaping from the wound.
I hastened therefore and sutured her wound and cared for it until it was healed and nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in her voice and she returned after days to a better state of health.
Therefore from this I say that there is no fear in the incision of the epiglottis.
Concerning the section of a hernia of the throat.
Chapter Forty-Four.
This swelling called a hernia of the throat is a large swelling according to the color of the body and it is found much more often in women.
And it occurs in two ways either it is natural or it is accidental.
In the natural one there is certainly no skill required.
The accidental one however is according to two ways of which one is similar to fatty nodes. And the other way is similar to a swelling that occurs from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear and one ought not presume to approach it with an iron except when it is small and you should test it and investigate it carefully with a probe. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty node and it is not suspended with any veins then cut it out as nodes are cut out and extract it along with what it contains from the cyst if it is in a cyst and if not then scrutinize its entirety. Then care for the place with what is necessary for healing until it is healed.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On the Section of Kinds of Knots.
There are many kinds of knots, and I have already said all their kinds in the division, and it is necessary that I tell here the difference between a knot and an excrescence, since it is a doubt-causing matter.
Therefore I say that when there is an excrescence, there is heat and fever and violent pains until the boiling of excess is settled and putrefaction is completed, then indeed the fever and heat subside.
But with a knot there is no heat nor fever nor pain, and it contains them in sacs called sifachin which are their own pouches, and they are according to the color of the body; and the beginning of them is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a bean, larger and smaller.
And they are according to two kinds: either fatty or containing humidity. And the colors of humidity are many, according to what I said in the division.
Therefore it is necessary, when you come to the healing of a knot, that you experiment on it and inquire into it first with an instrument called an intromissor, according to which its shape will come in the chapter which is after this.
And the method of inquiry for all abscesses and knots is to take this instrument and insert it into the wetter place which you find in the abscess; and you revolve your fingers with it gradually until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then finally place your hand according to the measure of the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the intromissor and consider that which comes out in its trail.
Therefore if humidity flows out, whatever its color may be, then dry it with a superficial incision, according to what I said in the other abscesses.
And if the humidity does not exit in the track of the Intromissi, then you will know that it is fat; therefore cut upon it with a section having the form of a cross, according to this form as I have taught you; and suspend it with hooks, and flay the skin from every part easily; and keep the kistin upon it if you are able, so that you may draw out the whole thing healthy with the knot.
But if the kistin is broken at the operation, and it is not possible to draw it out whole, for this often happens, then draw it out in pieces until nothing remains of it.
For if a little or much remains of it, the Knot returns most often.
And if it overcomes you, and a little remains of it: then fill the wound at its completion with certain corrosive and sharp powders; and bind the wound, and place upon it something that sits hot and poulticed, and care for it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
But if it is a large Knot, then suture its two lips together, and care for it as it grows into flesh.
But if a vein that is pulsating or not pulsating is contrary to you: then hurry and fill the place with triturated terebinth, and leave it tight for two days or three until the wound putrefies and the ebullition of blood subsides; then return to cutting that which remained from the Knot.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning external instruments for incision and perforation.
Of these are the forms of Intromissi, and they are of three kinds.
For of them there is a large one, and of them there is a medium one, and of them there is a small one.
Let them be made of tempered iron with square ends, so that they may hurry the entry into the abscess.
And these are the forms of Experimentis; that is to say, perforating ones; and one of them is called a "beris," that is, "perforator"; and they are of three kinds.
For from them there is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
It is agreed that they should be investigated through them abscesses, and fistulas, and buboes from that which is between them of bones.
And others are made round, smooth, polished, like large needles with which stitches are taken, of yellow brass, or of bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the better ones are those that are made of yellow brass.
And experience is also made of black lead, which are suitable so that fistulas may be tested through them, whose concavity has reflections, so that they are reflected with their smoothness with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. For from them there are long ones, from them medium ones, and from them short ones, according to the quantity of the amplitude of the fistula and its constriction.
The Form of Hooks, and they are of many kinds.
For from them there are simple ones, namely those which have one curve: and they are of three kinds as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And from them there are blind Hooks: and they are of three kinds.
And from them there are hooks having two curvatures, and they are of three kinds: and from them there are hooks having three curvatures, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of Scalpels, with which nodes and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three kinds: for from them there is a large one, a medium one, and a small one.
Their points with which the cutting is done are sharp, other points are not sharp: and they are not put so, unless so that relief may be given in scraping nodes when there is fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and that the sick person may be healed by them and find tranquility for a little time from the burning he finds in scraping an abscess.
They are made of bronze, and they are similar to a ladle, with which alcohol is made; and at the wide end there is a concealed spatulate point; and through it one can direct it inwards, and outwards as you wish, just as you see.
The spatulas which are concealed between the fingers at the opening of abscesses; and the sick do not perceive them; and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Forms of wind instruments with which the flow of blood is cut off; and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Let them be made of bronze, or brass, round, and somewhat long, as you see; and they should be delicate.
And it is necessary that you have these kinds of wind instruments, both large and small, so that you may cut blood quickly when needed, and then when medicine is not present for you.
However, do not use them in cutting blood in every place of the body, and do not use them except in fleshy places: such as the calves of the legs, and the hip, and the calves of the arms, and the breasts, and the belly, and the groin, and similar ones, from the fleshy calves, humid ones.
And sometimes they are made into other small instruments similar to pistachio shells, according to their shape; with which blood is cut off when it flows from the place of phlebotomy, or at the incision of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this shape.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the care of men's breasts which resemble women's breasts.
Sometimes the nipples of some men swell up during sleep (that is, when they begin to become aroused in their sleep) until they resemble women's breasts; and they remain foul abscesses.
Therefore, whoever shrinks from that, must cut above the nipples with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this form from line b to line g. Then flay off all the fat. Then fill the wound with flesh-colored medicine. Then bring together the two lips of the wound with suture, and care for it until it heals.
But if the nipple inclines downward and softens because of its size, as happens to women: then it is necessary that it be cut in its two upper sides with two similar incisions of a crescent shape, each of which is joined with the other at the ends of each, until there is a larger line containing the smaller one, according to this form from line b to line g. Then flay off the skin that is between the two lips and remove the fat, and administer what we said about suture and powders, which is necessary for healing, until it heals.
But if you do not complete the incision as needed because of the patient's restlessness or because of bleeding: then it is necessary that you fill the wound with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then care for it until it heals.
Concerning the piercing of an abscess which occurs under the nipple.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses that occur under the nipple are of hard types of Strophulus which contain cysts and those which contain moisture.
Those therefore that are of the types which contain moisture, must be cut with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this form, from line b to line g. When therefore all that is in the abscess is evacuated as pus, then fill it with old cotton and leave it until another day.
Then remove the cotton and treat it with a suitable ointment for that purpose, until it heals.
If the abscess is of the kind of nodes called Strophularia, then split it open above itself, just as we said in the section on Strophularia equally.
But if the wound extends so that it emits pus, then apply a cautery to it according as we said in what has been stated before.
Chapter XLIX.
On the incision of an abscess which occurs because of an artery and vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the skin above it consolidates, then very often an abscess arises from that; and similarly it happens with a vein, so that an abscess and swelling occur in it.
And the signs by which it is recognized that the abscess and swelling are due to an artery and a vein are these: because when the abscess is due to an artery, it is long, aggregated deep in the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there were a creaking sound.
And that which is due to a vein is round and manifest on the body.
And incision of this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, groins, neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very large. Therefore it is necessary that its treatment be directed with iron and what arises from them also in the extremities, or in the head, must be directed.
Therefore on that which arises from the inflation of the orifice of the artery, make an incision in the skin in length. Then open the incision with hooks, then the artery; and free it from the tunics until it is exposed.
Then insert a needle under it and make it penetrate up to the other side and tie off the artery with a double thread in two places according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then puncture the place which is between the two ligatures with a scalpel until all the blood within it exits and the abscess is resolved.
Then apply a treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall away; afterwards treat it with suitable ointments until it is healed.
But if it be an abscess due to the cutting of a vein, then you must hold with your hand as much as possible of the abscess with the skin. Then introduce a needle under the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it until you draw it out from the other side. Then tie with the abscess a good ligature; according as I told you about the ligature of a vein, from two parts with two ends of thread.
But if you fear that the thread may be drawn out, then introduce another needle with another thread under the whole abscess at the incision of the needle; press and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle until what is in it comes out; then cut away excess skin and let that which is tied be released from it.
Then place upon it a poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then apply treatment, which is made with lint and ointments until it heals.
Chapter 1.
Concerning an abscess that occurs from the twisting of Nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in an artery and vein, similarly an abscess occurs in nerves; which happens in them by a blow, or from excessive labor, and similar things; and it most often happens in those places where the auxiliaries are, or the heel, and in any place where joints are moved; and the abscess is serious.
It resembles the color of the rest of the body and is in many dispositions without pain; and when it is squeezed with vehemence, one feels a weakness in it similar to a stupor.
And it is not an abscess gathered deep within the body, but rather under the skin, and it moves to every part; and it does not go forward to the front, nor back to the rear.
Therefore, what is among them at the joints should not be entered with iron. For perhaps contraction may occur.
But for that which is among them on the head or forehead, cut the skin with a scalpel. If therefore it is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps and cut it off at the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook and flay it; then remove it, and bring the wound together with suture; and care for it until it heals.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the abdomen.
There sometimes occur in the abdomens of certain men, and in other parts of their bodies, swellings which are called fungal, by similarity to fungi.
Their root is slender, and their head is thick, and their lips are inverted. And some of them are small; and some of them grow very large.
And I myself have indeed already seen a man, and in his abdomen were two swellings which were more like fungi than anything else: there was no difference between them, white, of small root, and their lips were inverted and split, and moisture always flowed from them.
Therefore I cut into them and found that one weighed about ten and eight ounces and the other about six ounces.
For very often much blood is expelled at its incision; therefore hasten, if blood overpowers you, then cauterize it.
But if you see a weak and timid one shrinking from the incision with a knife, then take a thread made of lead cleanly, and bind it with that thread on the finger: the method of which is what we have said, and let it go for two days.
Then add to the lead constriction. Therefore do not cease to bind the lead whenever it presses on the fingertip until it is cut off and falls by itself without effort.
But if the fingertip is of a thick root, then there is some fear and trembling in its incision; especially that which is in the abdomen.
However it is possible to cut half of it. Then cauterize it so that it does not grow back.
Then care for it until the wound heals.
And be careful not to presume to cut a fingertip that is dark colored, numb, and ugly looking; for it is indeed a cancerous ulcer; and I will come with a remembrance of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of umbilical hernias.
An umbilical hernia occurs from many causes. Or it arises from fissures in the abdominal muscles, which are above the abdomen; and intestines or omentum comes out of them, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or from blood flowing from a vein or an artery, according to what was previously mentioned. Or from wind confined within it.
But if it is due to a fissure in the muscles, and there is protrusion of fat; then its color is similar to the color of the body, and it is soft, without pain; and it appears in various positions. And if it is due to the protrusion of intestine, then its location, as we have described it, is of a more intense variety. Therefore, when you press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it swells greatly at the entrance of the bath, and there is vehement labor.
But if it is due to moisture, it is soft, then it does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor is there constriction, nor is it added.
But if it is due to blood, then with these signs an abscess appears tending toward blackness from the cleavage.
But if it is due to flesh produced: then the abscess is grave, thick, hard, and is fixed according to a single quantity.
But if it is due to windiness, then its touch is soft.
And the operation on it is, that you then consider if the prominence of the navel is due to blood from an Artery, or Vein, or windiness: then it is necessary that you prohibit its cure. For there is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter, in which I said abscesses are due to artery and vein.
But if the prominence of the navel is due to intestine, or a rupture: then it is necessary that you order the sick person to hold his breath and stand straight extended. Then mark with encausto all around the circumference of the navel. Afterwards order him to lie down between your hands on his back. Then encircle with a wide speculum around the navel on top of the place which you marked with encausto. Afterwards extend the middle of the abscess upwards, with a large hook. Then tie it around firmly with thread, or with a cord from silk ligature: and let the knot of the ligature be away from the incision.
Then open the extended middle of the abscess above the ligature. If therefore you find it already seized by a ligament: then draw out the ligature, and insert into it the index finger, and press the intestine towards the interior of the abdomen; and if you find a tumor, then take it up with a hook, and cut off its excess.
But if it is an artery or vein contrary to you: then bind it very much. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circumference which you made around the abscess in the manner of a cross, which you make penetrate.
Then draw tight the abscess in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the extended flesh rots; and it falls off by itself: or cut it when it softens and decays.
Then care for it with what is necessary from ointments until it heals.
But if the prominence of the navel is from flesh growing in it, or from moisture; then it is necessary to make concave abscesses as we said. And draw out the blood or moisture which you find in it.
Then care for it with what strengthens the place.
Chapter Fifty-Three.
Concerning the Care of CANCER.
We have already said in the division of species of Cancer, and how the way is to its cure with medicines; and caution should be taken from it with its cure with iron, so that it is not ulcerated; and we have said Cancer generated in the womb, and caution from its cure.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where it is possible for its eradication; such as Cancer which is in the breasts or in the thigh, and those similar to them, from the limbs located for extracting all of it; especially when it is beginning and small.
But when it grows old and is large, it is not necessary to approach it.
For I myself was unable to heal anyone from it, nor did I see anyone before me who had attained that.
And the operation in this case, when it is situated as we have said, is to precede and loosen the weak one from the black bile several times. Then phlebotomy itself if you find him with manifest repletion.
Then prepare the weak one with preparation, in which he should be placed during the operation.
Then throw into the cancer hooked instruments which are suitable for it. Then scoop it out from every part with skin, according to examination, until nothing remains of its roots: and let the blood flow and do not cut it quickly, rather press the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand or with whatever instrument is possible there.
But if the thick flow of blood from the incision of an artery or vein opposes you: then cauterize the vein until the blood is cut off. Then care for it with the remaining care until it is healed.
Concerning the Care of Dropsy.
Chapter Fifty-Four. We have already narrated in the division the types of dropsy and how the accumulation of water occurs; and the signs of every type, and its cure with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is no other than the ascites type alone; and it does not approach the tympanitic type with iron nor the hydropic type at all, for that is fatal.
When you have already treated this type of dropsy, tympanitic, with medicines and your medication has not been successful, then consider. For if weakness has already overtaken the weak one or there is another ailment with him besides dropsy; such as if there is a cough with it, or loosening of the bowels, and things similar to those: then beware of treating him with iron; for there is fear of that.
If however you see a weak one of excessive humor, and there is no illness with him except dropsy alone: and he is neither a boy nor an old man: then the method of operation on him is that you raise the weak one standing between your hands, and the attendant behind him presses his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a thorny spathe, according to this form. Let it be long, with two pointed ends, sharp at the extremity like a spathe. However, let there be some slight shortness in it so that you do not pierce through to the intestines during the operation and injure them.
Then consider. For if the dropsy is generated from the intestines, then it is necessary that you lengthen the incision from the navel by the length of three fingers, downwards in its direction above the chest.
But if the dropsy is generated because of a liver ailment, then let your incision be on the left from the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is because of the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side; and it is also not necessary for the incision to be on the side where the weak one wishes to lie, so that superfluities do not flow to that weak place.
Then pierce the skin through with an instrument. Then insert into that incision, and raise your hand with the spathe between the skin and the flesh, and flay it as if you were skinning it; and let the amount flayed be the size of a nail, or thereabouts.
Then pierce the flesh until the spathe reaches the empty space: and that is the place of water. Then extract it and insert into the opening an instrument of this form. It is like a cannula; it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished; with a small hole at its lower end and three holes on its sides, two on one side and one on the other; and its extremity is cut in the manner of an incision on a reed, as you see: there is a ring at its upper end.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it descends immediately upon the instrument; therefore, it evacuates from the water in an hour a medium quantity.
For if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, perhaps the sick person dies in an hour through the resolution of his own animal spirit; or he suffers a swoon which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of its strength, and according to what the sick person's dispositions signify to you from the strength of his pulse, and from his goodness.
Then extract the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is because it is retained immediately on account of the skin which retains the hole which is above the sifac; about which I have spoken, so that you pierce it according to that method.
Then repeat the instrument on the next day, if you see the sick person tolerate it; and evacuate also from the water a small quantity.
Do that for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error: until there does not remain from the water except a little.
But if you fear for the sick person, and you release much from the water; then care for him with burial in warm sand, and much sweat in a bath and sun, and expect thirst upon him; and care for him with drying medicines until he is healed: and cauterize upon the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the extraction of the water; according to what I have spoken in what precedes.
Concerning the care of infants who are born with their urine passages not perforated.
Chapter 51.
When anyone of infants emerges from the belly of its mother, and the head of its penis is not perforated, or the hole is tight, or it is in an improper place.
Therefore it is necessary to assist with its perforation immediately when it is born with a fine Spatula, according to this form.
Then put a fine plumb bob into the hole and tie it; and hold it for three or four days.
When he therefore wishes to pass urine, remove it and let him urinate. Then replace it.
And if you do not put in a plumb bob, it is possible that the urine flowing over the place will not allow it to be closed.
He whose opening is tight should be treated with a plumb bob, as we said, for many days until it is enlarged.
Those in whom the opening is not in its proper place are injured. This is because in those who are born, the opening is at the end of the head of the penis; therefore he cannot urinate forward until he raises his penis with his own hand upward. Nor is there generation for him because the semen cannot reach the womb according to straightness and it is a very foul disease.
And the method of operation on him is that the sick man should be laid on his back. Then extend the head of his penis with your left hand by a strong extension and cut the head of the penis from the place of the glans, with a sharp knife or lancet; as the incision of a reed, or as you shave something so that its middle comes to resemble the head of the penis; and so that the opening falls in the middle as it should be.
And beware during your operation of the flow of blood. For this often happens. Therefore oppose to him something that stops the blood and care for the wound until it is healed.
Concerning the diseases which occur in the foreskin and head of the penis and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter Fifty-Six.
These diseases often occur on the penis and there is a protuberance of foul flesh.
And from it one is malignant and another is not malignant.
Therefore the non-malignant one ought to be suspended with a fine hook, and cut off until it is completely cleansed. Then place upon it cotton submerged in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterwards with dyafinicon ointment until it is healed.
But if the Albather is malignant and foul-colored, then it is necessary to administer a cautery upon it after its incision and excision.
But if the Albather is on the foreskin of an uncircumcised captive, and a part of the Albather is between the foreskin and a part is outside: then it is necessary to remove the Albather which is inside first until when it consolidates, then treat that which is outside; because when you treat them together, the foreskin will not be secured from being perforated.
And also there occur in the testicles a blackness and corruption of the foreskin. Therefore it is necessary that you scoop out all that has already been blackened and what desires to be corrupted or is already corrupted.
Then smear upon it afterwards with honey which has already been mixed with flour of pomegranate seeds and crushed sieve herbs.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
But if bleeding should occur, then administer a cautery with a lunar cautery according to this form.
For the cautery is helpful for two conditions at once, namely for bleeding and for a wound when it becomes corrupted.
But if the head of the penis is eaten away and destroyed altogether within an hour: then it is necessary to insert into the urethra of the penis a cannula made from lead so that the weak one may urinate upon it.
But in the adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis; and this adhesion indeed does not occur except in him whose foreskin is healthy and circumcision is not required upon it: and sometimes adhesion occurs because of a wound or an abscess; therefore it is necessary to scrape it away with a blunt Spatula until the ligament is loosened and the head of the penis is freed from every part.
If it is difficult to distinguish these precisely, then you must scrape something from the head of a twig, and from the foreskin. This is because when the foreskin is delicate, it may perhaps be perforated quickly on account of its delicacy.
Then separate the foreskin from the head of the penis with a fine linen cloth, which has already been moistened in cold water so that they do not stick together again.
Then treat it with astringent wine until it is consolidated.
Chapter LVII.
On the cleansing of infants and their treatment when injured by accident.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity like other wounds: nevertheless, since it is from our operation, by our will, and its use is especially in boys, it is necessary that we mark in it a better operation and an easier way leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books: because they do not use it in their laws and it is nothing but what we have acquired through experience.
This is because I have found the greater part of craftsmen and phlebotomists to use cleansing by circumcising with a razor and forceps: and they use alum and ligature with thread, and incision with a nail.
And I myself have already tried all these methods and found none better than cleansing with forceps and ligature with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Because the foreskin skin has two coats; therefore perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower coat remains: it is then necessary to incise again to cut the other, and pain will ensue. And with cleansing which is done by alum, that is, a digital method which tailors use, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secure: because it may perhaps enter its opening.
In cleansing, however, if the nail is involved, perhaps iron will come out and spoil your operation; and indeed the skin of a child is short by nature and very often they are born like this so that they do not need cleansing; and I have already seen this.
But cleansing with forceps and tying with thread has proven a good experiment for me; because forceps are proportionate to the incision: therefore, because the sharpness which is above is like the sharpness which is below. Therefore, when you apply your hand beforehand, the two sharpnesses are proportioned and they fall according to the single operation at one time; and a compression is made in a similar shape, containing the skin of a stick from every side, with which there is no error at all.
And the method of the operation itself is such that you should make a child, especially if he is one who understands a little, understand that you are not doing anything except tying thread on his stick and let him go until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be merry in every way possible for him so that he receives his intelligence from it. Then make him stand between your hands in a prepared posture and let him not be sitting; and hide the forceps in your sleeve or under your foot so that the boy does not see them at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then insert your hand towards his stick and blow on the skin and draw it upwards until the head of the stick comes out. Then cleanse it from what gathers in it of filth.
Then tie the marked spot with a double thread. Afterwards, tie a little further down a second ligature. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower ligature with good retention and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly lift the skin upwards and extract the head of the stick. Then let the blood run gradually (for it is better) and put powder on top: and on the powder a cloth in which an egg yolk boiled in rose water has been spread, shaken with good fresh rose oil; and leave it over it until another day: and take care of it with the rest of the treatment, until it heals.
The form of the forceps for cleansing is this.
Let the two ends be blunt, and let them converge: so that a clavicle branch does not come out of them: and let the length of the two points be equal to the length of two handles equally.
But an error falling in cleansing is this: because perhaps the whole inner skin escapes, or a part of it at the incision.
Therefore it is necessary that you extend it from your hour with your nail before applying the poultice to the place: and cut it according to equality.
But if you cannot hold it with your nail, then pull it out with a hook, and cut it.
But if three days have passed it, and what is under the rod remains swollen, poultice it: then let it be until the poultice becomes hot, and the skin loosens it easily, and cut it according to what is prepared for you: and beware of the head of the rod. For it will not harm it.
Therefore care for it with what strengthens the wound from powders, which we have described in the treatise on powders: and if it is cut from the skin above measure, and extends to the upper parts, it will not harm it either.
Therefore care for it with what we have said, until it heals.
Chapter LVIII.
Concerning the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction made from stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or flesh born, and similar things from those which we have said from the modes of treatment in division.
And if urine is not passed, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which is already in the neck of the bladder; then it is necessary that he be weak, bent over on his knees. Then let his feet be placed upon his back, and let him extend his belly from the upper part of his back, and let the weak one draw in his breath as much as possible for him. Therefore, when the neck of the bladder is directed towards the expulsion of the stone to the rear: urine is discharged.
And if it is not passed with what we have said, and the matter is vehement upon the weak one; then it is necessary that you administer extraction of it with an instrument which is called a syringe, which has this form: Let it be of silver, and let it be subtle, smooth, concave like the quill of a bird's feather in the subtlety of a ray, long in the likeness of a palm and a half; to which there should be a subtle cannula at its head.
And the method of the exit of urine through it is, that you take a doubled thread and bind wool or tow at its extremity with a good ligature; and insert the extremity of the thread into the lower part of the syringe; and cut off any excess from the wool with forceps, but insert it into the cannula with violence, like an alzer.
Then smear the syringe with oil, or butter, and egg white; and have the weak one sit upon a seat, and embrocate the bladder and its rod with moist oils, olive oil, and warm water.
Then insert the syringe into the rod easily until it reaches the root of the rod.
Then double it above from the part of the umbilicus.
Then press the syringe within it until it reaches the bladder, and let the weak one feel that it has already reached the empty thing; and this is not done according to this order unless because the passage in which it goes is where there is a twisting.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For the vein will follow the wool.
Then draw it out, and make the vein run along the path of the urine; and repeat the syringe, and do not cease to do that until the bladder is emptied, and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of the washing out of the bladder with a Clepshydra.
Chapter LIX.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder, or blood congeals in it, or pus is constricted in it, and you wish to distill waters and medicines into it; then let it be done with an instrument which is called a Clepshydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the concave place, in which is that which propels it, be according to the quantity which it will expel itself without addition, until when it is drawn with it something, from moisture it is attracted: and when it is propelled, with it it is expelled afar: according to what the clepshydra does, by which naptha is projected, that is, Greek fire in the battles of Mars.
When therefore you wish to project moisture into the bladder, introduce the extremity of the clepshydra into the moisture, and draw with it that which propels it upwards, for the moisture is attracted into the concavity of the clepshydra.
Then introduce its extremity into the urethra, according to what we have related in the syringe. Then propel the moisture with that which propels it; for the moisture reaches the bladder immediately, so that the sick person feels it.
The form of a delicate clyster by which the bladder is washed out.
Let it be made of silver, or electrum; whose head be higher like a small spoon, and under it a sinus into which a ligature may fall.
Then take a bladder of a ram and put into it the moisture with which you wish to irrigate the bladder. Then tie it between two folds with a strong ligature using double thread, and warm that moisture a little over the fire.
Then introduce the extremity of the clyster into a rod, then tighten your hand over the bladder with moisture with decent compression until the weak person feels that the moisture has already reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder at hand, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle out of it, and pierce it through all its roundness. Then insert strong thread into those holes, as they revolve. Then gather the circle as it is gathered as a bandage; after you put in it what you wish from moistures and oils and waters.
Then tie it on the instrument; and do with it as you did with the bladder from expression with your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
On the Extraction of a Stone.
We have already said in the division the species of stone and its treatment with medicine, and I said the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and the signs of it all; and I said the stone in which the operation falls with the hand and section; and there is a stone generated properly in the bladder; and that which is lodged in the urethra; and I am relating that with manifest exposition and abbreviation.
Therefore, I say that a stone generated in the bladder most often happens to boys.
And among the signs of it is that urine goes out from the bladder like water in its subtlety; and there appears in it white sand, and the weak man rubs his urethra, and labors with it, and very often hangs. Then he becomes erect, and goes out with it the anus in many of them.
And the healing of boys is easy, until they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in old men. But in young men it is intermediate, in that which is between those things; and of him whose stone is larger, healing is easier; and if small, the care is the opposite.
Therefore when we come to the healing, it is necessary first of all that we cleanse the sick person with an enema, which extracts all the dung that is in his intestines; for it sometimes hinders the discovery of the stone in the examination.
Then let the sick person be taken with his feet, and shaken, and moved downward so that the stone descends to the depth of the bladder, or jumps from a high place several times.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and let his hands be under his own hips so that the whole bladder becomes sloping downwards.
Then inquire of him, and touch him externally.
Therefore if you feel the stone in a space, then hurry immediately with an incision above it.
But if it does not fall at all under your touch, then you must smear the index finger with oil from the left hand, if the sick person is a boy; or the middle finger if he is a grown young man; and introduce it into his anus, and inquire about the stone until it stands under your finger, and turn him gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and drive it outwards towards the place where you wish for the incision; and instruct a minister to press the bladder with his hand, and instruct another minister to extend the testicles with his right hand upward; and with his other hand, to remove the skin which is under the testicles in the part where the section is.
Then introduce an incizing speculum whose form is this. And find in it what is between the anus and testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left buttock; and let the incision be above it on a stone, and your finger be in the anus; and let the incision be transverse, so that the external incision is wide and the internal one is tight, according to the quantity so that the exit of the stone from it is not greater.
Perhaps indeed the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone at the incision, and it exits without difficulty.
And know that from a stone which has angles and edges, therefore its exit is difficult because of that.
And from it is smooth, like a gland, and round: and its exit is easy.
Therefore to him who has angles and edges, add to the fissure.
But if it does not exit this way, then it is necessary that you ingeniously place something above it: or tie a string above it with a decent gesture, whose extremity is like the moon, which will bind above the stone and will not slip from it; or insert a subtle instrument of curved extremity under it.
But if you are unable to do so above it, then enlarge the opening a little. But if something from the blood overcomes you, cut it off with a lancet.
But if there is more than one stone, then first drive the largest one to the opening of the bladder. Then cut above it. Afterwards drive the small one after it, and similarly do if there are more than two.
But if it is very large, it is ignorance to make a section above it large. For it happens to the weak one of two things, either that he dies, or that there occurs to him a constant distillation of urine: because the place does not consolidate at all.
Rather administer its expulsion until it exits: or ingeniously break it with forceps until you extract it in pieces.
When therefore you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense and aloes and dragon's blood; and bind it up, and lay over it a cloth steeped in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that a hot poultice may be formed.
Then place the patient on his back upon his neck, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When therefore you unloose it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then care for it with dyafinicon ointment and basilicon ointment until it is healed.
But if it should happen that a hot abscess is added to the wound, and corrosion, and things similar to these; such as if the blood should congeal in the bladder and urine be prevented from it; and this is known from the issuing of blood with the urine. Then insert your finger into the wound and extract that blood. For if it remains, it leads to the corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt; and apply to it everything suitable for its cure until it is healed.
And it is necessary in the course of the whole treatment that you should bind both thighs and shins, because of the medicines which are placed upon the spot.
But if the stone is small and is in the urethra and is fixed there and prevents the urine from passing out; then treat it with what I have related before you come to section. Very often indeed you are excused from section by this treatment, for I have already proved it.
And it is that you should take a perforator of alferic iron, which has this form: triangular point, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread and tie the penis with it under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the penetrating iron into the urethra with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself; and revolve the perforator with your hand in the stone gradually; and try to make perforation of it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For then urine is immediately discharged.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outer part of the urethra. For it itself is broken, and goes out with the urine, and the sick person is healed.
But if this treatment is not prepared for you because of something which prevents it: then tie a thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone in the urethra itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards untie the ligature, and cleanse the congealed blood, which is made in the wound.
And it is not necessary to tie the thread below the stone unless so that it does not return to the bladder; and the other thread above unless so that when the thread is untied after the exit of the stone, the skin returns to its place and the wound heals; and for this reason it is necessary for you when you tie the upper thread, that you lift the skin upwards so that it returns to your complement and heals the wound as we said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the Extraction of Stones from Women.
Few stones are generated in women.
But if it should happen to any of them that a stone occurs, then the cure is difficult and is forbidden because of many ways.
One of these is that the woman perhaps is a virgin.
And the second is because you do not find a woman who will reveal herself to the doctor when she is chaste or from those having husbands.
The third is because you do not find a woman well-knowing this art, especially the operation with the hand.
The fourth is the distance from the stone's location: therefore it requires deep incision, and there is danger in that.
If necessity provokes that, then it is fitting that you take a female physician, well-knowing: and few are found.
But if you deprive her of those things, then seek a chaste physician, subtle, and present a female midwife well-taught in the matter of women, or a woman who inclines toward this art: therefore make her present, and especially instruct her to do all that you command her from inquiry about the stone.
First of all, it is necessary that she look to see if the woman is a virgin: then it is fitting that she insert a finger into her anus and seek the stone.
If therefore she finds it, let her constrict it under her finger, and then command the midwife to insert her finger into the vulva of the infirm woman and inquire about the stone, after she places her left hand upon the bladder and compresses it with good pressure.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that gradually she move it from the orifice of the bladder to the lower parts with all her strength until it arrives with it at the root of the thigh.
Then let her cut above it at the opposition of the middle of the vulva at the root of the thigh, from whichever part she prepares it and feels the stone in that part: and let her finger not be removed from the stone, constricting under it: and let the incision be small at first. Then let her insert a ray over that incision.
Then let her insert a ray over that incision. If therefore she feels the stone, then let her add to the incision according to the proportion of quantity as the stone goes forth from it.
And know that there are many kinds of stones. From these indeed there is a small one, and a large one, a smooth one and a rough one, a long one, and a round one having branches.
Therefore know its kind, so that it may be shown to you by this concerning that which you wish.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add in the place triticum flour, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation, until the stone comes out; and do that you prepare for yourself from the instruments which I said in the extraction of a stone in men, so that you may help through them in your operation.
But if the flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery which has been cut: then put powder on the place, and bind it with powders a decent binding; and do not change that; and leave the stone, and do not extract it. For perhaps the weak woman may die. Then care for the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of blood subsides after days, and the place festers, then return to your operation, until the stone comes out.
Chapter 62.
Concerning the incision for a watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not anything but a gathering of moisture in the tunica albuginea, which is under the skin containing the testicle; and it is called the tunica albuginea.
And sometimes it is in the panniculus itself which nature has prepared for it in that part, until it swells: which is another egg; and it is the skin of the testicle between the tunica albuginea, which we said; and this does not happen except rarely.
And the generation indeed of this hernia is from a weakening which occurs in the testicles; therefore this matter flows to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow upon the testicles.
And this moisture has many colors. For either its color is inclined to a citron yellow, or it is bloody red, or fecal black, or it is watery white, and that is what is more common.
And the signs by which it is known where there is a collection of water are these. For if it is in the white part that we mentioned, then it is a round abscess somewhat in length like the shape of an egg; and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it from every side.
And if the moisture is in its own pouch, then the abscess is round with a part like an egg, and because of this a man thinks it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white part, then it falls below sensation.
Therefore when you wish to know the color of the moisture, then experience the color of the moisture in the abscess with a square instrument, whose shape has preceded. According to that therefore which comes out in the track of the Instrument, judge concerning that which is between it.
When therefore we come to the treatment with iron, it is necessary that you direct the sick person to be bled if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then he is laid on his back upon something high a little, and put under him many cloths.
Then sit you upon his left, and direct the assistant to sit at his right, who shall extend his rod to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles and to the part near the navel.
Then take a broad speculum and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle through the length, as far as near the pubic bone; and let the incision be according to the straightness of an equidistant line which divides the skin of the testicles until it reaches the white part comprehending; and flay this and observe, or be careful from this so that you do not cut this: and let your flaying be such that by which you flay this, on the side which hangs with the egg more: and carefully scrutinize with the flaying according to the quantity which is possible for you.
Then pierce the full bladder with a wide perforation and extract all the water.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with hooks and extend the bladder upwards, and do not touch the skin enveloping the testicles; and incise the bladder in any way possible for you to incise it, either with its entirety or in parts, especially its thin side. For if you do not meticulously examine it, there is no safety in its incision lest the water return.
But if the external testicle proceeds from its skin during your operation: then when you have completed the incision of the bladder, replace it. Then bring together the two lips of the skin of the testicles with suture.
Then care for it like other wounds until it heals.
But if you find the testicle corrupted from blackness or another cause, then it is necessary to tie off the vessels which are the spermatic cord, fearing a flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with its spermatic cord and extract the testicle; afterward care for it as we have said.
But if the water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches; therefore cut off the other part as you did to the other equally.
And if it seems right to you for there to be one operation, then do so; then wash away the blood. And insert into the incisions wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil; and put outside other wool than that which you submerged in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and the belly; and put double cloths on top of these, and they are poultices; and tie them on top with a bandage having six folds according to this form.
Then take two cloths and soak them and fill them with carminated bran according to this similarity; and let their ends be those by which they are pressed from the elbow, or damp wool; and gather them together with a binding at the lumbar region of the arms; and let one end receive upon the anus up to the back, and let the second receive upon the region of the chest; and let two ends be upon the two thighs below, and let the other two ends be upon the two groins, and let the whole binding be gathered in the lumbar region of the arms.
And sometimes this wound is artificially treated with a cautery in the place of incision with an iron; and it is such that you should take a sharp knife-cautery and cut the skin of the testicles with it; and it is ignited according to what we have said: until when white pus containing water is revealed: then take a cautery, in this shape.
Greek letters are assimilated.
Then pierce that pus with it, and ignite it until all the moisture comes out.
Then extend the pus with hooks, and flay it with a thin ignited cautery, and cut it according to what is possible for you, until you root out all of it.
And be careful that the fire does not touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and farther from a flow of blood, and better than the operation with incision.
But if it causes you opposition in the hour of incision, or in the hour of cautery a hot abscess, or a flow of blood, or anything else: then it is necessary that you cure what opposition it causes you from it with the care that suits it, according to what has been said before.
But if he is weak and fearful, and does not await this operation because of its horror: then administer a perforation according to what I narrate that: and it is.
When it is confirmed to you that by that which we have said of the significations, that the wound is watery: then it is necessary for you to make the weak one sit on a raised seat.
Then pierce the abscess in the lower part of it with a broad spatula; and place the piercing according to the length of the body, and let the opening be wide until all the water runs out.
Then place a compress on the place and bind it, and let it be so that the remaining water runs out; then care for it until the wound is healed.
But if the opposition is to the water's escape in the hour that you pierce it, and this is not except because the white panniculus makes opposition at the wound's orifice, and prohibits the exit of the water, then it is necessary that you introduce a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which water from hydrops patients is extracted, whose shape precedes: or add a little to the operation of the wound.
