And know that water returns and gathers after six months, or thereabouts. Therefore when it gathers, then it is perforated in the same way, and the weak man will pass his days.
Chapter Sixty-Third.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy branches.
Know that in the section over this branch, there is fear leading to destruction in many constitutions. Therefore it seems to me that its dismissal is saving from it.
And I remember the operation on it and the kind of abscess.
I say, therefore, that many abscesses occur in bodies, from which testicles are composed, and this happens for many reasons, namely.
Either from acute or sharp superabundance, which is poured onto the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body and pain is not found in it: and the abscess is grave.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark brown, and there is no sensation in it.
And sometimes it occurs from the knotting of arteries and their inflation, as already previously remembered; or from the inflation of veins.
That which is from them by inflation of arteries, is recognized by the arrangement of the abscess when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary to touch it completely.
That which is done by the inflation of veins, nothing is dispersed from it upon compression with fingers.
And now it is possible that a section be made on this kind and the remaining flesh, and it is to cut the skin of the testicles. Then extend the testicle upward and extract it with white silk; and free the spermatic cord from the vessels and tie off the vessels; and cut the spermatic cord after you free it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
If therefore the testicle is consolidated with those flesh born with it then it is necessary that you draw out the testicle and cut it.
And if there is adhesion between something of the spermatic cord or in that which is between the vessels then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and cut it with a round incision.
But if there is a natural growth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind then it is necessary that you cut all of it and extract the testicle as we said.
When therefore your operation is complete then fill the wound with lint steeped in rose oil and wine; then care for it with the remaining care until it heals.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the Care of Branch Wounds which are with Vine.
Hydrocele is a swelling enveloped by a certain involution, like a hernia, with softening of the testicles; and it makes difficult movement and exercise, and walking upon the weak person.
And in the treatment of this disease there is fear because of that of which we have previously made mention.
But it is necessary that we say that the operation in it should be according to what the first did.
I therefore say that it is necessary that you make the weak person sit upon an elevated seat. Then press the spermatic cord of the testicles downward. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, together with the veins that are near the penis; and let another assistant hold them, and extend them with a vigorous extension.
Then make an incision with a broad, sharp scalpel in a transverse section in the direction of the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then flay the skin from every part, as we told you in the extraction of arteries that are in the temples.
Then fix a needle into them, in which there is a doubled thread; and tie it in the first of the places where the veins occur; and tie it again in the last of them. Then cut them in a straight section in the middle according to the length of the body, and extract what is collected from foul, corrupted humors.
Then care for the wound as you would care for other wounds, in which you wish to generate pus, so that the vessels that nourish one of the testicles may fall away. For it is not bad through that.
But if it should happen to the vein with all the veins, then it is necessary that the testicles be extracted along with the veins so that the testicle is not deprived of nourishment because of the incision, and waste away, or become scorched, and there be no benefit with them.
Chapter Sixty-Fifth.
Concerning the care of the branches of the intestines.
The occurrence of this branches is from a section that happens in the extended abdomen above the belly in the groin part of the belly.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one of the testicles. And this rupture is either from the abdomen or extension thereof.
And these two kinds occur from many causes; either from a blow or a jump or a shout or lifting a heavy thing and the like.
And the sign when it is from an extension of the abdomen. It happens gradually over a long time and does not happen suddenly: and it is an abscess equal towards the depth: because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And its sign when it is from the section of the sifach, because it causes to happen from its beginning intense pain suddenly: and it is a different abscess, appearing under the skin nearby: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it issues forth with intestine pus: and this is called intestinal Ramex and zirbia: and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes feces runs into the intestine and is retained there: and with it there is danger to the sick person: because it causes severe pain to happen, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of these kinds of sicknesses with iron there is fear: therefore it is necessary that one be cautious in this matter.
And the method of operation is, that you order the sick person to reduce the intestine with his own hand into the interior of its cavity, if it obeys return. Then he is laid down on his back between your hands; and his legs are raised.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin upwards, and cut all the skin of the testicles along its length. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to how you need to open the incision with them: and the incision should be according to the quantity which is possible so that an egg can come out from it.
Then scrape the siphash, and what are under the skin of the testicles, until when the white siphash is uncovered, hard on every side: then introduce your index finger into it which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphash, which is under the skin, and the second siphash: and loosen with it the adhesion which is in the back part of the testicle.
Then move with your right hand to the inner skin of the testicles and while doing so stretch the white silk upwards with your left hand; and lift the testicle with the silk towards the section part, and tell the assistant to stretch the testicle upwards; and you loosen the adhesion which is behind after the complete solution.
And search with your fingers that there is nothing therein from the intestine unraveled in the hard white silk. But if you find anything from it, then press it down towards the lower abdomen.
Then take a needle in which there is a thick thread made of licorice from ten threads and insert it at the last silk that is, the tunic which is under the skin of the testicles following the section; then cut off the ends which protrude from the thread until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some with a figure which is in the manner of a cross and tie them with the silk which we mentioned because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligament until none of the vessels which nourish them can send anything to them so that a hot abscess does not occur from there. And place again a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it by less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures dismiss from the silk which is under the skin of the testicles a quantity of the size of a finger and cut the remainder entirely according to its roundness and remove the testicle from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles with a section from which blood and pus flow as we have related in what precedes.
Then administer wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound; and administer the ligature which we have related. And sometimes the white silk which we mentioned is cauterized after its incision fearing the flow of blood. Very often it happens. And release the ligature until it falls off by itself.
But if its falling is delayed, then embrocate it with warm water and place that which we said upon it until it falls.
Then care for the wound with the remaining care of wounds until it is healed.
Chapter Sixty-six.
Concerning a windy branch.
In this branch I have not seen anyone who dared to treat it with iron.
And first indeed they said that it is done as we narrated in the branch which is with a vine. Which is, to tie the vessels after section above them with ease below: then cut in the middle and finally care for it with that which opens it up until the vessels fall off: and care for the wound according to what we said until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-seven.
Concerning a disruption which occurs in the groins.
When a rupture occurs in the groins as we said and the place protrudes and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it descends it is a little and returns in all hours: but if time prolongs with it, a fissure is added into the inguinal region until the intestines descend into the inguinal region.
And this happens from the extension of the inguinal region which is in the groins as we said. Which is because the inguinal region is extended: then it softens and the place becomes inflamed and protrudes.
And its care is with a cautery as its previous narration preceded.
And sometimes it is cared for with iron according to this manner: and it is that the sick person lies on their back between your hands. Then cut the inflamed and protruding place of the rupture by a width according to the quantity of three fingers.
Then bind the ligature and what is under the skin until a white vein, which follows it, appears.
Then take a lancet and place it upon the prominent spot from the vein and press it deep into the abdomen.
Then make two prominent spots above the end of the lancet from the vein and join them together with a suture; then withdraw the end of the lancet.
And do not cut the vein at all, nor strain the egg, nor anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of a ruptured intestine.
Then treat it with wound care. When therefore you cut the threads, cleanse it and restore the wound until it consolidates.
For the vein will contract and no prominence of rupture will be added.
And cautery is better in this place; for it is nearer to safety.
Chapter LXVIII.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Very often the skin of the testicles is softened in certain men, on account of which the flesh between them is softened and its appearance is marred.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is consulted in its treatment to make the sick man lie on his back and cut away all the softened skin upon the natural skin.
Then he will bring together the two lips with a suture; and if you wish, first make three or four sutures on the excess softened skin and secure the sutures.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat it with the care of other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall away.
Chapter LXIX.
Concerning castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law and for this reason it would be permissible for me not to remember it in this book of mine; and there is no need to recall it except for two reasons.
One of them is that it be in the knowledge of medicine when questioned about it, and that he know its cure to which it happens.
And another way is that we very often need the castration of certain animals for our benefits through it, such as rams, goats, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore I say that castration is done according to two methods. Or by abrasion. Or by fissure and incision.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the way of the operation is: that we make the animal sit in hot water until its testicles are softened and made pliable, and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are resolved and do not appear to the touch.
But in castration by fissure and incision, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament and cut above the whole testicle with one incision until when two testicles proceed: then incise them after you have skinned both, and do not leave anything above them except a fine silk thread which is above the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by abrasion: because abrasion may perhaps leave something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires copulation.
Then care for the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
Concerning the care of hermaphrodites in two ways.
A hermaphrodite is from males according to two methods. One of them is because it appears in it that there follows a space, or in the skin of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs: and sometimes urine flows from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women there is one species, and it is the vulva of a woman above the pubic bone just like the testicles of a man small indeed, prominent externally: of which one is like the penis of a man, and two are like two testicles.
And the treatment of the three species, two from men and one species from women, is: because it is necessary that you cut away the added flesh until the impression of them escapes. Then care for them with the treatment of other wounds.
But in the second species of men, from which urine flows out, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On the Incision of a Tent-like Structure and Prominent Flesh in the Vulva of Women.
A tent-like structure perhaps is added on top of the natural part until its appearance becomes ugly and foul: and sometimes it magnifies in certain women so much that it expands like in men, and reaches as far as intercourse.
Therefore it is necessary that you hold the excess of the tent-like structure with your hand, or with a hook, and cut it; and do not stop in the incision, especially in the depth of the root: so that bleeding does not occur.
Then care for it with the treatment of wounds until it heals.
But the flesh that arises is flesh which is born in the opening of the womb until it fills it; and perhaps it protrudes outwardly according to the likeness of a tail; and because of this, some of the ancients name the ailment tailed. Therefore it is necessary that we cut it just as we cut the tent-like structure, and care for it until it heals.
On the Treatment of Atresia, Which Is an Imperforate Vulva.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
Atresia is when the vulva of a woman is not perforated, or when there is a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
It is accidental from a preceding illness and it indeed arises either from added flesh, or a thin or thick ulcer and it occurs either in the depth of the matrix, or on its sides, or on its upper part, or its lower part; and it prevents coitus, impregnation, childbirth and perhaps menstruation.
And recognition of it is made by the midwife's inspection if the suture is an apparent approach.
But if it is not apparent then seek it with your fingers or a probe.
But if the suture is from a thin ulcer and is near both lips then hasten and disrupt it.
And it is necessary to place poultices upon the two lips.
Then place the index fingers of both your hands together and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then extend the two lips with force until the thin ulcer is disrupted and the suture is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and place it upon the spot, and let the woman come every day so that the place does not consolidate with other fluids.
But if the ulcer is large and thick then it is necessary to cut it out with a broad spatula like a myrtle leaf.
But if the suture is from added flesh then suspend it with hooks and cut into it and have with you medications which stop the flow of blood without biting such as acacia, dragon's blood, and litharge mixed with albumen.
Then insert a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not consolidate quickly, and hold it for several days; then administer linen cerate from dry flax, and then let the rest be cared for until it is healed.
And sometimes other flesh growths are born within the matrix; therefore it is necessary that they be cut according to this method: but if there is a cancerous abscess then an abscess which is cancerous within the matrix should not at all be opposed with iron.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil and Bothor rubeae in the vulvas of women.
Hemorrhoids are indeed the open orifices of veins, from which much blood constantly flows.
Therefore when hemorrhoids become ancient they become thelil and as I have already said in the division of their kinds and signs, I will say here which receive cure from them and which do not.
I therefore say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the uterus and do not appear to the sense, then there is no cure for them with iron.
Those however which are from them in the orifice of the uterus on which sense falls are those that are cured.
It is necessary therefore that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the athehelil with forceps or with a rough cloth and cut them out from their roots.
Then powder over them at the flow of blood from one of the cutting powders without modification: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and thus, and those similar to them.
Then press her feet against the wall for one hour. Afterwards place her in cold water.
And if the flow of blood persists then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate rinds, and galls, and similar things.
Then take barley flour and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it on her back.
If therefore the blood is cut off with it, it is good and if not, make the windy ones adhere to the back and breasts without scarification.
Then take wool and submerge it in the juice of tarathit or the juice of arnoglossa or the juice of the branches of alaula which is aglanter, Pontic wine, and make it adhere to the place.
Therefore, when the abscess is quiet, care for the place with ointments until it heals.
But albarth is reddish and similar to the heads of alfalfa, and has a rough appearance.
Therefore it is necessary that you cut out what appears from it, according to what I said in the chapter equally; and care for it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
Concerning the Perforation of a Fistula Which Occurs in the Womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and hot abscess.
And I have indeed already said all these diseases and their kinds, and their signs, and their treatments in the division: it is necessary that I say in this treatise about a hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is from abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is made with an iron instrument.
Therefore it is necessary that you consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is sharp with percussion, and burning, and fever; and you see it reddening; if numbness falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation; and care for it with that which helps with its maturation, until when the sharpness of the accidents subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit upon a seat which beats two seats, and recline, or lean upon her back, and gather her feet to the lower part of her belly; and let her hips be spread, and put her arms under her knees, and bind each one with a suitable ligament to it.
Then let the midwife sit from her right side, and administer the instrument by which the opening of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix it with the Speculum deep in the matrix of the woman: so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the matrix, and injures the delicate parts.
But if the instrument is more than deep, then it is necessary that you place pledgets upon the covering of the deep matrix: so that entry of the whole instrument into the depth of the matrix is prohibited: and it is necessary that you place a knob, which runs in the instrument, from its upper side: and that you hold the instrument, and that the attendant turns the knob until the deep matrix is opened.
Therefore when the Cervix appears and is touched by the hand, and it is soft and pliable: then it is necessary that you cut it at its head with a broad spatula.
Therefore when the blood is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place a linen cloth steeped in rose oil or green oil, in which there is some astringency: and place the linen cloth, and draw out the incision into the depth of the matrix.
And place externally upon the matrix, and upon the comb clean wool steeped in water, in which apples have been boiled.
Then let it be cared for after two days with ointment which suits it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the matrix and wound are washed, so that it is cleansed with water and honey: then with water in which the root of a lily or long birthwort has been boiled: then return to ointment.
But if the wound is hidden interiorly within the matrix, then it is necessary that you withhold from its treatment with iron, but rather treat it with what we said in the division.
Chapter LXXV.
Doctrine of Obstetrics, how living embryos are treated, according to a figure not natural.
It is necessary for a midwife to know first the form of natural childbirth.
Therefore, from these signs it is so that when you see a woman pressing down towards her lower parts, and she desires to smell the air, and relief comes over her about the matter of childbirth upon her, and she hastens the exit of the embryo: then you know that this childbirth is according to the natural form, and its exit is upon its head, and secondarily with it; or suspended, or hanging from the umbilical cord.
Therefore when you see these signs, it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon its head, the second child descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities by a good cleansing.
But that which is besides this childbirth is unnatural, undesirable.
For sometimes the fetus exits upon its feet, and its body exits before its head, and its feet and hands together, or one of its feet; or its head and hands exit; or its belly exits; and perhaps it turns upon its neck, and similar undesirable figures.
Therefore it is necessary for a midwife to be prudent, subtle in all these matters, and to beware of error and deception: and I am showing every figure from them, and how its nature is in it, and let it be signified by that, and let it stand upon it.
When the fetus exits upon its head in its natural exit, and it becomes vehement upon the woman, and childbirth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened; then make her sit upon a seat, or let women hold her; and apply vapors to her vulva with decocted fenugreek, and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and bind it with the second child, and cut it with her fingernail until what is in it runs out from moisture; and press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then she must be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesamum oil.
Then order her after the enema to be compressed; and make her snore while she is being compressed, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will immediately come out.
But if two hands of the fetus emerge first, it is necessary that you reduce them gradually with ease. But if it does not obey upon entering, then place the woman on a seat and elevate her feet upward; then strike the seat upon the ground; and let the woman be held so that she does not fall during the impact.
But if the hands do not emerge and the fetus dies, then cut them open and extract the remaining fetus; and tie his hand with a cloth and pull it out evenly, for it will come out itself.
The delivery of an embryo on its feet. When the fetus comes out on its feet, then it is necessary to push them upward; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards equalize it.
Then let him take hold of one of its feet and extend it gradually.
When they emerge up to the hips, then order her to be compressed and make her snore while she is being compressed; for it will come out if God wills.
And if it does not come out with what we have related, then reduce the fetus gradually until you place it according to its natural form. For it will then come out with an easy delivery.
But if it is hindered with all that we have related, then take fenugreek mucilage and sesamum oil, and dissolved gum; and strike the whole in a mortar with good percussion; then mix it with the vulva of the woman and below her belly. Then have her sit in warm water until the hypocondrium is reached.
When therefore you see that her lower parts are already heated, then give her a suppository of myrrh and insert it. When therefore she has held the suppository for an hour, then have her sit upon a seat. Then have her strain; and bind her mouth and nose; and press the lower part of the belly with a gentle compression. For the fetus will immediately come out.
The exit of the fetus over the knees and hands. When it exits according to this form, then begin gently inserting your hands. Then the woman is turned onto her neck, and her feet hang down to the lower part; and she lies upon the bed, and folds her hands. Then press upon her belly gently. Then extract what is under her feet from the pillows until it is prepared in the lower part. But if the fetus does not exit, then take hold of her feet and move them with a vigorous motion. Then press upon what is above the hypocondrium gently until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then let her insert her hands and equalize the fetus gently; and instruct the woman to compress until the fetus exits.
The exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. Begin by reducing her hand. But if she cannot reduce it, then raise the woman and have her walk.
But if she cannot walk, then have her lie upon the bed and strike her legs with a vigorous concussion.
Then administer the liniment which you prepared from gums.
But if she receives labor pains, then have her sit upon a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and equalize the swelling according to the natural shape; and have the woman cough, and instruct her to compress herself until it comes out.
The exit of the fetus over its neck, with hands spread, and its face toward the back of its mother. It is necessary for the midwife to hold the hand of the fetus, then gradually turn it and align it with ease.
When, therefore, it is aligned, then place on its vulva what we have described made of oil and mucilages.
Then instruct her to move gradually. When you have done this, then have her sit upon a seat and instruct her to lean towards her right side. Then instruct her to compress herself; and have her cough when it is condensed.
The exit of the prepared fetus over its side. When it descends to the orifice of the matrix in this way with the secundina, then cut the secundina with your fingernail until it is evacuated from moisture.
If it therefore hangs over the face of the fetus and its neck, then cut it from the navel so that it is not retained and dies.
Then push the fetus toward the interior until you place it according to what is necessary; and instruct the woman to compress herself several times: for it itself will exit with an easy passage.
The Exit of Twins, or Many Fetuses. Know that twins very often are born; and sometimes three, and four are born. However, that is extraordinary and rarely happens.
Five is a thing against nature and they do not live entirely; and it contains them, however many they may be, with one secundina; and they are separated by the sifach, distinguishing between them the ligature in the navel of each of them.
And sometimes they exit as easily as a fetus exits naturally; and sometimes they exit with an unfortunate outcome, as we have described.
But if some of them are difficult at childbirth and reach that which we said of unnatural figures, then care for them as we have described: and let there be a skilled midwife, and let her do what she does with ease and delay, and devise according to every shape with that which is prepared for her by ingenuity leading to health.
Remembrance of that which is formed in the womb from embryos, and miscarry.
Sometimes one is formed in the womb, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten: and it has already been verified by me that a woman miscarrying emitted seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
Which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise one.
Therefore all this must be in the knowledge of Obstetrics. For perhaps she may come across one like that; therefore oppose to her what is necessary.
Chapter Seventy-Six.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you care for a dead fetus with what I said in the division from medication, and it does not exit, and you are forced to use surgery with your hand, then it is necessary that you consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound health and is not with any accidents from which her loss is feared: then it is necessary that the woman be laid on her back on a bed, and let her head be inclined downwards, and let her legs be raised; and let assistants hold her from two sides, or let her be tied to the bed so that her body is not pulled out at the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with oily substances with a decoction of marshmallow, fenugreek, and flaxseed.
Then the midwife shall anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then she shall introduce it into the womb easily. Then she shall seek with it a place in which to fasten the hooks from the fetus, and places suitable for that.
Therefore consider then if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head; then fix the hooks into its eyes, or into its neck, or into its mouth, or into its palate, or under its beard, or into its clavicle, or into places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is upon its feet, then into its breastbone, and its middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand; and place the curve of it into that which is between the fingers of her left hand; and introduce it with her hand easily, and fasten the hook into one of those places which we have said until she reaches a void space.
Then she shall fasten opposite it another hook, or a third if necessary; so that there be equal contraction and it does not incline to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let there not be extension according to straightness only, but rather let it be moved to every side so that its exit is eased; just as is done when extracting a tooth.
And it is necessary that in that which is between it, the extension be softened. But if a part is retained from it, then the midwife must anoint certain fingers with oil and introduce them from the side so that she may turn what is retained with them.
Therefore if a part of the fetus exits, then let the hooks be changed to another place which is higher; do this until the whole fetus exits.
Therefore if the hand exits before the other parts and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction; then a cloth must be wrapped over it. Then extract it until the whole thing comes out, cut it either at the junction of the sole or on the elbow: and thus you must do in one hand, and in both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or constriction occurs at the outlet, or there is water collected in its head: then it is necessary that you insert the Spatumile spinosum between your fingers, and pierce the head with it, and extract the water: or break it up with an instrument called Almisdac: the form of which comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which follows this.
Similarly, if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, it is necessary that you make an incision with an algengemat: or extract it with a misdach, as we have said. Then draw out the bones with forceps.
But if the head comes out, and is constricted at the fork: then pierce it until the moisture which is in the chest flows out.
But if it does not become entangled, nor yield to the outlet: then cut the head into pieces as best you can.
But if there is an abscess below the belly, or there is dropsy in it: then you must pierce it until the moisture runs out of it.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then its attraction is easy, and its direction to the mouth of the uterus is slight.
But if it is constricted at the belly or chest: then you must extract it with a cloth which you have put over your hand: and pierce the belly or chest until what is in them runs out.
But if the remaining limbs are removed and the head returns: then let the midwife insert her left hand. Therefore, if the mouth of the uterus is open, let her insert her hand deep into the uterus, and seek out the head with it, and draw it up to the mouth of the uterus with her fingers: then let her insert one hook or two hooks into it and draw it out with them.
If the mouth of the uterus is constricted due to a hot abscess that occurs there, then it is not necessary for him to work on it, but rather he should then administer a pouring-on of humid and fatty things, and apply poultices, and sit in waters which soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it may be straightened; then let what we said about the living fetus be administered.
But if that is not possible, then let it be cut in pieces, and taken out.
And it is necessary that you do not leave anything from the secundines inside at all.
Therefore when you have completed your operation, then administer what we said about hot abscesses which occur in uteruses.
If a flow of blood should happen, then let her descend into astringent waters and administer the remaining care.
And I myself have indeed already seen a woman in whose belly a fetus had died, then she became pregnant again on top of it with another, afterwards the other fetus also died. Therefore it happened to her after a long time that an abscess occurred in her navel and it became swollen until it opened and began to emit pus.
I was therefore called for her care and I treated her for a long time, and the wound did not consolidate.
Therefore I put on it some poultices of strong attraction and an opening came out in its place. Then several days passed and another opening came out. I therefore wondered because there was no opening in the belly.
I therefore thought that they were from the bones of the dead fetus.
I therefore examined the wound and extracted many bones with the woman being in a better state; and she now lives thus for a long time, and emits little pus from its place.
I did not include this here except because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the craftsman physician administers with his hand from healing.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
On the forms of instruments necessary in the extraction of a fetus.
The shape of the spindle by which the mold is opened.
Below is the form of a Strictor, by which books are equaled, to which are equally two vortices at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, these Vortices ought to be more subtle than the Strictor: and so it is made, either from ebony or boxwood.
And let the width of all the wood be like two fingers; and the edge of each of both is like one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which have already been fixed in both, whose length is half a palm and more or less; and the width of each is like two fingers, or more or less.
And these two additions are inserted into the opening of the mold so that it is opened with them when you turn the two spindles.
Another shape similar to that one, more slender and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity let there be two additions, as you see here above; and the length of each addition from them is like a palm; and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore when you wish to open the mold with it, then make the woman sit on a bed with her feet hanging down, opening what is between her thighs. Then insert these two joined additions into the mold; and you are holding the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument as you do with forceps, equally according to the amount which you wish from the opening of the mold's mouth until the midwife does what she wants.
The shape of another spindle which the ancients called the Impelling Form, by which the fetus is impelled.
The shape of Almisdac, by which a large head is crushed; and it is assimilated to forceps, which have teeth at the extremity, as you see.
And sometimes it is made oblong, like forceps according to this shape as you see, which have teeth like the teeth of a saw by which it is incised and crushed.
The form of the instrument having one spine.
The form of the instrument having two spines.
The form of the Spatula having two ends for cutting off the head.
The form of two spatulas also wide.
All these instruments are used, and the more their kinds are multiplied and they are prepared by the craftsman, the quicker they are for their operation, and the higher they are esteemed by men, because of their quantity.
Therefore do not scorn to have from them an instrument prepared at hand. For the necessity of them is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the Extraction of a Second Child.
When the second child is retained at delivery, it is necessary that you command the weak woman to retain her breath. Then make her groan when she bears down; and bind your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it goes forth by this means, it is good.
If not: then take a jar and make a hole in the lid; and put into it herbs which open the mouth of the womb, such as calamus, rue, dill, chamomile, saffron, cinnamon wood, and centaury; take all these herbs or some of them, and steep them with water, and place them over the fire.
Then put a reed tube over the hole in the lid of the jar and insert the other end into the mouth of the womb; and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her groan as we have said. For the second child will then come forth with ease.
If, however, it remains after this treatment and does not come out, then order the midwife to submerge her hand in sesame oil or hollyhock mucilage. Then let her insert it into the vulva and seek with it the Secondine. When she therefore finds it, then let her take hold of it and gradually extend it.
But if it is attached deep within the uterus, then let her insert her hand as we have described until she finds the Secondine and gradually draw it out according to its straightness so that the uterus does not fall due to a strong pull; rather, it must be moved with ease to the right and left sides; then increase the force of the attraction. For it then obeys and is preserved and comes loose from its adhesion.
But if the uterus opening is constricted as we have already described, treat it with sneezing and a decoction from herbs.
But if it does not come out with all that we have described, then be careful not to tire yourself in repeated attempts at extraction.
But it is necessary that what comes out to the uterus opening from it, you tie to the woman's thigh. Then give her an enema with tetrapharmacum ointment. For it will putrefy it after a few days and dissolve, and come out.
But truly when it putrefies, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head and harms the weak one. It is therefore necessary that suitable fumigations be administered for it.
Some of the ancients who are already experienced have used fumigation from nasturtium and dried figs.
The form of the instrument by which a woman is fumigated for retention of the Secondine and menses.
Let it be made from glass like an alembic or from lead; and let the wide end be placed over the fire; and let the fumigation be placed on top of prunes; let her hold it until that fumigation is consumed; and repeat another.
Chapter Seventy-Nine.
On the Treatment of an Unperforated Anus.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which have already been constricted by a delicate membrane. Therefore, the midwife must perforate that membrane with her finger; and if she does not, she must perforate it with a sharp spatulette; and beware of touching the sphincter muscle.
Then place wool soaked in wine and oil upon it. Then care for it with ointments until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will constrict, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
And it now happens that constriction of the anus also occurs from consolidation of a wound or an abscess. Therefore, it is necessary to cut that consolidation, and then treat it with what we have said about treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty.
On the Treatment of Fistulas which Occur in the Lower Part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are swellings and enlargements which happen near the anus externally; or in the space of one portion; and there is one fistula, or several.
When that swelling grows old, it opens, and a watery or thin pus flows from it.
And sometimes these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore penetrating ones are recognized by the fact that they discharge during defecation and flatulence when the patient uses defecation; and perhaps a worm emerges from them.
And there are fistulas which, when they are penetrating into the space up to the bladder and up to the urethra of the penis. And there are penetrating ones up to the hip joint and up to the coccygeal node.
To determine whether the fistula penetrates to the anus, or not, you should insert your index finger into the anus and pass a slender probe of metal, such as brass or iron, into the fistula when there is no twisting.
But if there is twisting, then insert a slender lead probe, or a horsehair, into it until you feel the probe or hair against your finger.
If you do not feel it deeply and nothing issues from the opening, neither excrement, nor flatulence, nor worms as I have said, then know that it is non-penetrating; therefore hasten to treatment.
And when the fistula penetrates to the bladder or the urethra, its indication is the discharge of urine from it and the prevention of urine flow so that the site may be consolidated with medicines.
But if it penetrates to the hip joint or the acetabulum, its sign is that a probe reaches there if there is no twisting up to the opening and the finding of pain around the area and pus always discharges from it; and medicine does not work in it nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all; and their treatment is toil and vanity for foolish doctors who undertake it.
But in that which is not penetrating and not old, healing is hoped for with a cautery according to what I tell you, and which I have already proven: it is that the patient lies between your hands on his back; and raise his legs up above his hips, and let his hips be inclined towards his belly. Then insert a lead or brass probe, if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
But if the patient feels it around the anus, then it is necessary that you insert your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel that the tenon has already pierced through itself and detected it, beyond which you feel between your finger and the sifac, or flesh; then therefore know for certain that it is penetrating. Do not therefore labor in it, since it is not curable as we have said.
They however said that the flesh is healed in some men, and from the medication by which aid is hoped for, it is, to burn a subtle cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has preceded, and to insert it ignited into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat that twice or three times until you know that all those added filtered fleshes similar to a bird's feather quill have already been scorched.
Then treat it with linen cloths wrapped in butter until those scorched fleshes come out; then treat it with ointments generating flesh and strengthening them.
If therefore it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it never heals with any other treatment.
And if the tenon comes out and does not reach your finger which is in the anus, and there is a thick membrane of flesh or sifac between it and the tenon; and you see the fistula in that which follows the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will cut with the tenon that is in the fistula until the cutting reaches where the extremity of the tenon has reached, and the tenon is freed and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered fleshes which are assimilated to a bird's feather quill so that nothing remains from them.
But if blood overcomes you and loosens between you and your operation so that you cut those fleshes; then it is better, and quicker for healing, to cauterize it with fire or a sharp medicine.
For the cautery with fire brings together good qualities, burning those added fleshes and cutting off blood, and drying up moistures.
Then care for it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, or bruised stems, until the place emits pus, and those burnt fleshes go out into the pus.
Then care for that wound with ointments that are consolidating, producing hard flesh; and they are medicines in which there is astringency and desiccation.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed; and do not fear repetition.
But if the fistula has already reached as far as the depth of the anus, and is lengthened from an excess of the body; then insert your finger into the anus, and inquire with it.
If therefore you feel a probe, and between you and it there is a membrane of fat, or flesh, and it is near penetration; then there is no operation in that, except according to the way of trust and hope.
Which is because it is administered in it one of three ways: either that you cauterize it itself, as we have said; or you cut away from it until you reach its concavity so that it is possible for you to insert cloths, and care for it from the proximity. And do not proceed much with the cutting so that you do not fall into the muscle containing the anus, and there occurs upon the weak an exit of excretion without will. Then care for it with what we have said.
For perhaps it will be healed; or you lengthen that membrane and clean it after you have dried it up to the anus, as we said, either with a probe, or with another instrument of sharp extremity. Then clean those filtered flesh which are in the incision, up to where you are able with examination.
Then try the restoration of the whole wound with the incision, and the upper orifice where it is consolidated; and the fistula remains open in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier on the weak.
And sometimes a fistula is sutured according to this way; and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula, and it is on the side of the anus around the surface of the body with skin and the margin of the anus; then take a probe of perforated extremity, which is like an awl aleskef, that is, preparing leather, according to this form. Insert into it a thread wrapped with five threads around it. Then insert a probe with a thread into the fistula until it reaches its concavity.
If therefore it is penetrating into the inner margin of the anus nearby, then draw out the thread from that hole so that you insert your finger into the anus and draw out the end of the thread, and join together both ends: and tighten both, and let it remain for one or two days; and as much as the thread sinks into the flesh, tighten it well until those pieces of flesh which are between the two ends of the thread fall off; then treat the wound until it consolidates and heals.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate by any means prepared for you unless it is very deep. For it is not fitting for you to do that because of a tear, so as not to fall into it.
Then proceed with it as we said until it heals.
The shape of the speculum of a spine by which a fistula is split, whose curvature is very sharp and the other part is not sharp so that it does not fall into what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI.
On the suturing of hemorrhoids from which blood flows from their incision and treatment of fissures.
There are hemorrhoids according to two manners. For they are either within the anus similar to bladders of coals or plums, or as if they are grains of a grape. And from them there are small ones and large ones; and blood flows from them always, and it is one and there are many. And they are outside the anus and at its extremities.
But those which are outside the anus are according to most few in moisture from which runs clear water or a little blood with constant flow and they are according to the color of the body.
The treatment for that which is within the anus is to make the patient strain, and press until it comes out of the anus, and you see the hemorrhoid. Therefore suspend them with hooks; or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at their roots.
But if hooks do not hold them because of their moisture and softness, then take them with a rough cloth or with a swab of double wool and draw them out with your fingers. Then cut them, and sprinkle some hot powders over them after the incision so that they stand in place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then care for them with the remaining treatment until they are healed.
But if the anus does not yield to evacuation, then give the patient an enema in which there is a little bite so that it washes what is in the anus and yields to evacuation quickly when the patient strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are external from the anus, then their matter is light and easy; and it is to take them with your fingernail or take them with a hook, and cut them. Then care for them with what we have said until they are healed.
And he who recoils from incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer suture of them according to this manner. Take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle. Then draw out the hemorrhoid to above and make it penetrate with the needle at its root on one side; and twist the two ends of the thread at the lower part of the needle so that they are lateral; and tighten the hemorrhoid with a strong ligature. Then tie the thread and remove the needle.
Do this with all the hemorrhoids and leave one out of them; nor suture it so that blood flows from it freely.
Then place upon the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil, or carrot, and put this upon the anus; and instruct the patient to rest. Then let him go until it falls off.
When therefore the bandages fall off, care for them with ointments, and other treatment, until they are healed.
On the Care of Fissures Very often they happen from the drying of feces, and constriction of nature.
But when they become ancient, and medicine does not prevail in them: then it is necessary that you scrape them with the point of a Spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the upper layer which prevents them from consolidating be removed from them. Then care for them until they consolidate as is necessary.
But if they do not consolidate: then repeat them with a more vigorous reason than the first, until they bleed much, and are skinned; then care for them until they are healed.
On the Care of Inverted and Non-Inverted Hemorrhoids, and Dry Bandages and Adhesions.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
Know that a hemorrhoid is nothing but a round node according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail: and it occurs throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes: and pain arises from them upon walking.
It is therefore necessary that what is around the head of the nail be cut, and be held with forceps or a hook, and be eradicated from its root.
Then care for it; and if you wish cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
But from dry bandages one is a small eminence, rough, filtered, round above the surface of the body. And they very often occur in the hands of children.
And its treatment is to extend it with a small instrument and cut it; or to tie it with silk thread; or with hair, until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been said before with fire or with a sharp medicine; and now the inverted and non-inverted hairs are eradicated with an instrument that I will mention.
However, a wart is also a small, thick eminence, filtered over the surface of the body, extending deep; and when it finds cold, it feels like an ant bite therein and happens throughout the body; and most of it occurs on the hands.
And its treatment is to take a cannula from a goose feather or a strong vulture feather; and place that cannula over the wart until the circle of the cannula contains its parts. Then revolve your hand with the cannula until you cut what is in the circumference of the wart; and place it deep into the flesh and eradicate the wart from its root.
And if you wish, do this with an air or iron cannula according to this form.
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawing upward, covered with a bandage so that its grasp on the finger and its own revolution are eased.
And if you wish, cauterize the wart according to what has been said before in the cauterization of hairs in the chapter on cautery.
Chapter 83.
Concerning the forms of instruments which are administered for fistulas in diseases of the anus, and the resolution of the abdomen, and colic.
Sometimes the clyster is made of silver, or brass, or cast bronze, or hammered bronze.
And sometimes it is made from these instruments small and large according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the treatment of small children, let it be small; and those whose anuses are weak or painful, so also their clysters.
Large enema.
Its tube, that is, the upper head in which the uterus is tied, should be wide according to this manner: and let it have a distinction so that the uterus is tied as you see, and its lower end which is inserted into the anus, should be deaf, that is, not open, smooth, very drawing to subtlety: and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker, and let the uterus in which the medicines are placed be from an animal's bladder: or from a sheepskin, which should be according to the shape of a small asofrati: whose root is one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes as it revolves, and the space which is between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread from raw silk, which should be from ten threads, or thereabouts, and join with it a uterus like an asofrati. When therefore the uterus is placed in it, tighten at the head of the enema between two distinguishing constrictions strongly. Then enema with it.
And this is the shape of the circle and the holes depicted here.
But the quality of enematizing with it is: to fill the uterus, or bladder with medicine which has been heated according to the amount tolerated by the sick person: and be careful that it is not too hot, or too cold.
Then let the sick person lie on his back, and raise his legs to a higher position: and put under him an inthaa, that is, a piece of leather on which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then smear the end of the enema with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or similar substances. Then insert it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant grasp the clyster with both his hands at once and squeeze it forcefully until he evacuates everything that is in the clyster into the intestine.
Then withdraw the clyster, and let the patient raise his legs to the wall.
If the medicine compels him to evacuate, then he should hold it in as long as he can. And if he sleeps with it all night, it is better for his recovery.
But if you clyster him in illnesses of abdominal distension and his evacuation is hastened, then repeat the clyster until he is healed.
But if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very fine and gentle, like those used for children.
And this is the form of a fine clyster over which there should be a tight skin.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
I have already said in the division on the treatment of wounds what was fitting for the book, and I will say here their treatment with the hand and with medicines according to supplementation.
Therefore, I say that wounds are diversified according to what causes them and according to the place on which it falls.
The things by which wounds are made are often these, such as the throw of a stone: or a cut with a sword or knife; or a blow with a spear, wood, or arrow: and similar things from many objects.
But wounds that are made according to the locations of the body are like a wound that falls on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or back; and similar ones from those limbs.
And I am going to relate the treatments of some wounds so as to establish that reasoning and rule for the rest of the wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head: for the recollection of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say. Therefore, when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture of the bone, then consider whether it is from the throw of a stone or something similar, and has already broken only the skin, and the wound is large, and you fear an unfavorable outcome of a hot abscess: then hurry and immediately perform cephalic phlebotomy, and do not delay it; and draw out blood from him according to the amount of his strength. If there is nothing prohibiting phlebotomy, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the sick person beware of overeating, wine, and rich foods.
But if something prohibits reduction, then let him reduce his food intake and not approach meats or wines.
And place over the wound, if a hot abscess occurs to him, cotton infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
But if you are safe from a hot abscess, then place over the wound, if it is fresh with its own blood and has not been changed by the air, the powder which I will relate a little later: and if the air has already changed it, then place something on it as an ointment until it heals.
But if the wound is large, or is from an incision of a sword and similar instruments: and the lips thereof do not come together by powders, then bring them together with suture, according to what I will relate in abdominal wounds about their sutures.
But if the wound is already detached from the bone and hangs, and there is no retention of it except in a small suspender, then incise it. Then care for the wound with medicines and restore hard flesh in its place instead of skin, and instruct the sick person to nourish himself with foods that are dense, such as heads and cooked wheat, until it is like a pulse; and things similar to these.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut out all the putrefied part and throw it away; then treat that.
But if in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flows and it is not severed by medication, then examine above the artery. If therefore you find it not cut, then cut it with a scalpel or tie it off. But if necessity compels when that which we have said does not work, then cauterize it until the blood is severed.
But if these wounds are small and simple, then the matter of them is easy, light so that you sprinkle this powder over them: because it hardens them before they change from air.
Method of Incarnating Powder. Take one part frankincense, and two parts dragon's blood, and three parts quicklime; grind the whole very well, and sift it; and fill the wound with it and constrict it well with a bandage until it adheres to it well and clots with the blood.
Then bind it on top with wool, and leave it alone, and do not loosen it while it remains so that it does not fester. For medicine is not removed from it until it heals.
And sometimes lime alone does it when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood present; and it also performs the same action in many large wounds, when it is fitting to put it on them and bandage them tightly, and they are recent wounds with their own blood which has not been changed by air.
But if it has already been changed by the air with some alteration, especially when it is in summer time, then the application of this powder is not appropriate; therefore treat it with what we said so that you put some digestive ointments on them or apply poultices made from barley flour with water and honey on them until they discharge pus. Then let the rest be treated with care until they are healed.
But if it should happen that there is a fracture in the bone with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps; and I will now speak of the treatment of head fractures in what is to come.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
But if a wound should happen in the neck, then there is no difference in its treatment and that of a simple head wound.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has already been cut, then if it is a nerve, there is nothing to be done except to apply treatment that promotes strong astringency, such as alum and saffron and those similar. Because they are harmful to nerves and constrict them.
And do not put anything cold at all on the wound; because the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is more sublime than other members: and let it be treated with medicines that are mild, such as lime slaked with sweet water repeatedly infused with oil or rose oil, and tutia when washed even with sweet water: and the remaining mineral stones, in this manner, and soft ointments.
But if the wound is large, then administer a suture or bind its lips with powders. Then treat it until it is healed.
But if the wound is deep or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus has already collected, then pierce it in a deeper place.
But if an artery has already been cut in the wound and its blood flow follows: then separate or cauterize it and tie it; or cauterize it, if necessity calls for it.
But if the vertebrae of the throat or the whole throat have already fallen and the ligaments are unharmed: then bring the lips of the skin together with a suture over the canal of the throat, and do not touch the throat at all; rather equalize it and return it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a decent ligature and let it alone for several days. Then care for it with ointments which have astringency and drying properties until it heals.
But if the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then examine it: then if there are bone fragments in it, extract them all, or as many as you can; and what is not prepared, leave it until the wound putrefies, and separates and its discharge is alleviated.
But if the wound is recent, then sprinkle upon it the powder which I have described. But if bone appears to be seen in the wound and a fistula forms: then treat it with that of which its remembrance comes in its own chapter.
Concerning a wound on the spatulas.
If there is a blow from a spear or knife and you see its depth, then consider. Therefore if windiness comes forth from the wound when a weak person breathes out from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it is not deep for him and is recent with its own blood: then do not put powder on it from the first hour, nor bind it up so that the blood is not retained in its depth and returns to the heart and kills the weak person; but put an attractive ointment on it and dissolve it twice a day.
But if ointment is not present to you then put an old rag in the opening of the wound so that it absorbs what comes out of it from its humidity; and make the weak person sleep on the wound so that what gathers in it runs.
But if three days or more have already passed since the wound and it does not happen to the weak person that there is a spasm nor bad trembling of the heart nor constriction in breathing, and you see its remaining dispositions are good: then know that the wound is safe. Therefore treat it carefully with ointments and other care until it is healed.
But if healing is delayed and it is always open, then know that a fistula has already formed. Therefore treat it from its chapter.
And if the wound is from an incision by a sword or knife and it is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what precedes from suture if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone and chips come out of it, then probe the wound and hasten to extract those chips if they are separate. But if they are not separate, then leave them until the wound festers, for extraction is alleviated then.
But for the accidents of the remaining wounds on the other limbs, judgment in treatment is the same judgment that we have said.
Concerning wounds of the belly and the protrusion of intestines and their removal.
Disruption which happens to the belly is great, medium and small.
From a great one therefore sometimes intestines or a number of intestines protrude.
Therefore insertion and suture of them is more urgent and difficult.
And even a small disruption is difficult in another way. It therefore follows that its reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten in inserting it back into its place immediately; because it swells, opens, and enlarges, therefore its entry becomes difficult.
Because of this, therefore a medium disruption is better: because reduction of the intestines with it is not difficult as it becomes in these two kinds.
Know that suture of the belly is according to four methods.
Two sutures are common and apply to the suture of the belly and the suture of other wounds of the body; and two other sutures specifically belong to the remaining wounds. But they are more appropriate for suture of the abdomen and I will describe each one to you with explanation.
Therefore I say that when a wound is small and something from the intestines protrudes from it and it is difficult to reduce it: then it is due to one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption as we said or because there is inflation in the intestine due to cold air.
When therefore it is so, it is necessary that you warm it so that you submerge a sponge or cloth dampened in lukewarm water alone. Or you boil in water rue and cyperus and spikenard and embrocate with it the intestine until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine in which there is astringency does it and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see that the inflation has already been resolved then moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow or mullein has been boiled. For by that its entrance is eased when it is small.
But if its return is excused after this treatment then make a small incision in the disruption with an instrument by which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when prepared than incision and it is not necessary to resort to cutting after necessity and lack of instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted and sharp and the other part not sharp and let its subtle end not be with the subtlety of a spatula but rather be a little duller and it is that which resembles a chisel as you see.
When therefore the wound is enlarged and the intestine enters then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its own place if possible above it for it is better.
But when the disruption is wide in the lower part of the abdomen then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back and put his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place its head and chest higher than its lower parts.
And similarly if there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your attention and consideration always on this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And thus it must be done in large wounds and in medium wounds.
But in small wounds, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then make a pious minister present between your hands, who will hold the whole rupture with his hand and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it for him who follows a stitch after a stitch, and this is the way of a common single suture.
And it is necessary that you take a needle or a number of needles according to the size of the width of the wound. Then leave from the extremity of the rupture a length of a ring finger, that is, of a physician; and fix a needle beyond which you will insert thread into it at two extremities of skin with two extremities of the underlying tissue, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have now brought together the two extremities of skin and the two extremities of the underlying tissue, and four rows are made.
Then tighten with double thread around the needle several times from both sides at once until two lips of the wound are brought together with decent joining.
Then leave a length of a finger also and fix another needle in them; afterwards draw it with thread just as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease doing this until you complete the constriction of the whole wound.
And let the needles be medium between thickness and thinness; because thin needles penetrate flesh very quickly and thick ones are difficult to enter into skin. Therefore it must be that they are medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in two nearby extremities of the skin too much: so that the flesh does not quickly fall in, and the wound opens before its own consolidation, and they are not even elongated with the suture, so that the wound is not prevented from consolidating.
Then cut the extremity of the needle, so that they do not injure the weak one in his sleep; and put little wads of cloths on every side, which hold the extremities of the needles; and let them remain until you know that the wound has already consolidated.
And this kind of suture with needles is indeed more suitable in small wounds. Because it suffices in the suture of those, one needle, or two, or about that many.
But the second suture is common, so that aggregates with a suture have four extremities: two extremities of skin, and two extremities of silk in one thread, with a needle in which is wrapped a thread equal in subtlety, and thickness.
Then when you have penetrated with the needle those four extremities, reduce the needle from the side from which you began the same way, so that the drawn thread falls from the upper part of the wound: so that it is a suture, like the suture of cloth with which cloths are tied, and put between every suture an amount equal to the thickness of a finger.
But men know this suture.
And with this suture I sewed up my own wound which had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm: and about two palms had come out from the intestines from the middle intestine, and the disruption was in the middle of the belly.
I therefore returned it after his intestine stood outside the wound for 24 hours.
The wound therefore consolidated in a space of about 15 days; and I looked after it, and it was healed; and he lived afterward for many years, engaged in his own affairs.
And the physicians had judged that it would not heal.
And it was a wonder that I did not treat it with ointment. For I was in a place where neither ointment nor anything from medicines was found. I therefore placed an old shirt over the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water until it was healed.
But I will mention two proper kinds of suture according to the account of speech.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture: and you introduce the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle penetrates into the skin, and goes into the muscle according to the length and width of the whole body: let the end of the thread remain on the side in which you introduced the needle, and make it penetrate at the other end from the inner part to the outer part at the other extremity.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate thus in the same extremity from the outer part to the inner: and let the end of the thread which is on this side remain, and make the needle penetrate into the other extremity from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration that you make it penetrate into the thread at the extremity which is in its part, until it penetrates the whole.
Then begin also from this same side and sew it with the end which is from the thread on the other side, and draw out the needle from the skin which it has penetrated.
Then return it into that skin and sew the end of the thread which is on the other side with this end: and draw it out from the skin which is on its side, and do this vice versa one time, and another, until you have sewn all the outside according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that you sew it like the sutures of skinners who sew skins: so that you sew the thread once from one side with one end of the skin: and that you let it go again on the other side, until you complete it.
The second kind of suture proper, which Galen mentioned.
His speech is recounted so that it should be done in a similar way as some physicians do, in such a manner that they bring every part to its corresponding matching counterpart by nature: and they apply the extremity of sifac to its other extremity, and the extremity of mirach to its other extremity. And that is done according to what I tell you.
You must fix the needle into the extremity of mirach nearest to you from the external part, and make it penetrate to the internal one in that alone: and let go of the two extremities of sifac. Then revolve the needle and make it penetrate from the external part to the internal one into the two extremities of sifac, namely on both sides.
Then revolve both, and also make both penetrate from the internal one up to the external one, into the other two extremities of mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is the suture of which previous mention was made.
This then is the speech of Galen also.
And some said. The suture of the abdomen is rectified with two needles. Which is, that you introduce one thread into both of them: and begin with the introduction of the needle, from that which is near you, and make it penetrate up to the other side, according to the suture of those who have equal skins.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the abdomen, then its suture is more difficult than that of the other places of the abdomen.
But judgment of treatment of a wound is judgment of other wounds.
Which is, because when you obtain a fresh wound with its own blood, before the air alters it; and you reduce the intestines and fit them to themselves: then put powdered incarnatium on top of it.
If the air has already changed the wound, then put some ointment upon it, which are present to you, until they make pus, and the filaments fall away, and the sutures become consolidated. Then treat it as the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed.
But if medicines are not present to you, then immediately put upon it, from the time it begins to discharge pus, old lint; and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need another medicine in many conditions when wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound may extend to the principal limbs in injury, then it is necessary that you immerse soft wool in oil of tempered warmth, or in rose oil, and put it around the places which are between the thighs, and under the armpits.
But if he feels pain, or punctures, or putrefaction in his bowels (for this often happens), then give him an enema with strong, black, warm wine; especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and is about to penetrate as far as its cavity.
And know that what is from the large intestine is of easier healing, and what is from them of the subtle (intestine) is of more difficult healing.
The intestine which is called Jejunum does not admit healing from a wound that occurs in it at all; and this is because of its multitude (of veins), which are large, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the nearness of its nature to nerves.
But if that which proceeds from the wound is gushing forth, and you overtake it fresh, draw it back according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
But if a space has passed it by and it has already become green, or black, then it is necessary that you bind it with thread above the place which has been stained from it, so that bleeding may not occur. For in the hip there are veins and arteries.
Then incise what is beyond that ligament, and place two ends of a thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that its attraction is relieved upon you, and its extraction at the hip, and the discharge of pus from the wound.
Recollection of a Wound That Occurs in the Intestine.
When disruption occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it is possible for it to be restored in some people; therefore because I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with a lance thrust, and the wound was from the right of the stomach, and the wound was old; and a fistula was made, and discharge came out of it, and flatulence.
Therefore I applied treatment until I was not doubtful of its healing; nor did I cease to refine it until he was healed and the place consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the sick man, that a bad accident might occur on his belly; and no bad accident befell him from it. And he remained in better dispositions, eating and drinking, and becoming fat, and entering the bath, and exercising in his diets.
And already some experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it must be ligated according to this method. And it is, that you should take ants of large heads. Then bring together two lips of the wound, and place one ant from those that have an open mouth above the two lips of the wound.
