When its head is therefore above them and it clenches its mouth, cut off its head; for it adheres and does not loosen.
Then place another ant near the first one, and do not cease doing that with an ant after an ant according to the quantity of the whole wound.
Then reduce it and its wound. For those heads remain attached within the intestine until the intestine is eaten and healed so that no harm befalls the sick man entirely.
And now it is possible that the intestine also be ligated with a fine thread which has been drawn out from an animal's intestine and attached to it after it is introduced into the needle.
And it is so that you take the extremity of this thread from the intestine and it be pared down greatly. Then bind at its end a fine linen thread wound around. Then introduce that thread into the needle and with it the intestine. Then reduce it into the rupture.
And this method of suture with ants and intestine is not except according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is large and wide especially if it is in one of the slender intestines then there is no remedy except refinement and there is altogether no healing of it.
On the care of fistulas and fissures
Know that every wound or abscess when it grows old and becomes ancient and turns into an ulcer and does not become firm and emits pus always and is not cut off then it is called universally a fistula no matter in what limb it may be and we call it zuchemen.
And a fistula indeed according to truth is a knotted filtration, hard, white with which there is no pain and which has the concavity of a bird's feather and on account of that some call it risati that is a feather.
And it is moist in certain hours which emits pus. And perhaps the moisture splits open in certain hours. And sometimes this moisture is much and sometimes little and it is thick and sometimes subtle.
And I have already set forth above when I spoke of wounds that every sore which does not heal and flesh is not born in it is not due except to one of the novel causes
One of these is because of the small amount of blood in the body or because of its bad nature or because there is tough flesh between it and its lips preventing the birth of flesh or because it has much filth and dirtiness or because there is a putrid ulcer in it and the matter sent to it is of bad quality or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment or because there is a falling corruption in the region which is a kind of pestilence, or by the nature of the region as happens in Caesaraugusta in which digestion of illnesses is difficult and healing of abscesses is delayed by nature; or because it has one bone or a number of bones.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their treatments with medicines in division; and it is necessary here to make you know the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by the way of operation with the hand.
Know therefore that a Fistula occurs in all the members of the body. For from them are Fistulas which reach the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews; or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs; or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints composed in composition; such as the hand, and foot; and from them are those which go to the proximity of the principal member, and similar ones.
Which therefore of these Fistulas is according to this manner, is from illnesses difficult of healing, and does not receive a cure, and dealing with it is toil and ignorance.
But if it is from Fistulas some of whose depth does not reach these places which I said; and you hope, and trust in its cure, and wish to come to the knowledge of it, then take a probe of iron, or steel, if the Fistula tends according to straightness, and inquire into it with it.
But if there is crookedness in the Fistula, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead. Since lead is softened by the gentleness of its own body and bends around its crookedness.
If a fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to mark upon it with a probe. Therefore cleanse out from among its openings one opening.
For the moisture with which you cleanse it flows towards the other openings and runs out of them.
Then carefully inquire in what way it is possible for you to know that there is a bone or a nerve and whether the fistula's cavity is distant or near; or whether it is a single fistula which has many openings, and stand upon the whole of it with all your strength, inquiring of the sick person and finding pain by compression with your hand upon the place, and similar signs and causes arising from an abscess.
Therefore when you have stood upon all this true station, then go to the cure with confidence and consider then whether the fistula is near and apparent or in a healthy place far from a joint or a nerve or an artery or a vein or one of the places which I told you: then cut the fistula according to what has been previously said in my account and remove what is in it by filtration and corrupted flesh which arises at its opening from flesh or pus and similar things and treat it until it heals.
But if the fistula is of distant depth and according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut it deep as much as it is possible for you. Then clean it from all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply linen wrapped in sharp medicines and introduce it up to the cavity of the fistula which you comprehended with an iron; do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the cavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it heals.
If it does not heal by this means, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
Therefore if the cause of the fistula is bone and it is confirmed with you, then examine it yourself and cut it according to what I have told you; if nothing prevents you from a vein, or a nerve, or a principal member as we said.
If therefore bone is revealed to you and there is something of corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it out until all its corruption is removed. Then care for it with that which causes it to flesh, until it heals.
But if it does not heal and emits pus as it was doing, then know that you have not reached the total eradication of the corruption.
Therefore expose it again a second time and make a precise incision into it, and cleanse it with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If therefore it heals, that is good; and if not, then know that the corruption is in a distant depth of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore there is no skill for you except to leave it to nature.
But if the bone is small and corrupted and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps that are suitable for it.
But if there are many bones, then make a precise extraction of them all and do not leave any with your strength.
If something from the humors opposes you and there is no fear or error in their incision then cut into those humors and flesh if they are there and free the bones.
But if you do not have preparation for removing the bones from your time then place something over the wound so that it putrifies it and leave it until what is around those bones from flesh and humors putrefies.
And watch the wound so that it does not consolidate. And let the incision which you have made in your operation be constricted. If you are afraid, place on it a cotton wool soaked in sulfur ground with oil; or cotton wool soaked in Egyptian ointment, or some green ointments.
For when those humidities, to wit, putrefy and the bones are separated from them, their removal is eased, and their attraction as well.
But if there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg, or the bone of the hip, and similar to those, and that which has become corrupted from it is a large part; then scrape it with the last rasp until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
But if what is corrupted in it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it, and to cut it all the way to the place where the corruption reaches; then care for it until it consolidates.
And I tell you a story of a man who had a wound on his leg: so that you may place it as an example and help for your operation.
There was a young man of about thirty years of age to whom there came pain in his leg, from a cause which was moved upon him within the body, that is, from within, until the materials reached the leg, and it became inflamed with a large abscess; and he had no external cause.
Time therefore was extended with him in error of the physicians until the abscess was opened and many materials gathered with it. And his treatment was postponed until his leg became fistulous and many openings were made in it; which all emitted pus and the humidities of the body.
Therefore a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years and there was no wise man in the art of the hand until I was called and I saw his leg and the materials which were flowing from those openings with great flow: even the whole body of the man had already wasted, and his color was yellow.
Therefore insert a probe into one of the openings and let it reach the mouth.
Then I examined all the openings and found that they all led to one another from all parts of the leg.
I hastened and cut above one of these openings until I exposed a part of bone; and I found it decayed, for it was corroded and blackened and putrid and pierced through until it reached the marrow.
Therefore I sawed off what was revealed to me and prepared it from the decayed bone.
And I determined that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption which I cut off and sawed. And I had now eradicated it.
Then I placed the restoration of the wound with strengthening medicines over a long space. And it did not consolidate.
Then I probed and revealed above the bone a second time above the first revelation; and I found continuous corruption with the bone; and I sawed off what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I tried its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore I revealed above it again and did not cease to cut the bone in pieces and try its restoration; and it was not restored until I cut out from the bone about a palms breadth and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines and it consolidated quickly and healed; and this iteration was not necessary in its operation and section except because of the weakness of the sick man's constitution, and his lack of tolerance, and my fear for him concerning death; for he suffered from it every hour a bad fainting spell from excessive discharge.
He was therefore healed with perfect healing; and flesh grew hard in the place of the bone, and his condition became good, and his strength returned; and he returned to his usual habits; and it did him no harm at all in walking.
If however the prominent bone is already fractured in its place: it is necessary that you grasp it, in this manner: and it is: that you take a ligature and tighten it at the extremity of the prominent bone: and order someone to extend it upward: and place another ligature of thick wool above the first ligature. Then bind it above the flesh which is beneath the bone and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh downwards and you expose the flesh over the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood or a plank beneath the bone below decently: for when you do this, it is not prevented that it may cut corrupt flesh.
And it is necessary that the saw be over the corrupt place a little while so that there not be corruption in the concavity of the bone and that it does not appear in its appearance to the sense and compel towards the saw of its own vice another.
But if the bone is corrupt and is not prominent but rather continues its part with its part and the corruption is in its middle or in a part of it then expose all the flesh from all sides entirely. Then place wood beneath.
Afterwards saw it from the first part where the corruption is until the saw continues from the other part and let the saw be according to the length from the corruption a little while as we said.
But if the corruption is at the junction cut the corrupt junction itself and saw the bone where it continues from the other part.
But if the corruption is in the continuation of two junctions then there is no skill there except abrasion.
But if the corruption is in the bone of the hand or in the bone of the foot then the matter there is very difficult. But it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you and with whatever skill it is rectified for you when it does not oppose Vein or Nerve.
And know that cutting instruments and saws for cutting these bones are many, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and thinness, and size, and smallness, and hardness, and rarity. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare a suitable instrument for each type of operation.
And know that these operations signify to you the kind of instrument you need, when you have long study. And knowledge of the methods of this art, a testimony to the methods of illnesses; for then you will find by yourself what is suitable for it from instruments for every ailment.
And I affirm to you in the last part of this chapter the number of instruments, which you put as examples on which you cut, and the testing by which you try on others.

And it is not suitable unless joint heads are rubbed with it when they are corrupted, or the mouth is wide and large.

The shape of a razor which suits its purpose because it is perforated from bones.
Its end is triangular, and its points are sharp.

On the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Limbs putrefy, either from an external or internal cause.
Therefore when you have treated that corruption with medical care, and the treatment does not work; and you see the corruption entering into a limb which nothing repels: then it is necessary to cut that limb up to where the corruption reaches so that the weak person may be saved from death or from harm which is greater by the loss of a limb.
And the sign that this happens to a limb is that it blackens until it seems burned; or it rots after the blackness: until that rot reaches what follows the limb, and spreads into the whole body.
Therefore hasten with the incision of it, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain venomous creatures such as a sea scorpion, viper, weasel, and similar ones.
Therefore if the corruption or sting is in the extremity of a finger, then cut the finger and do not give space to the corruption so that it advances up to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it from the root of the forearm and do not give space to the corruption until it reaches the forearm.
And if it enters into it, then cut the arm at the elbow at its joint.
But if the corruption passes through and you see it around the elbow, then death is upon the sick man there. And besides that in treatment, the quantity of virtue is to be administered.
And do the same with the foot, when the corruption enters into a toe, then cut off itself at one of the nodes; and if it seizes the arch of the foot, then cut off the entire foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption already reaches above the knee, then there is no skill in it except dismissal and leaving the sick man to death.
And the method of cutting off a limb or binding it is to tie a ligature below the place where you wish to cut; and tie another ligature above the place. And let one attendant extend the lower ligature downward; and let another attendant extend the upper ligature upward. And you separate the flesh that is between the two ligatures with a broad spatula until all the flesh is exposed. Then cut or bind it.
And it is necessary that you put linen cloths on all sides, and the saw does not touch the healthy part;  and let the sick man have an added injury, and a warm poultice.
But if internal bleeding occurs while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly; or put some powders that staunch bleeding on top of it. Then return to your treatment until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage for it and care for it until it heals.
And I tell you that it happened to a man in his foot, the same thing that I tell you.
Because blackness occurred in his foot, with a burn that resembles fire; and that corruption first happened to his toe until it began to spread to the whole foot.
Therefore, a man recovered from himself after he saw the corruption fall into the member with its violence, which he found from pain and its burning; and it befell at the joint, and it was healed.
And after a long time passed him, that same corruption befell him in the index finger of his hand.
He came to me then, and I tried to recover that excess with what I placed upon his hand from medicines after the purification of his body; and the excess was not repulsed, and it began to proceed into another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
He therefore called upon me to cut his hand; and I did not want that, hoping from myself on the repulsion of that excess; and I also feared for him at the cutting of his hand death. For the virtue of the man was above the chance.
Afterward therefore he despaired of me, he returned to his land; and there reached me about him that he recovered, and he cut off his whole hand; and he was healed.
And I did not bring this account except that it may be a help upon that which falls of this kind of illness; and that there may be a meaning by which it is signified and according to which it is acted.
Concerning the cure of Sinuses, and the quality of clystering with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members, and the space of the abscess extends, until it makes a collection of pus; then it breaks or is perforated, and what is in it goes out as pus, and a vacant place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is upon it like a cloth that has already been thinned; and it does not reach its ultimate corruption, nor press into the mouth, nor into a nerve, nor into a ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus, and not a Fistula. However, if the space of it extends until the corruption presses into any one of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or a zuken.
And the care of it is, that you should cut all of its skin just as it turns over; and especially when it has been thinned and made like cloth; and you trust that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and that last corruption does not follow from it; and if there is a thickness in the skin from flesh, then care for it with a clyster; and it is as if you were to direct it towards the Sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it has a foul odor; then clysterize it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar and oil and ziniar, taking equal parts, are gathered in a vase; and they are decocted over fire until the medicine reddens; and comes to a thickness of honey.
Then take from it what is necessary for you; and dissolve it with water and honey, and clysterize the Sinus with it; and constrict its orifice, and let the medicine remain in it for the quantity of two hours.
Then extract it with expression; do that for so many days until the Sinus is cleansed and the stench is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with ash water when this medicine is not present for you; and it is as if you should take ash from vine wood, or ash from holly wood; and pour water over it. Then strain it and clysterize with it until you trust that the Sinus has already been washed.
But if the weak person does not tolerate the Egyptian medicine nor ash water, then clysterize it with water and honey to which something already mixed from ground ziniar has been added. Or clysterize it with honey and wine mixed together, since honey's property is to cleanse and wash; and the wine makes the Sinus adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times and are confident that the sinus corruption has already been removed, then give him an enema with something which promotes the growth of flesh in it.
And take diaphycon plaster and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or give him an enema with certain drugs and other ointments the properties of which I have written in the treatise on ointments.
But if the sinus opening is narrow and will not admit the enema cannula, then widen it a little with an iron instrument; or place in it a licorice stick wrapped in Egyptian ointment or basil ointment until it is enlarged.
And similarly if the opening is wide, then bring together its lips with sutures and allow from it a quantity into which the enema may enter without addition.
And similarly if the opening from which pus flows is raised to a higher place, then dry it in a lower depression within it so that the pus may flow downwards; for when pus is confined in the depth of the sinus it prevents flesh from growing there.
But if it is not possible for you to dry the sinus around its lower part according to your desire, then try to prepare the limb with a preparation so that the pus flows from it easily according to what is prepared for you. Try that nothing of the pus be retained in it at all; and take consolidating medicines which are placed over a sign suitable for what you desire. And spread them on linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a quantity that it makes contact with the whole sinus. Then cut with forceps a hole directly into the sinus opening, somewhat larger than the sinus opening. Then spread some soothing ointments on another cloth according to the size of the hole and place it over the first cloth at a distance from the sinus opening so that the discharge of pus is relieved; and do not remove the large cloth except after several days at once.
He who is above the Sinus opening is he whom you remove every hour so that what collects in it from pus may exit; and so that you may know whether what flows from it is much or little; digested or undigested.
And again consider with this the place of the Sinus itself, whether its master feels pain in it or not, and whether there is an abscess in it or not.
Therefore when your treatment proceeds thus and you see the matter which exits above the Sinus opening, then you know that the Sinus is already adhering or approaching adhesion; then loosen it in the morning or on the third day; and when you loosen the wound, then change another cloth and renew the ointment.
But if thin pus flows from the Sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, since there are some men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds and there are some who act contrary to that.
But if undigested pus flows from the Sinus after many days, then you know that the sinus is not adhering.
Therefore if the adhesion of the Sinus is delayed and its matter is prolonged; then add to the drying medicines, as physicians truly do, so that the medicines are moist in their substance and dry in their power: such as dyafinicon ointment when an excess of addition from zegi is added to it.
And the last of all by which it is cured is that you take dyafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and pour old wine equal to it in substance over it.
Then mix it well with it and use it.
And now honey acts similarly to this action when you do not have dyafinicon ointment present; boil it until it thickens and use it. Or take myrrh, aloe, and frankincense; grind the whole thing and sprinkle it over honey while it is on the fire. Then lay a linen cloth over it, and bind it tight over the Sinus; and anoint it with honey which you have boiled down until it is thick; and sprinkle powdered spices over it, and put a linen cloth over it, and bind it.
And sometimes long birthwort is administered with it, and roots of blue lily, and lily, and herb flour, and centaury. Use them simply or mixed, grind them, and sift them; and sprinkle them over the honey which I have described, and use it.
And if the Sinus condition persists and does not heal with this treatment, then know that there is corruption deep within it; or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or ligaments: then treat it with a treatment for Fistulas, according to what has been stated previously.
On the treatment of whitlow and nail abrasion, and incision of an added finger, and sectioning.
Whitlow is an abscess which arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot; and perhaps it arises in the other fingers.
And therefore when the matter of it persists, and its cure is neglected, and it becomes inflamed with a hot abscess; or it corrupts and emits pus until it corrodes the root of the nail; and perhaps it corrupts the whole of it, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone until it has a foul smell; and the end of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore when you treat it according to what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not take effect; then it is necessary that you cut with an instrument the whole excess which remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cautery is very helpful.
But if the bone is sound, and the nail is also sound, and the added empty parts of the nail already press the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it, and injure it: then it is necessary that you place a thin Radium under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it upwards; and cut the flesh easily, and put over what remains of the flesh medicines that burn and corrode until all of it is removed. Then care for it with ointments until it heals.
But if corruption has already set in the bone, then it is necessary that you cut into that bone and extract it: for it will never heal as long as a corrupted bone remains within it.
But if you see corrosion and corruption advancing into the finger, then cut it at one of its joints, according to what was previously mentioned about it. Then care for it until it heals.
But if it should happen that there is attrition or percussion to the nail, and there is a sharp pain in it, then it is necessary first to let blood from a weak person. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatulum in a transverse section from above downward.
And be careful that the incision does not reach the flesh which is beneath the nail, for you will cause a very sharp pain through it and it will be a reason for added inflammation of the flesh in the place.
Then care for the area with that which soothes pains.
Indeed the added finger which is born in some men's hands, perhaps it is all flesh; and perhaps there are bones in some of them; and perhaps a nail exists in it: and some of them are born at the root of the joint, or its origin is in some joints of the finger. That which is born in some joints of the finger does not move; and that which is born at the joint of the finger perhaps moves.
Therefore the incision of that which is fleshy from them is easy. That is, you should cut it at its root with a broad spatulum.
But the treatment of that whose origin is in the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore abstain from cutting it.
But in that which is born on the finger at the last joint, then it is necessary that you first cut its flesh with a round incision down to the bone. Then a saw with one of those saws suitable for it. Then care for it until it heals.
Moreover, consolidation which occurs between the fingers often happens either from the birth of man, that is, he is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or from burning by fire, and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place cloths between them soaked in rose oil so that they do not consolidate quickly and separate them, or place a thin sheet of lead between them until the place consolidates as it should.
And similarly if consolidation occurs with certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation according to what is prepared and suits the shape of the limb.
On the Incision of a Vice.
Varices are veins enveloped, thick, full of superfluous melancholic humors which occur in many members of the body; and most often their outcome is in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel and work, and carry things.
Therefore, it is necessary that you administer exercise of the body from black bile several times with vigorous shaking.
Then phlebotomy for the patient to those who are basil-like.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they are cut and black blood is extracted. And the other method is that a vein is lifted and the whole thing is extracted.
The section of it is according to this method. First embrocate the leg with plenty of hot water until the thick fecal blood is resolved. Then bind the leg of the sick person from the upper part of his hip down to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw out the blood into your hand from the lower part of the leg, up to the top, and the phlebotomist must first weaken the one above. Then cut the vein all the way down until a quantity of blood comes out which you see to be sufficient and which the strength of the sick person can tolerate.
Then tie it off and instruct him to abstain from foods that produce black bile; and repeat the evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full and it harms the sick person.
The raising of it is according to this method. Shave the leg of the sick person if there are many hairs on it. Then immerse him in a bath or embrocate his leg with warm water until it reddens and the veins swell; or he should be exercised vigorously if there is no bath available for him until the limb is heated.
Then cut the skin directly over the vein lengthwise; or at its end near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks and expose the vein from all sides until it appears to the senses; and at its appearance you will see it reddish towards a dark decline. When therefore it comes out of the skin, you will see it white as if it were a cord.
Then insert a cannula under it until it rises and comes out of the skin; suspend it with a blunt hook, gentle.
Then cut another incision near that incision by three fingers. Then expose the skin from the upper part of the vein until it appears. Then raise it with cannulas as you did and suspend it with another hook as you did first.
Then dry another incision, or several incisions, if necessary. Then elevate it and cut it with the last incision at the heel.
Then draw it out to the incision which is above it.
And do that until you draw it out from the incision which is above, or higher than all the incisions, until it comes out whole; then cut it.
But if it does not obey you to be drawn out and elevated: then introduce a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and draw it out; or introduce a Radium under it, and revolve your hand in every direction until it comes out. And be careful that it is not cut. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to elevate it greatly, and harm enters upon the weak part from it.
When therefore you have drawn it all out: then place wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil over the place of the wound; and care for it until it is healed.
But if the Vine is contracted, having a twist with an involution towards its parts, and it is not as we said according to straightness: then it is necessary that you split it over all the parts of its twist, and the places of its own involution.
Then suspend it with hooks until you raise it all; and be careful at your incision over it that you do not cut a vein, or wound it: for it will become difficult for you to raise, or draw it out. Therefore preserve your prudence.
The Form of the Falling Vine by which it is Healed.
The Form of a Blind Vine.
It does not have a curvature like the other Hooks, nor an acute extremity, so that it would not wound a vein; and it is of a thick, gentle flexuousity; for if it were slender, it would cut the vein by its slenderness: but let its thickness be excessive, as we said.
On the Extraction of a Leg Vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Alizegi and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands of little tenderness, that is, those in which neither herb nor tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its generation is indeed from putrefaction which occurs under the skin, just as serpents and ascaris worms occur within bodies, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this vein's event is that a violent inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the place blisters. Then the vein begins to come out from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
When therefore its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary that you wrap upon it a piece of lead, whose weight is from one scruple to two scruples and bind it: and let the lead hang suspended from the leg.
Whenever therefore anything exits from it to the outside, wrap that in the lead and bind it. If it lengthens much, then cut a part of it and wrap up the remainder. And do not cut it out entirely before it comes out completely. For if you cut it, it contracts and enters into the flesh, and causes an abscess to occur, and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be handled gently and extracted gradually until it comes out from its last part, and nothing remains of it in the body: and from this vein indeed, there exits in some men what is in its length five palms and ten. And now it has come to me that it came out of a man from twenty palms.
But if it should happen to you during your time of treatment: then introduce a ray into its hole, and perforate it with a long hole, with a body, until all that is in it from matter is evacuated: and administer putrefaction of the place with medicines for several days. Then care for it with treatment for abscesses.
And indeed this Vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot, or in the foot itself: and it happens to have many openings, and a branch comes out from every opening.
Care for it therefore as we said in the division, and with that which has been said before.
Concerning the incision over Worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine disease.
This disease is named in a certain land among us bovine disease: because it often happens to oxen, and there is a small worm, which is born between the skin and flesh.
And it wanders in the whole body, ascending and descending: and it is shown to the senses during its locomotion of a limb to a limb, until it breaks wherever it breaks in the skin, and comes out.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. Just as worms and snakes happen, that is, earthworms, and roundworms in the belly.
And it is feared from its malice. Because when it itself wanders in the body, and rises to the head, and reaches a place: then perhaps it opens in it, and comes out, and destroys the eye: and that happens very often.
When therefore you wish to cure it, and extract it, then it is not done except at its locomotion, and appearance to the senses.
Therefore, it is necessary to tie what is above it and what is below it with a strong ligature constriction. Then cut above it and extract it.
But if it is buried deep in the flesh and you cannot find it, then place a cautery with fire upon the spot until it dies.
And the greater thing that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we said.
But if you see that it has already reached the head near the eye, then constrict the eyebrow well above it. Then cut above it and extract it.
And it is necessary that the weak person use cleansing of their body with medicines that dissolve foul and bad humors.
And let them beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the cutting for the ailment Nakir.
This ailment is called Nakir in our land, and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, and then it changes from limb to limb.
And I myself have indeed already seen it, according to what I tell you. I was summoned to a weak woman in certain boils. And she exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the brachioradialis muscle.
And after I remained for an hour, I saw one swelling advance with a focus, just as a worm advances, ascending to her shoulder faster than is possible: so that it was like molten silver when it runs from place to place.
Therefore the pain receded from that place and became fixed in her shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour, and it ran through the rest of the body until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it was revolving throughout her whole body, according to what I saw.
Therefore I have been amazed at the speed of its transfer from limb to limb. And for my part, before that I had never seen this ailment with my own eyes in such a manner. However, I have seen many who find pain transferred from limb to limb: and I did not see it myself with my eyes as I saw it in this woman.
And I do not think that this is so except because the woman was one of those from whom dry bodies with exposed veins are detected: therefore, this perceived windiness transferred here appears.
And it is necessary that they do not appear according to this reasoning in those who are delicate and with moist bodies, hidden veins.
When therefore you desire its cure and the patient feels that pain: then if it is apparent to you by eye, as we have said, then bind above it and below it quickly; and cut above it until that constricted windiness goes out; and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes: then treat it with shaking of the body and with that which expels windiness and drives them away with foul-smelling pills and pills from lizard's tail and similar things.
On the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their kinds and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is indeed because some of them have large points and small ones: and concave ones and blunt ones. And some of them have three angles. And some of them which have tongues. And some of them which have stars.
Those indeed which are according to the limbs in which they fall are according to two manners. For either the limbs are from the principal concave limbs such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and those similar to them. Therefore, when it falls into any of these members, and the signs of death appear to you, which I will tell you after, then it is necessary that you avoid the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in many dispositions. And when these evil signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it, and care for the wound.
For among the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the mouth and exits through the siphac which is above the brain, there is: because vehement soda and scotoma and vertigo and redness in the eyes and inflation and redness of the tongue and spasm and mixing of intellect and vomiting of cholera occur from it. And perhaps blood exits from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off and the voice is destroyed. And humidity white like poultice exits from the place of the wound. And a likeness of the aqueousness of flesh exits from it.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment and extract the arrow if it does not exit.
But the signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left nipple and he feels as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in a hollow thing. And perhaps there is motion of the arrow similar to the motion of the pulse and black blood runs from the wound. And coldness of the extremities follows, and cold sweat and fainting. Know therefore that death descends without doubt.
And the sign of an arrow when it has wounded the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound and the vessels which follow the neck become inflamed and the color of the sick person changes. And he breathes out a great, deep spirit and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If an arrow should fall into the membrane which is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs: and there is great panting with vehement pain, with a sigh: and all the members of the shoulders are moved.
And if an arrow should fall into the stomach, then perhaps some undigested food may come out of the wound.
And if an arrow should fall into the belly, and is fixed therein: some dung may come out of the wound, or phlegm, or intestine which has already been ruptured. Therefore there is no hope in its cure, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
And if an arrow should fall into the bladder, and urine should come out: and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger on the weak: then know that it is mortal.
But the remaining members, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and scapula, and armpit, and vertebrae of the back, and collarbone, and thigh, and leg, and those similar to these members are saved according to many dispositions: when the arrow does not find an artery, or a nerve: and the arrow is not poisoned.
And I tell you what I have seen concerning the being of these arrows, so that you may know through it concerning your cure.
Which is this: because to a certain man an arrow fell into the tear duct of his eye, at the root of the nose. Therefore I extracted it from him on the other side, under the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had fallen to him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already concealed, and I did not obtain anything from it except a small part of it, with which it adheres to its wood: and it was a large arrow from the arrows of Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no evil accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is one that has ears. I therefore cut above it between two guideges, and it was already submerged in his throat. I therefore proceeded carefully with it until I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly, and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed him, and nothing changed in his condition, I cut above the arrow, I devised a plan with regard to it, and extracted it; and he was healed, and no evil accident befell him.
And I saw a man to whom an arrow had fallen on his back, and the wound had become consolidated over it. After therefore seven years had passed, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow had fallen in her belly, and the wound had become consolidated over it, and the arrow remained inside her: and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman did not find harm from it in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow had fallen on his face: and the wound had become consolidated, and it remained, not finding him much pain; and similar things to these, many.
And I extracted an arrow from a man of the King's princes, which had fallen to him in the middle of his nose, and it had already inclined towards the right side a little; and the whole arrow was concealed.
I was therefore called for his treatment three days after the fall of the arrow. I therefore found the wound of the arrow very tight.
Therefore I examined it with a delicate probe, and did not feel it; and he himself was finding the puncture and pain under his right ear. Therefore I hoped that it was that puncture from the end of the arrow.
I therefore plastered the place with a plaster in which there was an attractive and digestive power, confident within myself that the place would become abscessed and the sign of the arrow would appear to me so that I could cut above it; and it did not happen in the place any accident indicating that the arrow had reached the place.
I therefore persisted with the plaster upon him for many days; and no accident happened, and the wound meanwhile became consolidated; and he remained weak from its extraction over a period of days, until he felt the arrow on a certain day inside his nose. And he related that to me.
And he related that to me. I therefore put upon that wound a sharp, corrosive medicine for many days, until it opened; and when I probed it, I felt the slender extremity of the arrow adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine until the extremity of the arrow appeared visibly to us; and it passed for me with him approximately a space of time of four months.
Then after the wound had been enlarged and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, and it did not obey its exit; then I did not cease to refine and contrive upon it with ways of ingenuity until I began upon it on a certain day with forceps neatly made, according to which their form will come at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And physicians indeed judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. I therefore restored it, and the wound became consolidated, and he was healed with complete health; nor did anything harm him at all.
And I tell you the quality of extracting certain arrows: so that you put that rule and meaning upon that which I did not say.
For the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with narration; nor does this book comprehend it, and a prudent craftsman compares with little upon much; and with that which is present, upon that which is absent; and he finds a new operation, and a new instrument at the descents of foreign matters of this art when they happen.
I say, therefore, that arrows do not depart from the limbs in which they are fixed except according to two ways. Or by attraction from the place from whence it entered. Or contrary to the other part.
And that which departs whence it entered, either is an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place; and it is attracted and departs.
And if it does not obey to exit at the hour in which it falls: then it is necessary that you dismiss it for several days until the flesh which is in its circumference putrefies; and the attraction of it is relieved, and its exit.
And similarly if it is fixed in bone, and does not obey you to exit, then dismiss it also for several days; and repeat it with attraction and motion every day until it departs.
And if it does not obey you to exit after days, then it is necessary that you pierce in the circumference of the arrow in the very bone from every part with a fine awl until the arrow is enlarged. Then attract it and draw it out.
But if the arrow is already hidden in the body and concealed from the senses, then seek it out with your hand. If you feel it, then draw it out with certain instruments that are suitable for its attraction.
But if you cannot get hold of it because of the tightness of the wound and the length of the arrow deep inside, and there is neither a bone nor a nerve nor a vein there, then cut above it until the wound is enlarged and it is possible to extract the arrow.
And if it has ears by which to hold it, then free the flesh fixed in them from every side with all the skill you can, and be ingenious, if it is not possible for the flesh to escape in the fracture of the ears and twisting them until it does escape.
And when you are performing the extraction of an arrow, wherever it may be, then use twisting of your hand with forceps on all sides until you make it come out, and with subtle finesse at the very end so that the arrow is not broken and its attraction and extraction become difficult for you.
And if you cannot get it out within an hour, then leave it for several days until what is around it becomes putrid. Then repeat the process, for then it will be relieved. But if a flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we said about treatment in its chapter and guard your strength from cutting a vein, nerve, or cord.
And administer skill in every way possible for the escape of the arrow, and let it be with ease and tranquility, or slowness and firmness as I have described to you.
And it is necessary that you make, when you draw out an arrow, that it be weak according to the shape in which it was at the time of the arrow wound itself. Because it is more beneficial. But if that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the shapes.
An arrow which departs from the opposite side, or has already proceeded from it something to the outside; or makes a hump at the end of the arrow by feeling from the upper part of the nearby skin, and you see it protruding: cut therefore above it, and let the incision be according to the amount that forceps can grasp in it.
Then draw it out; for it itself becomes easy for removal.
But if it is held fast in bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness until the arrow imprints in the bone and enlarges itself; then draw it out.
And if not: leave it for several days; then repeat it until it comes out.
But if the wood of the arrow is in it, then drive it in with that.
But if the wood has fallen out, and you wish to administer driving: then introduce a concave instrument to it so that its concavity enters the tail of the arrow; then drive it in with that.
But if the arrow is concave, then drive it in with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is relieved by that.
But if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you scoop out all the flesh in which the poison is: if that is possible for you. Then treat it with what suits that.
But if an arrow falls into the chest, or the belly, or the bladder, or the side; and it is nearby from what you touch with your hand, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and beware of cutting a vein, or nerve, and draw it out.
Then treat your wound, if you see it, and care for it until it heals.
And this is the form of forceps, with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be like a bird's beak, which are made like iron: when they grasp the arrow, they do not let it go.
And many kinds are made from them, large and small and medium, according to the size of the arrow, its slenderness, the width of the wound, and its tightness.
The shape of the impellent is concave.
And the shape of the deaf impellent.
This is a deaf extremity like a ray so that its entrance may be eased into the concave arrow and it is propelled with it.
Concerning the Phlebotomy of Veins.
Veins to be phlebotomized, whose course is customary in the body, are thirty.
Of these there are sixteen in the head: two pulsating veins which are behind the ears, called two Herbs; and two Arteries which appear in the two temples; and two Veins which are in the tear ducts of the two eyes, called Gazing; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein at the end of the nose. And two Guidegis which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
But the veins that are phlebotomized in the arms are five veins in each arm.
One of them is Cephalic and it is from the sylvan side; and the common people call it the vein of the head. And Brown. And it is the Middle vein and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic; and the common people call it the vein of the body. And Basilic, and it is placed on the domestic side; and it is also called Tittillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And Cord of the Arm, and it is placed above the fossa; and it is that in which one lets blood, and it is that which appears above the thumb with a manifest protrusion.
And Saluatella; and it is a vein which is between the annular and the little finger; and it has two branches.
And in the thigh and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curvature of the thumb from the sylvan side. And the second is Saphenous; and its location is at the heel from the domestic side. And the Nesse vein; and its location is at the heel from the sylvan side.
And in the other thigh there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning bloodletting of two veins behind the Ears.
Bloodletting indeed of the two veins which are behind the ears is beneficial for old catarrhs and hemiplegia and apoplexy and old bad ulcers of the head.
And the quality of bloodletting from them is according to what I relate. And it is that the head of the sick person be shaved; and his occiput be rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth with good rubbing. Then let the sick person bind his neck with a band until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed places on the head. Therefore inquire for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark there with a cautery.
Then take a lancet-type phlebotomy knife, which is called the Incidens. Then insert it under the vein in the skin until the phlebotomy knife reaches the mouth of the vein. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upwards, and cut into the vein and skin with an incidental incision. And let the length of the incision be the size of two joined fingers, or thereabouts; and let as much blood flow out as you wish.
Then bind it with lint, and let it go until it heals.
And now the incision of them has preceded at the beginning of the book, and their cauterization.
The phlebotomy of the two arteries which are in the two temples is beneficial for an old migraine: and chronic gout; and persistent ophthalmia: and a course of sharp, overflowing superfluities to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is as I tell you. Let the weak person bend their neck a lot until two veins appear clearly to the sense and their pulse is shown under your finger. Then mark them with a cautery.
Then raise the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger and insert the incidental phlebotomy knife underneath, and raise the vein upwards. And cut into it as you did with the other two veins; and let as much blood flow out according to your need.
Then release the constriction of the weak person, and place your finger on the vein for an hour. Then place a dressing and cotton on it, and bind it tightly above with a strong ligature; and leave it until it heals.
And the recollection of these has already preceded, and their incision and extraction at the beginning of the book.
The advantage of venesection of the frontal vein is after venesection of the cephalic vein, for old facial ailments: and for hemiplegia, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of that venesection is as I tell you. Let the sick person tie their neck with a band until the vein appears. Then take an instrument called a fossa.
Place the prominent point which is at the head of the fossa upon the vein itself and strike it with a comb, or some other thing in that manner; and let the blood flow according to the amount you wish. Then loosen the perforation of the sick person and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide venesection. However it should not be of an acute point like the other venesections, but rather of a wide point. And let the venesection be transverse; because the mouth is nearby and perhaps the venesection will break when it is thin.
The advantage of the two veins which are in the two lachrymals of the two eyes is for an eye afflicted with scabies and redness; and a sebaceous cyst in the eye, and facial ailments.
And the quality of their venesection is such that the sick person should tie their neck with a band. Then let blood-letting be done to them: and you shall stand above their head.
Let the blood-letting be made across the length, a little, with a small lancet, a little wide, for there is not much flesh in the place. For if the lancet is too fine, perhaps it will break.
Then let as much blood run out as is necessary for you. And put a compress over them, and bind them up at night with one binding, and undo them.
But blood-letting of the veins of the nose is fitting for acute fever and vehement soda and illnesses of the face, such as headache, and redness which happens in the face; especially when it is old.
And the quality of its blood-letting is that a weak person should constrict their own neck. Then hold their nose with your left hand, and take a fine, long lancet; and fix it in the middle of the hare itself, between that which separates the nose according to straightness. For the vein does not appear to the sense there, and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you hold your hand with the lancet for a little while; and let as much blood run out according to your need. Then bind it up at night with one binding. For it is restored quickly.
But the benefit of blood-letting of the two temples is for constriction of the breath and beginning of leprosy and melancholic illnesses and impetigo and bad ulcers and corroding ones, that is, summer boils.
The quality of their phlebotomy is such that the weak person should be strapped under both arms in his neck with a bandage; and the craftsman should stand above the head of the weak person, and the weak person should be sitting on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist makes a slightly wider cut in the vein according to the length of the desired phlebotomy. Then extract a temperate amount of blood, or according to that which you see is necessary for it. Then do the same from another vein.
Afterwards loosen the bandage and constrict two veins in the middle: so that the weak person does not suffer a relapse. And let him go until morning. For the wound will heal.
Concerning the Phlebotomy of the Veins by Albucasis Veins, however, are beneficial for phlebotomy according to Albucasis because they contribute to alcohol in the mouth and corruption of the gums; and bad ulcers; and fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers, even those that are in the nose and around it.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that you should make the weak person sit before you and constrict his neck with a band. Then turn back his lip and look at two veins; of which one is on the right side of the lip and the second on the left; and the demonstration of both is by the blackness of both. And this is true: because around them are small, black veins. Therefore, cut into both with an incidental incision.
But if there is doubt upon you and you do not know which of them it is: then attend to the incision of the larger of them and the more manifest.
And do the same with the two veins that are in the upper lip; and indeed, most customarily this is done; and cut into the two veins that are in the lower lip.
On Phlebotomy: Of the Vein under the Tongue after Phlebotomy from the Cephalic Vein. But the benefit of phlebotomy from both veins which are under the tongue is that phlebotomy from the Cephalic vein is for premonitions which are in the throat, and pain of the uvula, and pains of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomy from these is that the sick person should sit between your hands in front of the sun; and elevate their tongue, and look beneath the tongue on one side for a vein, and on the other side for a vein; and the color of both inclined towards blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize both; and be careful not to place your hand at the end in phlebotomizing them. For under them are arteries, and perhaps there may occur a flow of blood from those arteries.
But the three veins which are phlebotomized in the elbow, are those of which phlebotomy runs customary in all men.
And phlebotomy from them is done according to two ways. Either by puncturing with a myrtle phlebotomizing instrument broad; or olive-wood sharpened to subtlety; or by cutting with a phlebotomy knife, which is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from these are broad and slender kinds according to the width of the veins and their tightness. And it is suitable for opening concave veins, full, proceeding, apparent, large, and those which contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotomist is of lesser width, and more delicate extremities.
And it is suitable for phlebotomy of subtle veins, which contain subtle yellow blood
But the vein of Albucasis, which is one of these three veins, is a benefit for phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest, and belly; and it is suitable for the phlebotomist to be cautious with this vein, and to be fearful of it, for under it is an artery. Therefore if he errs, and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will strike that artery, and there will be a flow of blood. Therefore it is necessary for the phlebotomist not to pierce it with the phlebotomy lancet; rather, let him cut with an Alnesil.
But if the Basilica does not appear with manifest appearance, then it is necessary for you to seek another vein and change to another, or to search for a branch of it; or let the phlebotomist in place of it use the Vein of the Arm. For it itself is manifest, and cut it with a phlebotomy lancet, as we said.
But if you wish for the phlebotomy of the same, then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm, to touch the place until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark on the place with a cautery, afterwards constrict the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision with a phlebotomy lancet, as we said. And be careful that the percussion does not fall through the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, swelling in the place which you marked with a cautery, then that swelling is swelling of an artery; therefore avoid it.
And if you see blood rise during phlebotomy, like a child's urine, and it is thin, reddish blood; then know that it is from arterial blood. Then hasten, and place your finger upon it for a long time, then remove your finger. And if the blood is cut off (for it often is), then bind the arm and let it go; and let the weak person be cautious of its movement; and let there be fear upon him.
And let him not move it for several days until it heals.
But if the blood is not cut off and overcomes you and there is no medicine present to you at that moment, then cut into the artery if it appears to you. For its extremities contract and the blood is cut off.
Or take pistachio bark and grind it; take one half and bind it over the place of a vein constriction with a ligature and poultices until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not, then treat it with that of which previous mention was made from the place of powders cutting off the flow of blood; and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in many constitutions because of the small place of the wound and the possibility of a ligature from the arm.
The phlebotomy of the basilic vein is advantageous because it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; therefore, because it is composed of a branch of the basilica and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
And it befits the phlebotomist to fear from its phlebotomy, for under it is a nerve. Therefore, if he adds to the puncture in bloodletting and finds a nerve, there occurs numbness in it, and healing becomes difficult for it, and perhaps it does not heal completely.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. But if it is concealed in some people and is subtle and does not appear, then it is necessary that you place your bloodletting where the phlebotomist makes a section with Alnesil, and avoid the nerves with your strength.
But if a vein is between two nerves, then find the vein in length.
Concerning the Cephalic Vein. But bloodletting of the Cephalic vein is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and contributes to eye diseases.
And it is necessary in this vein especially, if you wish, that you puncture it with an olivewood phlebotome or a myrtlewood phlebotome because it is the safest of all veins, since there is neither an artery below nor a nerve.
However, it is fitting for you at bloodletting to avoid the head of a lizard only with the phlebotome and to seek a softer place: and it does not harm it if it is not found with the first percussion, so that you repeat upon it several times with the phlebotome.
However, perhaps it becomes inflamed in some people when not bloodlet in the first percussion, but that inflammation does not harm it at all.
Concerning the Benefit of Bloodletting and Precautions to be Observed in It. But the quality of bloodletting and its incidents from it, in whose correction you must think.
First of all, it is necessary for you to know that bloodletting is not administered except in the preservation of health and its continuance, and caution from the advent of diseases.
And for bloodletting to be done in one of three veins which are in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilica. And so that bloodletting be done at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, in truth, and in summer; and autumn, and winter after eating, when the signs of repletion appear.
And let bloodletting be done on the first day, or the third, after three hours have passed from the day.
Bloodletting which is administered in illnesses does not have a determined hour; but when suitability and necessity provoke it, at night or day, and in every hour, and at every time.
And it is not necessary that children be bloodlet until they reach fourteen years upon them. Nor let a pregnant woman be bloodlet except from the fourth month up to the seventh. Nor let old men who have already passed sixty years be bloodlet.
Therefore when someone wishes to be bloodlet for whatever reason it may be: it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels before bloodletting with a gentle enema if there is much retained dung within them so that the veins do not draw up harmful putrid superfluities from the intestines at bloodletting.
Nor let a patient who has indigestion, that is, sickening fullness, be bloodlet; nor one who is drunk, nor recovering from drunkenness until that be removed from them. Nor an invalid with dropsy.
And beware of bloodletting again after a colic attack, and vomiting, and diarrhea, and multiplication of intercourse, and labor, and exercise, and wakefulness, and fasting, and anything which dissolves the power from the bodily or animal thing.
Then consider in subtlety of humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if it is possible to do that.
Then let him enter a bath if nothing prohibits it; or exercise himself with some exertion so that the blood becomes thinner.
And perform bloodletting on his chest, around the third hour of the day, as we have said; and endeavor that his chest be evacuated that day from all animal evils: such as anguish, anger, and fear; and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and excessive intercourse, and those similar.
And let the things with whose use mankind has become accustomed from fragrance and smell and joy and similar things be present to his seat as much as possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit upon a cushion which is higher than the cushion of the patient; afterwards let him extract his arm and rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then let him tie a ligature with a tourniquet and wind it twice.
And let the constriction be temperate, for when the constriction is unequal or excessive, it then obstructs the course of blood; and also in relaxation it obstructs the course of blood.
Then after the constriction it is necessary that the phlebotomist rub his hands together until the veins swell and declare themselves to the senses.
Then let the phlebotomist cleanse the place to be cut with a little old oil.
Then let him place the index finger of his left hand upon the very vein below the place where he intends to perform the phlebotomy, slightly so that the vein does not flee and percussion occurs. For some veins are like a fleeing cord at phlebotomy and some are full of wind; and when you place the lancet upon them they contract under the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist and do not open; and if it does open, it is constricted. Therefore it is necessary that the phlebotomist be firm and attentive in all these things.
Then let him lay down the lancet. Therefore, if a vein is opened from one side only, it is good. And if not, then repeat that on the other side a little below or above it quickly: if the place does not swell up.
But if it swells up, or becomes weak and stretched, then let him go for a day or two: and let him not tighten the ligature. For perhaps it will draw a hot swelling. And let him not enter a bath.
Then let him repeat the phlebotomy, if he wishes.
But if the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly, and you fear that the quantity of blood you desire will not come out: then repeat the phlebotomy in the same hole according to its straightness, and add a little more in the operation.
And do this quickly before the place swells up, for a swollen place is frequent among many people during a small operation.
Therefore if you see it already swollen, then do not repeat it on top at all, for it will excuse nothing to you. And put something of oil feces over it: because it relieves the course of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer oil feces in all your phlebotomies, in which the phlebotomist intends to stop the course of blood; and they do this with tyriaca alfarach, and sagzenea, when one of them is placed on top of the place.
For blood becomes thinner and resolved when it is thick.
But if it happens that there is a large swelling in the place of phlebotomy, for it often happens, especially to him who is not a phlebotomist except from that side, or the opening of the vein is small, then hurry and put a sponge soaked in water and salt, slightly warmed, on top: and bind it for an hour: because it will dissolve.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has been let from the vein itself, with its complement, or from another vein.
But if anything of blackness or greenishness remains in the place after a few days, it does no harm; and if you wish, put on it something of aloe or myrrh dissolved: or something from the juice of mint, that is, calamint or something like it.
And very often indeed there occur abscesses and swellings at the phlebotomy in Basilicata.
Place then your hand upon it, and if you find softness under your pressure upon it, then that swelling is bad. Therefore be careful to put on it something of what we have said. For perhaps arterial blood will flow from it.
But make a plaster for it with that which has astringency, so that the place hardens. Then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you draw from every man blood according to the amount of his strength. And what appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow out until it reddens; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it flow out until it becomes thin; and similarly if it is sharp, then let it flow out until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting once a year, that the phlebotomy of the vein be enlarged and the incision wide; and in him who is weak, let it be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that you draw it in stages and that the opening be narrow; and better in phlebotomy of a vein, what is administered is a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from the flowing of blood and from the cutting of a nerve; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And for whom fainting is customary during bloodletting, it is necessary that he be given food before bloodletting with a small amount of bread soaked in pomegranate juice: or secaniabin if it is heated; and you should take from him three or four times the amount of blood.
And if his complexion is cold, then he should take bread soaked in syrup of rue, or in syrup of honey spiced with aromatics, or in aromatic fragrant syrup before bloodletting.
But if fainting occurs during bloodletting and the cause of it is excessive blood loss, then he should drink broth water and subtle fragrant syrup; and use aromatics with algae, and line his chest with them; and use the other remedies we mentioned in the chapter on fainting from evacuation.
But he who wishes to spare his arm and let out his blood a second time: it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is due to excessive evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he should gradually let out blood according to the amount of his strength on consecutive days.
But he who wishes to spare his arm and let out his blood a second time, and whose body is strong, then let him do it after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
But he who wishes to draw blood from his body to the opposite side from which it inclines: it is necessary that it be done to him on the second or third day.
But in whose body there is already much hot blood, and it is sharpened, and causes a fever to occur: it is necessary that the blood should leave from him at once, and a large amount should be drawn from him; and the opening should be enlarged until fainting occurs: after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and put your hand on his pulse at the course of the blood, so that death does not occur in place of fainting. It often happens that this occurs when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs.
And it is not necessary when you wish to release the arm and release blood a second time, when the opening of the vein is already closed and the flow of blood becomes difficult, to press down on it forcefully and quickly extend it. For that is very bad, rather let it alone until it is phlebotomized a second time.
Or remove with the sharpness of the phlebotomy that which is coagulated from the blood in the opening of the vein and place something of salt dissolved in water on top of it. And place something from tyriacal alfaroch or sagzenea on top of it and apply a soft compression to it until the blood comes out.
But if the vein is already inflamed, then let it be and do not bind it until the inflammation subsides.
But if necessity compels you to release blood a second time and there is no excuse then either phlebotomize above that place or in another arm in another vein.
The vein of the arm that the phlebotomist cuts in Fuscae and Basilicae when they are not found and are hidden because it is composed of them.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the sick person should insert their hand into warm water until the arm reddens and the vein appears clearly.
Then it should be bound above it with a ligament with slight constriction in the middle. Then make the phlebotomy across it gradually, not laterally, nor according to its length; and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be so that you phlebotomize it on the juncture of the hand a little.
But if the flow of blood is prohibited then immerse your hand again in warm water and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your need.
But if you are in the summer days, then you excuse yourself from repeating the hand in warm water. And indeed most of all the course of blood is put in warm water in the time of winter.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins because there is no pulsating vein beneath it nor nerve.
Phlebotomy however of the Saluatella vein of the right hand, brings relief to liver ailments.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that you bind the mahason hand with a ligament or your own hand after you have immersed it in warm water until the vein is inflamed and made very evident to the senses. Then phlebotomize it transversely. And if you should cut it totally it will not harm her in any way.
And preserve so that you do not touch your hand with phlebotomy. For beneath it are nerves of the fingers laid bare from flesh.
Then repeat the hand to warm water and let the blood run in it. For if you do not repeat it in warm water the blood clots in the orifice of the vein and is prohibited from its course.
When therefore you have drawn out the necessary quantity of blood then put oil and salt upon the vein so that it does not consolidate quickly.
And similarly it is fitting that you do with every strict branch.
The advantage however of its phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it brings relief to spleen ailments and similarly proceed in its phlebotomy as you did in the second equally.
The advantage however of phlebotomy of the Saphenae is for ailments which are in the lower part of the body such as ailments of the wombs and retention of menses and infirmities of kidneys and ulcers of the hips and thighs old and similar ailments from these.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is such that the sick person should immerse his foot in warm water and rub it until the veins protrude, and bind above the junction of the foot with a ligature. And indeed the location of the veins is near the heel, apparent towards the thumb, and from it many branches extend into the surface of the foot.
Therefore, the phlebotomy should be performed on a larger branch of it, or at the heel in its collection; for this is better and safer.
But if the phlebotomist performs it on the surface of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And make your incision transverse, as if you wished to cut it, and let the phlebotomy be Nesil.
But if the flow of blood is prevented, then immerse his foot again in warm water and let the blood run into it until it is evacuated.
But if the phlebotomist misses the vein in the first try, then repeat the phlebotomy a little way higher up, for this place is safe and you need not fear any harm from it, when you are careful of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the Saphena vein of the other foot equally.
But as we said, the location of the Sciatic veins is near the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of this phlebotomy is for pain in the ankle, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is such that the sick person should enter a bath and hurry; and bind his leg from the side of the ankle up to above the heel with four fingers, with a thin, long ligature; for it will not appear except because of this.
When it then appears, perform the phlebotomy in whatever way is possible for you, or transversely; and it is better, either to cut it with an incision or to divide it with a section. For its place is safer, and it itself is very hidden in many men.
For its place is safer, and it itself is very hidden in many men.
