But if you do not find it, and it does not appear to the senses at all: then let phlebotomy take some branch of it, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot, and towards the auricular: and beware of the nerves, and let it run from the blood a quantity as much as you wish.
Then loosen the constriction, and put leather over the place of phlebotomy, and bind the place. For it heals quickly.
Concerning Cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cups are made from horns, and from bronze, and from glass.
And cupping indeed is done according to two methods. With scarification, and extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. For it is either with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the places of the body where cupping is administered with scarification and extraction of blood are fourteen. Of which one is cupping Alnocrati, and it is the last of the head: and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck: and cuppings Alhagdahim, and they are two laminae of the neck from both sides at the same time: and cuppings of the menti, and it is under the jawbone in the lower part of the mouth: and cuppings of the two spatulas: and cuppings Alachosos above the end of the coccyx: and cuppings of the two focilium, and they are the middle of the two forearms: and cuppings of the two crura: and cuppings of the two caulae.
And cupping indeed does not extract blood from you from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore its virtue does not fall from them, as is the case with it from phlebotomy. Nor should a windy treatment be administered with or without fire in any diseases arising from repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity compels to windy treatment from illness or on account of custom, we administer it at any hour in the beginning of the month, at its end and in its middle, and at whatever time it may be.
This is because there are men who when blood multiplies in them until they need its extraction by windy treatment, find heaviness and headache in their head.
And among them are those who find repletion and redness in their face, head and neck. And among them are those who find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dulness in their eyes.
And some of them scratch the place of their windy treatment. And some of them have increased laughter. And some find the taste of blood in their mouth, or their tongue swells and discharges blood. And some have increased sleep. And some see blood and redness in their dreams, the dead, wounds and things similar to those.
Therefore when we see anything of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order the windy treatment to be done after two or three hours have passed from the day.
The benefit of a windy treatment on the nape is that it contributes to heaviness in the head and what flows to the eyes. But indeed it must be done after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this windy treatment is in place of cephalic bloodletting.
And it is not permitted for one with a cold complexion or who has catarrh to use it. For it brings great harm to him.
And for that reason, old men should not use them, and those in whose heads are cold illnesses.
But whoever is diligent over it, oblivion is generated upon him.
And for that reason it is necessary to command the one who uses the wind instrument to lay down his hand when the wind instrument is applied a short time to the lower parts, fearing from the generation of oblivion.
Cupping or cautery is in place of venesection of the Fusca and basilica vein, and for that reason it relieves asthma, constriction of breath, fissure of the instrument of breath, cough, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the cupping instrument or cautery be applied a short time, for if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Cupping with hagdahim relieves pains that occur in the head, ophthalmia, hemiplegia, perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth; and it is in place of venesection of the basilica vein.
And it is necessary that you command the one who uses cupping not to press his hand with scarification so that he does not strike an artery and cause a flow of blood.
Cupping under the chin relieves drunkenness in the mouth and corruption of the gums, and similar ailments that are in the mouth; and it stands in place of venesection of the alierich veins, which are in the two lips.
Cupping with spatulas relieves alchafach, which comes from repletion and heat.
Cupping of the interior parts with two cups relieves the benefit of venesection of the three veins, Basilica, Fusca, and Cephalica; for it draws blood from all those small veins that are in the flesh; and those small veins draw from other thicker veins until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper not to proceed much in the scarification; because the place is laid bare of flesh, and under it are nerves and arteries.
But the cupping glass which is applied over the fleshy parts brings relief to hemorrhoids of the anus, and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper that the cupping glass be large, and that it be of metal; because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps slender cupping glasses may break; and let it be scarified with a large scarification.
Cupping glasses diminish the legs with manifest reduction; because they draw blood from the whole body, and they bring relief to old pains in the kidneys, and wombs, and bladder; they provoke the menses, and they bring relief from cough and asthma; and they stand in place of two vein openings of the saphenous veins, and the alhorcobeni.
However they weaken the body greatly, and they cause fainting to occur in many people.
And the benefit of cupping the alhorcobeni is close to the benefit of cupping the two legs.
The quality of the position of cupping glasses is that you place the cupping glass first empty, and suck with temperate suction; and do not prolong the placement of the cupping glasses, but put them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that the humors may come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to repeat this and continue it until you see the place already reddened and swollen, and the redness of blood appears; then therefore scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the disposition of bodies.
For he who is of men of tender flesh, rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, no more; so that the place may not be ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper that he enlarge the scarification, and make it shallow; and that he temper the suction in ease, and speed, and gentle motion.
If there is swelling in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify it twice. In the first incision, a way is opened for the thinning of the blood and its wateriness; and in the second, for a careful extraction of thick blood.
But if the blood is very feculent, then let a third incision be made so that the last stage is reached.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually or a little, one incision is enough for us. But if we wish for a large extraction of blood, we make many incisions.
And if we determine that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to scarify with a deep incision; and the equal end in the incision is the depth of the skin.
What oil ought to be administered for ventosas and what precautions should the phlebotomist take?
Now, in the case of one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places for ventosas be anointed with opening, softening, and resolving oils; if it is summertime, such as oil of alkari or violet oil, or oil from sweet almonds, or oil from pumpkin seed.
And if it is wintertime, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or syrup of sarsparilla; and those similar to them.
But if the superfluity is thick and cold, then let the oil be bitter almond, or balm of Gilead oil, or white oil, or anise oil; and those similar to them.
But if the phlebotomist is dealing with a person who has open pores and delicate flesh, then it is necessary that oil be prohibited, and those people ought to wash the ventosa sites after cupping with rose water, or cold water, or sun water, or pumpkin water, or purslane water; and similar ones to them.
He whose blood is of much humidity should wash with vinegar, or myrtle water, and sumac, and similar things.
But he who has thick humors should wash his wounds with old wine, or samsuck water, or anise decoction, or chamomile, and similar things.
And it is necessary that dressing be done in a bath, or in the trace of a bath, but rather it is necessary that it be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary for anyone to sleep after dressing.
What should be regulated by cupping or phlebotomy before dressing?
And afterwards it is necessary that you consider first: then if cupping or phlebotomy be choleric and conquer upon his blood sharpness and inflammation: then it is necessary that he receive refrigerants such as pomegranate rind, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb, and similar things.
And make his foods chickens, and eggs boiled hard, and foods seasoned with sourness, and similar things.
And he whose constitution is cold should take in drink syrup of honey, or of almonds, or of alsecaniabin made from seeds: and let him take fragrant wine medium, which is between old and new: and it is ordered that he use a scarcity of food. And put its food, chicks and sparrows: and finches, that is, small birds: and dove chicks he should prepare.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than food: and it is necessary that it be given in the drink in some men in the hours tyriaca alfaroch, or a medicine from musk, that is, musk, or alesselithe before cupping: and before bloodletting, or after: so that the principal members are strengthened, and the blood is thinned. And it is not necessary for them to take it in heated drink.
Concerning Cupping Without Scarification. But cuppings which are without scarification are those which are placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gouty toes, and upon the belly, navel, and places of the kidneys, and the anchae part: because these members do not endure scarification upon themselves.
And it is not sought by them, unless either attraction from the blood of a member to a member: such as we place the cupping upon the nipples in the illness of a flow of blood from the nose. And we use them so that we loosen cold invisible windiness from a member into a member: such as we place the cupping upon the belly and navel. For it itself rarefies the member and heats it, and removes pain therefore because it dissolves that windiness.
It is also placed upon the kidneys when obstruction or a stone occurs in them. For by its power of attraction it perhaps opens the obstruction, or eradicates the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed upon the liver and spleen for relieving windiness in both of them.
And these cuppings are administered empty with section only. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes full of warm water are administered in pleural diseases: and that is, so that a cupping glass be filled, and be large, with hot water alone, or water in which herbs suitable have been boiled.
The form of a cupping glass administered with fire.
Let the width of its opening be two open fingers, according to what we have formed: and let its depth be half a palm: and let there be on its side in a place which is approximately its middle, a small hole, according to the amount that a needle itself enters into it; let it be made from brass together, or from citrine, with thick edges, smooth, equal, polished: so that it does not injure the limb upon its position above it: and let there be in its middle a crosswise rod from brass, or iron, where a candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, greater than that which we have described, and a little less than that, according to the illnesses and the age of he who uses it.
For cupping glasses for children, and weaklings are different, than cupping glasses for men, and thick bodies.
The manner of placing this cupping glass with fire upon a limb, is that you kindle tow with fire from linen decently, or a small candle from wax: and place it upon the middle of the crosswise rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass: so that the fire be ignited approximately at the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then let it be placed upon the limb: and a finger should be upon the hole which I have said, until when we hold the cupping glass: whenever we need to, let us remove the finger, and let vapor go out over that hole, and the cupping glass will be released immediately.
Then kindle tow according to its manner, and repeat it if you need it.
But the cupping glass which is administered in pleural disease with water, does not have a crosswise rod, nor a hole: and it is not administered except because it is filled with water, and is placed upon the limb only.
And this is its form.
And the larger this cupping glass is, so that it may hold much water, the better it is.
On the Suspension of Bloodletting Instruments.
Bloodletting instruments are not administered on many [parts of the body], except in those members where it is not possible to place cupping glasses, because of their small size, such as the lip and gum and things like them. Or on a member stripped bare of flesh, such as a finger and nose and things like them.
And the quality of using them is that you should come from bloodletting instruments which are in sweet, clean waters free from putrefaction.
Then they should be let go in sweet water day and night until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body should first be evacuated with bloodletting and cupping. Then wash the sick person's limb until it reddens. Then place them upon it.
Therefore when they are filled and fall off and it is possible to suck the place with a cupping glass, then that is the best outcome for healing; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then wash it thoroughly with much water and rub and squeeze it.
And if the flow of blood is prolonged after the leech falls off and it oozes, then infuse a linen cloth in cold water and place it on top until the oozing stops.
And if the blood multiplies then sprinkle powdered alum or gallnuts and things like them from astringents on top until the bleeding stops.
Or place bean poultices, peeled, on top and let them stay until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood will stop.
And it is necessary if a repetition of leeches is needed, whenever possible, to change them.
If bloodsuckers are prohibited from suspension, then let the place be washed with fresh blood. Or fix a needle in the places until something comes out of the blood. Then let them be placed, for they themselves when they sense something from the blood, adhere immediately.
Therefore when you wish them to fall off, sprinkle something ground from aloes, or salt, or ashes upon them, and they will immediately fall off.
This chapter is also very often necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know you sons that foolish physicians and the common people boast about this chapter who have never looked upon an ancient book nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason it happens that this method from the sciences is private in our land. And I have never encountered anything altogether good in it and have not obtained from it that which I have obtained except for the length of reading ancient books and study upon their understanding until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and the study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you in this chapter all that contains my knowledge and upon which my experiment passed after I shortened it for you and freed it from the occupation of elongation and shortened it with the end of abbreviation and showed it with the end of declination and formed within it many forms of instruments that are administered in it, when it is by addition of declination, just as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we shall say in the prologue of this chapter, the main points and chapters, to which the craftsman is first urged to attain understanding of their truth: namely, so that you, and those who are ready, may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when it happens to someone a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or fall: then it is necessary that you hasten first to phlebotomize him, and to loosen him, or both together: if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength: or he is a child, or decrepit old man, to whom something like this has happened: or it is a time of intense heat, or intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, and poultry and goat meats: and forbid wine, and thick meats, and filling up from foods and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are safe from a hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the sick person return to his first usual regimen with which he ran.
When therefore a broken bone begins to be restored, it is necessary that the sick person eats nourishing foods, much, thick, heavy, in which there is viscosity: such as food made from boiled wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and guts of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and things similar to them. For with this regimen coagulation of the fracture is faster and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in the veins which are in the first old age and decrepit ones, it is not possible that they continue and consolidate according to their own nature always, because of the dryness of their limbs: and those which are from them in the end of tenderness continue and consolidate.
Indeed nature attaches something similar to glue to a fractured bone on every side, in which there is thickness, so that it adheres and constricts it until they are joined together and are bound together, until finally it comes to strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its functions: and for this reason it is necessary that you place nourishment for the weak food in which there are gravity, viscosity, and thickness, as we said.
And know that fractures are of various kinds, according to the diversity of limbs: since a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the dorsal bone; and similarly all the other members are different from each other; and I have come with a recollection of every species of fracture exposed in its own Chapter distinguished from another. And the species of bone fractures are also diversified within themselves.
For it happens that its fracture is arched, besides which stars occur in it. And sometimes its fracture happens according to the length of the bone: and there are fractures of it that are splintered, that is, stars, and additions proceeding, such as are in a reed, when a knife is fitted for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head: and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to every species there is an ingenuity or proper instrument in its restoration, according to what will come to its recollection, distinguished in its places.
And from that by which a bone fracture is known, there is its crookedness and prominence: and the appearance of it to the sense and its sound when you press it with your hand.
Therefore when there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor a sound at your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the weak one find great pain; then there is no fracture there, but it is possible that there is crookedness or a small fissure: therefore it is not necessary that you move it with your hand and compression altogether, rather place upon it from medicines of which its recollection comes after that moment which suits the place: then let a fine bandage be applied.
And know that when a place arches and is split in two, except for what may happen to it in terms of boils, nevertheless all parts are removed from their companions: then you must begin at your hour to straighten it out, equalizing it before a hot abscess occurs to it.
But if a hot abscess occurs to it, then let it be for several days until the abscess is quiet. Then equalize it according to whatever method is possible from gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that its restoration and equalization is easier than bone, in which boils occur.
Then constrict it according to how the memory of it will come to you.
But if there are boils in the bone, then extension of this broken limb is necessary from two parts, if it is a hand or foot: or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two cords, or with a cord and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb upon an equal place, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the broken limb are extended: then try to make those additions return with all your ingenuity and according to whatever method you can in their places; and be careful according to your strength, lest pain or injury should occur to the weak one through your operation: and try according to your strength to apply one of the two bones with its companion, according to the better forms.
And it is necessary at that hour for you to try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, straighten it out and equalize it according to the amount of your strength.
And be careful of vehement extension and strong compression, as many stupid people do. Very often they cause a hot abscess or contraction to occur in the limb through their operation as we have already seen many times from their operations.
Then apply after equalization and decent operation and constriction of that member and quiet and tranquility and admonish the sick person about it. And make him beware lest he move it in the hour of his vigils and sleep at its turning and stirring and at evacuation and all its motions with the last strength. And let him beware that the position of the member not be in a position with which pain follows. For this is because when he feels pain or injury in the arrangement of the position of the member then it is necessary that he change it to another arrangement from which he does not feel pain.
And beware with him that his position be equal and straight so that crookedness may not occur in the member when it is restored.
And the quality of the constriction of the fractured member is according to what I relate.
Know that fractured members are differentiated in their smallness and their magnitude and their form.
Therefore those which are small from them, such as the arm and finger and calf and those similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft subtle cloth. But those which are large from them such as the hip and leg it is necessary that there be a wide, hard wrapping in them.
Because a broad bandage connects a large member and constricts it from every part with equal constriction so that a stain does not enter into it.
Then begin after your evacuation from equalization to place a suitable unguent on it in a soft pledget over the place of its fracture. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the place of its fracture with three or four wrappings according to what the member deserves and constrict your hand a little with the bandage.
Then go up with it to the part above the place of the fracture and constrict less than the constriction with which you constricted the fractured place. Then elongate it with inversion from the place of the fracture a little, and soften the stricture gradually, until you take a large thing from a healthy place.
Then take another ligature and apply it again over the fractured place with inversions. Then go with inversion to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the stricture of inversion, and its softening, according to what we said in the first upper inversion.
Then place between the inversions from soft lint, or cloth, with which the crookedness of the fracture may be equalized, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then apply another ligature over it. Then equal stars moderately made immediately over these inversions, if there is no swelling in the place nor a hot abscess.
But if there is swelling or an abscess in it: then place over it what settles that abscess and removes the swelling; and leave it for several days. Then bind stars over it then.
And let the stars be from halves of wide cane, planed, wisely formed; or they are from wood from which strainers are made, which are made from pine or terebinth, that is, palm branches, or calamus; and let the width of the whole star be according to this shape, and the same figure.
However, it is necessary that the star which is placed over the very traction be a little thicker and wider than the remaining stars; and the length of the star be according to what is allowed for the member by size and smallness.
Then bind over the stars with another ligature, according to the same first stricture. Then bind over it with threads moderately, according to what we said about the stricture; and it is that your stricture be greater over the place of the fracture; and the farther it is elongated from the fracture, let the stricture be smaller.
And it is necessary that threads be medium in thickness and fineness, and be made from soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of stupid people putting their threads from twisted flax cords, it is a great error: because constriction falls with them outside of quality.
And thin threads again are not suitable: for you do not achieve through constriction with them that which you desire.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger between star and star.
And if the weak one is injured through the extremities of the stars after constriction in healthy places: then place under them from soft tow, or carded wool, until nothing is injured from it.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and disruption occurs in the skin, I will come with a recollection of it in the second chapter singularity.
And know that not every fractured limb ought to be constricted with stars from the first day. For it is because when a limb is large, it is not necessary for you to place stars upon it except after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the event of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients used to restore, which are placed upon fracture and dislocation.
A Method of a common abbreviated plaster, restoring fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially for women and boys: because it is from it above which neither heat nor cold overcomes.
And it is that you should take from the powder of a mill, and it is the marrow of flour which hangs in the walls of the mill at the movement of the millstone: and make it as it is, except that you sift it with bran: and put its confection neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
Another Method for a Plaster, Restoring a Fracture, and Dislocation, and Torsion. Take almonds, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugwort: and the juice of all, each ten drachms; myrrh, and aloes of both kinds, each five drachms; and alethil twenty drachms; let all be ground, and sifted, and mixed with alethil water: or with the white of an egg, if the complexion of the sick person is heated. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and brings benefit to many people.
Another Method for a Plaster, which also Brings Benefit to Fractures and Torsion. Take mugwort, and mess, and white alteae, all ten drachms each; myrrh, and aloes of both kinds, each five drachms; acacia six drachms; Armenian bole twenty drachms; let all be ground with much grinding, and sifted, and made into a plaster with water, or the white of an egg: and apply.
Another Method for a Plaster for Joints, and Bones Separated from Their Places: and It Quiets the Pain Happening to Them, and a Crack in the Bone, and a Fracture. Take boiled wool, and submerge it in vinegar, and boiled oil; and place it upon the area.
And truly this plaster has no power of restoration, but it is good for quieting a hot abscess: and properly expels pains.
Another Method for a Plaster, Which Restores a Broken Bone. Take leaves of the deaf fig, and leaves of wild poppy; and grind all, and make a plaster from both.
Another Method for a Shortened Plaster, Applied in the Restoration of a Fracture of a Bone: and When You Wish the Resolution of a Remaining Abscess. Take root of altaea, and chamomile, and flowers of violets, and herb flour, all one part each; grind all, and make them into a plaster with wine, if the limb is not heated.
If it is heated, then prepare it with coriander water or with water and use it.
Method of another stronger plaster for dissolving, than this first, when a hard abscess occurs during the restoration of a limb. Take of the root of altaea, and flaxseed, and balaustium, and melilot, and samsuck, and the flower of violets, and chamomile, all an equal part, grind them all together, and prepare them with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. The whole of it according to the heat of the limb, and the rest of its heat.
However Hippocrates did not say in his book that a plaster should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration unless it was made from wax and oil: nothing else; and he narrated that it should be a medium between thickness and subtlety.
But Galen thought that things should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration in which there is a drying of heat, such as myrrh and aloe and frankincense, and similar ones to them from those which we have narrated.
Method of a Plaster Which Brings Together Injury and Pain. Take of mugwort and chickpea and human hair cut off, or bird feathers, and altaea and salt equal parts; grind them and sift them and prepare them; and make a plaster from it.
But the quantity according to which it is necessary for the ligature to remain, then be dissolved, is that you consider then if neither faint pain nor itching occurs, nor does the fractured bone move from its place; then do not dissolve it in many days. But if violent itching occurs to it in the place, or restless pain, or swelling; then hurry and dissolve it in an hour, and remove the plaster from it; and take a soft cloth, or a soft damp sea sponge: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain settles.
But if violent itching occurs to it in the place, or restless pain, or swelling; then hurry and dissolve it in an hour, and remove the plaster from it; and take a soft cloth, or a soft damp sea sponge: and immerse it in lukewarm water, and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain settles. Then let the limb rest for an hour. Then place upon it wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil; and bind it on for a night until you are secure from hot abscess and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then apply it to a delicate strapping and a small plaster; and do not constrict it with your first binding, but delicately with it until it is healed.
And if you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all accidents are now entirely removed, and it no longer needs plaster and strapping, then apply it just as you did at first equally.
But if none of those things which we have said happen to the limb, then do not unbind it except after three, four, five, or seven days. And when it is released entirely after twenty days according to what appears to you from the unbinding of the limb as we have said, until when the fracture adheres and coagulation of flesh approaches it: add then even in binding more than your whole first binding; and add also in the increase of food for the weak, according to its preceding recollection.
But if you see the place of the fracture already drying out and weakening more than it ought to: then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore embrocate it with warm water at every time you unbind it, and let it be so for three days; and ease the binding a little.
By this operation, nourishment flows to the limb and heals quickly.
What foolish restorers do from a fracture of the limb with other juices when it is not restored at first according to what is necessary and restored according to crookedness is an error from their operation and great fear; and if it were right, the ancients would have said so in their books and operated accordingly.
And I have not seen any trace of them there at all, and it is better that it not be done.
On the making of an accidental wound on the head.
There are several types of fractures and many shapes of them, and their causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture that occurs from a blow with a sword and the blow removes the whole bone until it reaches the dura mater, which is beneath the bone, just as an axe does to wood; and because of this, this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or a sword strikes a certain part of the bone and strikes its surface and the incision does not penetrate to its end; and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Elevation.
And a wound may be of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is comminution or attrition; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone, or things similar to them.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the periosteum, which is beneath the bone; or it is on the surface of the bone: and a wound from these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone to the subtlety of a hair; and it is a small fissure and because of this, this type of fracture is called Capillary.
And from these is a fracture that occurs from a fall or a blow from a stone and things similar to them which cause a plate of bone to enter inwards and a cavity is formed; just as it happens to caldrons of air when they are struck by a blow and a part of them enters inwards.
And this indeed most often occurs in the heads of soft bones, such as the heads of children.
And all these types of fracture are made distinct and separate, and I will come with the remembrance of it all in its place.
And we recognize all these kinds of fractures through examination upon them, and inquiry into them with probes, and removal of corrupted flesh which is upon them.
A capillary fracture, however, is recognized by this, that it is detected upon the bone, and is removed and smoothed over with a cautery: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the sick person: then if you see from the accidents that it signifies with manifest indication fear, such as vomiting, cholera, extension and loss of intellect, and cessation of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and things similar to these in many constitutions, without a doubt it will not be healed: and if you see accidents that are not terrifying to you, and you hope for safety: then begin its treatment.
Which is this, if a wounded person comes to you first when he is wounded: and it is this in the winter months, then it is necessary that you endeavor in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every constitution.
And if it is in the summer months, then it is necessary that you hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is beneath the bone from the subcutaneous tissue becomes corrupted, and those accidents occur which we have described.
And if the fracture of a bone has already reached the subcutaneous tissue touching the brain, and it is with small fragments and traction: then it is necessary that you cut into the crushed and abraded part, according to what I tell you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded person: and expose the bone according to any method possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light upon the sick person.
And if it should happen to you upon the exposure of the bone a flow of blood, or a hot abscess: then oppose it with what is necessary. And it is, that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil; and make the wound rest, until the pus subsides, and you are safe from the flow of blood. Then begin in the cure of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is, that you cut the bone with a sharp-pointed chisel; and this is its form.
Then apply another chisel after the first chisel somewhat wider; and this is its form.
Then apply yet another chisel wider even than the second.
And the second method is, that you have a number of diverse chisels; some of which are wider than others, and some of them shorter than others; and they are at last sharp at their extremities, and are made of steel or iron; and use ease in striking upon the chisel so as not to jolt the head and injure it.
But if the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary that you pierce around its circumference before you apply chisels, with awls which are called non-penetrating awls; and they are not called thus except because they do not pass through the termination of the bone into the flesh beyond it; therefore, because the awl has a round end above what is beneath its sharp point, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents submerging and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take a large number of these awls, each of which suits the quantity of thickness of the bone, until you have present for every skull an awl; whose sharp end's quantity in length and shortness be according to the quantity of thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three species of awls, or trepans.
The quality of piercing around a fractured bone is: that you place the awl upon the bone and revolve it with your fingers until you know the bone has already been penetrated. Then the saw should be moved to another place and put what is between all the holes the size of the radius or about that.
Then cut with the incisors what is between all the holes from the bone and make it thinner than you can on that from the end of lightness as we said until you eradicate the bone either with your hand or with some other instruments which you have prepared for it such as pincers and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you are careful with all caution lest the saw or the incisors touch anything from the dura mater.
When therefore you have gone around the bone perforating and it has been separated from the dura mater if it was continuous with it and you have extracted it it is necessary that you scrape and make equal the extremity of it which remains in the bone with another instrument similar to the incisors. However it is necessary that it be thinner and more subtle than the other incisors.
And if anything remains from small bones and splinters then take those with ease with what you have from your instruments. Then care for the wound after that with linens and ointments which we said.
And another method of the operation is easy altogether far from fear which Galen said and praised it with great praise and this is his word. It is necessary for you first to begin to uncover the part of the bone from the place in which the fracture is more violent and more manifest until when you have uncovered that part and placed under it the extremity of this lentil-shaped incisor which has this shape.
And the lentil-shaped part gentle does not cut anything and the sharp part of it on its two sides extending in length as you see so that the lentil-shaped part is fixed to the dura mater with its face incident acute in the bone.
Then strike upon the incisor from one part with a small mallet until you cut through the whole bone with ease as it revolves and you are safe from the fatty tissue that no accident may happen to it which you fear altogether.
And if the surgeon were not more foolish than men and ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the fatty tissue in certain places, he would cut it away with the extremity of the lenticular knife, and free it easily. For it is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate as far as the fatty tissue, and that which is cut from the bone is its surface, and roughness and subtle spines remain in it; then it is necessary that you shave off that roughness, and root out those spines with subtle razors from which you have prepared a number of various sizes, so that it will be possible for you to administer from them in every place the more suitable ones, and the straighter ones, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone itself, and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer in your reason, by which you first shave the bone, the wider of those razors. Then use a more subtle one after it.
And it is necessary that you administer in your reason, by which you first shave the bone, the wider of those razors. Then use a more subtle one after it. And do not cease doing that according to continuity, until you arrive at administering the thinnest and most subtle of them all.
But in every one of the remaining small capillary fissures and subtle fractures, it is necessary that you administer care according to that which leads to its correction.
And it is a thing which is not hidden from anyone who has any study at all in this art, and has stood upon that which we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the fatty tissue which is uncovered is retained when you root out the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth according to the size of the wound and submerge it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth doubled or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil and place it over the first cloth; and do that as easily as you can over it so that it does not weigh down the wound.
Then apply a wide bandage over it; and do not bind it tightly except by the amount that will hold the wound only; and leave it thus with this care for one day or two days until you are secure from hot abscess. Then unbind it and apply afterwards medicines with which there is superfluous dryness: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and aristolochia, and the like; and let it be that you make a powder from these medicines, which shall be powdered over the wound as they are dry.
And generally apply to it every medicine of the quality which is to cleanse and not bite. And be careful according to your ability that the wound be clean in your care; and let there not be filth of ointment nor dirtiness in it.
And be careful according to your ability that the wound be clean in your care; and let there not be filth of ointment nor dirtiness in it. And do not allow the pus to collect in it at all; for when pus collects upon the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and a great harm befalls the sick afterwards; and now it happens in the skull, when it is cleansed from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore consider then, if these accidents which we have mentioned happen to the sick man, know that it is undoubtedly pernicious.
And if the blackness does not happen except from the medicine which is put upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to make that blackness; then it is necessary that you take one part from honey, three parts from rose oil, and saturate cloths with them. Then put it upon the skull; afterwards take care of it with suitable kinds of treatment until it is healed.
On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except its two upper parts together, or one of them; for it has two bones, because the lower part is cartilaginous and is not broken, and nothing happens to it except attrition, twisting, and the like.
If one of its two parts is broken, then you must insert your little finger into the nostril and equalize that fracture with your index finger, and push it outward with your thumb, until the nose returns to its natural shape; and this should be done skillfully and easily. And take care that during your operation that pain does not fall upon the weak one.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and your finger does not reach it, then it is necessary that you equalize it with the extremity of a radius, in which there is a small thickness.
But if the fracture is in two parts, then do as described for one: and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day at rest apply a warm poultice.
Then insert into the extremity of the nose a linen wick if the fracture is in one part; or insert two wicks if the fracture is in two parts; and let the thickness in the wick be of such quantity that it fills the nostril.
And some ancient restorers have said to infuse the wicks in butter and change them every day. However, that does not seem right to me; rather, it is necessary that the wick be infused in albumen or egg white mixed with a powder of millstone grit. Then leave the wick until the mouth becomes firm and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is introduced into the nose in place of the wick after it is covered with a soft cloth; and it happens that it holds the fracture of the nose more strongly and so that the weak person is not prevented from breathing.
And this thing is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, do wicks.
But if it should happen that the nose breaks again while you are working, apply a poultice to the nose with carrot, or with quince infused in rose oil, or a small amount of diascordium.
But if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that poultices be made with sesame flour and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. Then place a soft pledget on top of it, and do not bind the nose at all.
But if the bones are broken into a small fracture or are crushed, then it is necessary to make an incision above them and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them. Then suture the incision and treat it with what heals and strengthens it with suitable poultices or ointments.
But if a wound should occur between the nostrils, then it is necessary that you treat it with lint and insert a lead tube until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw when it is fractured.
When the lower jaw is fractured and the fracture is not with a wound, then observe if its fracture is only external and it is not broken into two and curved inwards; then recognition of it is easy.
Therefore, it is necessary that if its fracture is on the right side, you insert the index finger of your left hand into the mouth of the sick person; similarly, if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and push the gibbosity of the fracture inwards with ease to the outside with your other hand from the inner part of the bone, and equalize it with it.
But if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you apply extension from both sides, according to straightness; until its equalization is possible.
But if there already occurs concussion or disruption in the teeth, then bind those of which you hope that they will remain with gold wire, silver, or silk thread. Then place a cerecloth over the fractured jaw. Then place upon it a doubled cloth, and upon the cloth a large star carefully made; or a piece of skin equal to a sole, in length to the mandible.
Then bind it from above, according to how its ligature is prepared for you, and it suits its application, until it is not destroyed; and instruct the infirm person to be in tranquility and quietude; and have their food given them as gentle drinks.
But if you judge that something is already changing in some way: then hurry and untie it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered from it, and plaster it with powder of millennian, with albumen or with sesame flour, after you have removed the crust; and put soft wool on top of the plaster.
Therefore while that plaster remains attached to it, and the bone's position is not altered, leave it alone, do not untie it, until it is healed; or bind the fracture. Very often this fracture is bound for three weeks.
If it should happen that a hot abscess occurs in the meantime, then administer what we have said many times for its relief, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a piece has already separated from the bone, or from the periosteum: then carefully perform this so that you remove those fragments of periosteum with what suits you for their removal from instruments.
But if the wound's opening is narrow, then enlarge it with a phlebotomy according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those fragments of periosteum, and nothing remains from them, sew the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, place one of the suitable plasters upon it so that it heals until it is cured.
On setting a fractured clavicle.
The clavicle is most often broken from the anterior part near the shoulder, and the fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Either it is broken and separated into two pieces, with or without splintering; this type is easier to set.
Or, splintering occurs within the bone; this type is more difficult to set.
Or, there is a fracture with a wound.
When the fracture is without a wound, the operation is performed as follows: have two assistants present, one to hold the fractured clavicle in place and the other to extend the neck towards the affected side.
Then, straighten the fracture with your fingers until it resumes its proper shape; there should be neither protrusion nor indentation.
If further extension is needed, place a sphere of cloth or wool under the patient's shoulder blade; its size should be appropriate to the situation. Extend and elevate the clavicle and compress the sphere with your hand until the fracture is aligned correctly.
If you cannot draw the end of the clavicle outward because it has sunk deeply, have the patient lie on their back and place a medium-sized pillow under their shoulder; have an assistant press down on their shoulder until the sunken portion of the clavicle is raised upward. Then straighten the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If you feel splinters from the clavicle moving, cut them open and carefully remove the splinters.
If the splinter is embedded in the bone, make an incision with a prepared chisel, after you have placed an instrument under the clavicle to hold it steady; this instrument can be made of wood or metal. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon which has no concavity.
And let its width be according to that by which it does not fit into the bone from the size of it and its equality. Its length, however, also according to what is possible for you for the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
But if the disruption of the wound which you have opened when you extracted the shattered bone is wide, and you are secure from hot inflammation: then join two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the disruption is small, or you fear hot inflammation: then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the amount of the fissure of the wound.
And if it should happen that hot inflammation occurs, then infuse a cloth in rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and place it upon it.
But when the stricture of the bone is without wound, and without incision, it is, that you place upon it a plaster made from mill flour with egg white. And place a soft wad upon it. Then place a sphere under its nipple, if you need it. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long: and let its width be one palm, or thereabouts. Then place doubled lint upon the wad and plaster. Then take a splint from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the splint in the place of the himematem which falls upon the fracture and the place. Then place the himematem upon the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it upon its neck, and under its sound nipple, and under its unsound nipple: and reduce it over every part, and in the way that you see that the stricture holds the fracture with decent stricture: and this is that which is not concealed above you, and all of it, so that the splint is not removed from above the fractured bone. And for this reason it is necessary that you look at the sick man every day. Therefore, whenever the ligament is softened and you see that the star is already being removed, then straighten it and tighten the ligature.
Then place the sick man's sleep upon his back and put under his buttock at his place of sleep a little cushion at night so that it may rise with him as an aid from his side. The fracture of the clavicle is therefore raised by elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck; and do not unbind the ligature unless it should happen in a place affected by itching, or from an abscess, up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face toward it. And add to the constriction and dismiss him until he is restored and coagulated.
And a fracture of the clavicle indeed is bound and strengthened according to most within twenty-eight days, and sometimes it happens within a lesser time in some men.
On the restoration of a fracture of the scapula.
The scapula is rarely fractured and in its broad part; and its margins are not broken from it unless.
When therefore a place is broken from it and broken in its middle and it is not recognized except by touch; then according to what the fracture's shape is, try its preparation. And reduce it upon the natural form in every way possible for your ingenuity.
Then place over the area powdered starch with egg white, and a soft pledget, and put over it a cushion from doubled cloth. Then place over it a wide star from a thin board according to the quantity of the whole scapula, or a little wider.
But if there is indeed a concavity under the star in any place of the scapula, then equalize that concavity with a soft pledget until the star descends according to evenness.
Then bind over it with a long strip of linen, with decent constriction and the firmness of a star, until it does not move from its place.
And consider the ligature every day. Therefore whenever the ligature becomes softened, bind it again and equalize the star if it moves from its place; and let the sick man lie on his sound side.
And the spatula indeed is restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then untie the ligature; and you will be in safety. For it is from bones whose union is not feared, nor their loosening.
But if a piece proceeds from the bone and pricks under the skin: then split above it and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the clavicle from the calming of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
On the restoration of a fracture of the breastbone.
The chest is sometimes broken in its middle, and that rarely happens. Its extremities indeed are most often broken and crushed from its accidents.
When its middle is broken, which inclines towards the lower parts, and it happens to him a violent pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps he vomits blood, and a concavity is shown in the fractured bone, and is not concealed from the sense, Its restoration is, that the sick man be laid on his back; and put between his spatulas poultice.
Then press his shoulders and join the ribs from both sides or flanks with your hands; and with subtlety in its equalization, according to every way as possible and it suits, until the shape of the bone returns, according to what is necessary.
Then put plaster and a wad on top of it, and place above it a star from a thin board of willow, or hazel, and those similar in lightness, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then carefully bind it over the fractured bone so that it does not move; and go around with the bandage according to its roundness on the back several times, and tighten it with a decent constriction.
Then consider the bandage every hour, and whenever it becomes loose, tighten it; and if its need provokes its loosening at itching, or corrosion that occurs in the place, or pain, or an abscess: then hurry, and undo it, and remove the plaster, and correct what happened with the method of its treatment.
Then apply a plaster, if you see it fitting for that: and bind it with a constriction until it is healed.
On the restoration of ribs, when they are broken.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur except in thick places which follow the back, and from their extremities on the front side. And attrition does not happen to them unless because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from the sense in inquiry with fingers.
And its restoration is done thus, that you equalize the fracture with your fingers according to the possible way, until the shape is equalized, according to what is fitting. Then apply plasters and bind the fractured bone with splints if it needs that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inwards: then there occurs a violent pain and stabbing, like that stabbing which occurs to one who has pleurisy; because the bone stabs the membrane, and also difficulty in breathing occurs to him, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have already been ingenious in it with many ingenuities: and among them are those who say that you should give the sick person foods which generate inflammation and windiness so that the belly becomes inflamed and extends and pushes the fracture outwards.
We, however, shrink from making a hot abscess quickly, unless it should happen. But if it already happens, then it adds to it.
And some of them said: let it be placed over the windy place, then let it be sucked with strength, and it is similar reasoning. However, it is feared that the windy one attracts superfluities to the place, because of a weakened disposition.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been soaked in warm oil; and let poultices be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that there be an equal ligature when you encircle it according to its roundness.
Then let the weak one be treated for pleurisy with food and medicine.
But if a violent thing constricts the weak one, which it does not tolerate, and pierces the veil with a stinging puncture, and we fear for the weak one; then it is necessary that we should cleave above the place and expose it from the broken rib. Then let us place upon it an instrument that preserves the suture, of which remembrance preceded: and let us pierce the mouth with ease, and pierce it itself.
Then let us join the two lips of the wound, if it is large with a suture; and let us treat it with plasters until it heals.
But if a hot abscess should happen meanwhile, then hasten and infuse poultices in rose oil, and put them upon the place; and let the weak one be treated with what suits the abscess.
Meanwhile, also let it be propped up on the side on which it sleeps until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured vertebra of the back and neck.
Fracture indeed sometimes befalls the bones of the neck, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly to the vertebrae of the back also. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not: then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and benumbed, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor constrict them; and when you constrict them with your fingers, and prick them with a sharp point, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and is mortal in most cases.
But if he moves them, and feels constriction in them with fingers and pricking: then know that the nape of the bone is safe, and that the weak person will be healed with treatment.
But if it happens to the vertebrae of the back similar to that, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not: then consider his feet: then if you see that they are softened, and what happens in them happens in the hands: then when he lies on his back, wind passes and defecation occurs without volition: and when he is turned onto his stomach, defecation occurs without volition: and when he is turned onto his back again, and wishes to urinate, but cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in its treatment.
But if none of that happens to him, the matter is lighter.
And the treatment of what happens to him is that you try a poultice of hot fomentation: so that you put oil of roses alone on the affected vertebra: or with boiled eggs.
Put that on it several times a day until the hot swelling subsides: then put one of the strengthening and drying plasters on the place, and bind it with a bandage: and advise the sick person to use quiet and tranquility. And let him not sleep on the side where he finds pain until he is healed.
If it happens at the abrasion of a fractured bone, or something that is already separated from it: then it is necessary, so that you make an incision above the skin, and remove that. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with suture. Then care for it with some flesh-producing substance, from ointments, until it heals.
But if the last bone of the haunch is broken, and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary, so that you insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and equalize the broken bone with the other hand, as much as possible, and in a second equalization.
Then place something over it, and remove it: and care for the wound with the healing that preceded, until it heals.
On the restoration of the anus.
Chapter XI.
Rarely are the bones of the anus broken. But if they are broken, then the fracture of them is not, except a comminution at their extremities: and they split lengthwise, and incline towards the interior: and there occurs a feeble pain in the place, and pricking: and the leg that is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration indeed is, so that you extend your hand over it, until you stand above the fracture in the manner of its shape.
But if the fracture is only at its extremities, then equalize that fracture according to what is prepared for you from equalization, until it resembles its natural shape.
But if the fracture is lengthwise, or has already inclined inwards: then make the weakling lie on its stomach, until preparation for the restoration of that fracture is made for you. When therefore you have equalized it, place a plaster over it: then place an splint made from wood, or from hide, over it: and bind a bandage over which you do not fear the displacement of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And he should make the hollows equal to that which will fill them, until it is grasped by a constriction according to equality; and he should order the sick person to sleep on his back, or on his sound side.
But if a hot abscess should happen to it: then cease from its extension and its restoration, until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it what subsided, according to what preceded, then return to its restoration; and constrict it as is necessary.
But if a fracture or a shattering occurs in the bone, extract it.
On the restoration of a fracture of the Collarbone.
The Collarbone is that which is between the Elbow and the Head of the Shoulder Blade.
Therefore if it is broken, its restoration is according to one of two ways.
One of which is: that you should take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form which follows.
And two bandages should be tied at its two ends.
Then it should be suspended from an elevated place, and the sick person should sit on a seat. Then he should project his broken arm onto the wood, until it reaches its nipple, at which point the middle of the curve of the wood is attached.
Then suspend something heavy from above it, so that it extends itself downward. Then the physician should equalize the fracture with his hands at the same time, until the fracture returns according to what is necessary.
And the other method is that the sick person should be laid on his back; and suspend his hand from his neck with a bandage. Then order two assistants, that one of them should hold that which is above the fracture with his hands; and that the other should hold below it, and each should extend towards his own side.
And if you wish for there to be stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with a ligature, and above it with another ligature; and let each of the two assistants extend them to their two parts.
Therefore if the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, it is necessary that one ligature be placed below the acromion, and another below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a ligature be placed above that location, and above the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease and without labor, until when the fracture is equalized according to what is necessary, and a good dressing is applied; then bind it, if there does not occur a hot abscess.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then loosen the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it wool cut and soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides; then bind it; and the method of binding it is that you put a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a wrapping from new cloth over the plaster. Then let him bring his arm up onto the supporting aid and place his hand open upon his shoulder.
And place a cloth and bind it over the supporting arm so that the arm stands in the place of the stars; if nothing prevents you from this, nor does anything change upon you from the broken bone.
But if you fear that something may change upon you from the broken bone, then use a splint; and it is that you place upon the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place a finger's breadth between every splint; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind over the splints with a binding such as I have described at the beginning of the chapter.
And let your bandage be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the longer the fracture is, let the bandage be less tight.
Therefore if you see the arrangement of the stars and the bandage, as we have said, in the hour that you restore the limb immediately do this: and if you fear a hot abscess then loosen the bandage and the splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the bandage every three days so that itching or swelling does not occur in the place, or food be prevented from reaching the limb because of the excessive tightness of the bandage. Therefore correct all of it according to what we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it then do not loosen the bandage except after three days or more.
And let the sick man sleep on his back and his hand on his stomach; and place under him a bolster full of wool evenly.
And consider every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb is not loosened or the bandage softened. Therefore correct all of it with your power.
And give the sick man food according to the order we have previously prescribed so that it is delicate food at first until his mouth begins to tighten then it is necessary to increase his food.
For by custom the helper and leg tighten for forty days; therefore it is necessary that it be loosened and a bath be given and treated with ointments that correct it.
But if the fracture is foul and crushed then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it until fifty days and up to two months.
Concerning the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called two humeri.
One of them is small and it is that which follows the thumb. The other is large and it is that which is placed below the small one from the lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger bone is broken, or perhaps the smaller one; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the smaller upper bone is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower bone is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing difficult.
And worse it is when the two bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone which is broken is the smaller upper one, then it suits the doctor at its restoration to put a small extension with ease until it equals it.
If however the larger bone is that which is broken: then it is necessary that he put a stronger strapping greatly.
And it is necessary that he put the figure of the hand at its restoration, and its extended extension upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher towards the superiors than all the fingers; and let the lower joint be lower than all the fingers, or the rest; and let the weak one sitting squarely upon himself, and let the cushion be before him, or in his straight line, at a height so that the weak one does not incur labor.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, or with his hand, or with a bandage. And let another attendant extend similarly from above.
Then let the doctor equalize the bone until he reduces it according to the best figure as he can.
Which if there are splinters in the fracture of the bone, then try to reduce every fragment into its place with your strength.
If however a separated splinter appears in it and pricks the skin, and there is no hope for you in its restoration, then cut above it, and remove it according to the way which we said in those things which were mentioned before.
If there is a fracture with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for it, therefore take its treatment from there.
If a hot abscess should occur at the beginning of your restoration, then make a linen cloth with cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium in thickness between thinness and thickness; and bind the cloth over it with a fine bandage until the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate and apply a plaster prepared from millstone powder with egg white. Then put on splints, and let the splint that is placed over the fracture itself be a little wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements: whether there be a fracture in one focus or in two foci at the same time.
Then place your bandage over the place of fracture stronger and tighter, and somewhat more so with a bandage above the fracture or below it; or place a softer bandage a little less tight than the previous one according to the previous recollection of it at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture be soft, damp cloths; and let them not be hard or rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of flax properly, medium between fine and coarse as we have described.
And consider the limb and bandage after a few days. If something should happen which requires correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb: then it is necessary to embrocate the limb with warm water until that itching subsides and let the limb be unbound until it quiets, then repeat the bandage.
If the bandage has already become softened and the bone already moved and similar things: then correct all that with your skill and consider it again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tightness of the bandage, then it is necessary to loosen it a little and let it be for several days until nourishment flows to it, then bind it again.
If, however, none of these things which we have mentioned happen to the sick person, then it is not necessary that he be untied, except after twenty days, or thereabouts.
Then let the hand of the sick person be suspended at his neck. And let his neck be equal, and let it be guarded by its own strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days; and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and the dispositions of strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the hand.
Know that fractures of the bones of the hand and the nodes of the fingers rarely occur. And it does not happen to them except through much attrition.
When therefore a fracture or attrition of the bone occurs, then it is necessary that the sick person sits squarely; and let there be a seat in front of him according to equality. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the assistant extend the broken bones, and let the physician equalize them until they are joined in good union.
Then it is necessary that he put on a plaster and a bandage, if there does not occur a hot abscess. Then place a splint upon it according to the quantity of the place; and you have already enclosed it in linen cloth.
But if the fracture is at the lower parts around the belly of the bone, then make a sphere like shape out of cloth; and command the sick person to press upon it with his broken bone; then let it be tied with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather may be applied with the body of the bone; and bind it according to what is necessary.
If however the fracture is external, then it is necessary that you place a splint on top and another splint from below onto the sole of the foot; and let the hand be open and straight. Then apply a bandage, as the hand is turned over; and extend it between the fingers with a ligament.
But if it should happen that a fracture occurs to any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be set according to what is necessary. Then bind it with the sole; and if you wish, place a straight small splint for it so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
But if the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle finger and index finger, or ring finger and little finger, then it should be set and bound with the sound finger that follows it; or they should all be bound according to their sequence and this is better. Or place a small splint on top of it, as we said regarding the thumb.
And consider in your restoration and after the arrangement of a warm poultice; oppose it with what is necessary when something occurs from it, according to how its account is repeated.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is often fractured and it is made known to the senses because it turns towards the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done in this way, that a bandage is applied above the fracture and another bandage below the fracture. And the patient should lie face down.
Then let all the bandages be extended by the attendant to their own side according to equality; and this when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
But if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let the bandage be towards the side of the breastbone so that the extension falls upwards; and another bandage below the fracture.
And so similarly if there is a fracture near the knee, then let there be a bandage near the knee, so that there is extension towards the lower parts.
Then let the doctor equalize the bone with both of his hands, until he reduces it according to the likeness of the natural shape; and let the bone be joined with a good conjunction.
Then it is necessary that you put on a plaster and binding, if there does not happen an abscess to the limb. But if it should happen that there is an abscess in it: then let it alone for several days until the hot abscess subsides, then return to your treatment.
The binding of it however should be like this. It is necessary that you wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, that is, a binding; and double it two or three times; and let there remain an excess from it. Then wrap the leg until you reach the heel at the root of the thigh.
And insert a long thread between the hip and leg near the knee toward the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then revolve over the leg and hip what is left over from the bandage.
Then put on the hip in the place of the fracture itself splints, and put one splint from them over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and hip with soft cloths so that the binding is equal.
Then begin with a strong binding from the middle over the place of the fracture with three or four wrappings. And the more you extend with the bandage from the place of the fracture, let your binding be smaller and softer, and gentler.
Then attend to the two ends of the thread which you have inserted between the thigh and the leg. Therefore tie three ligatures with both, which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts until you reach the heel. And with both, tighten also the ends of the ligatures from the other side so that the ligament is not removed from its place.
Then release the constriction upon it while it remains so that neither corrosion nor abscess nor swelling nor anything like them may happen to the limb. But if anything of that sort should happen. Hurry then with its solution and correct everything which arises from it entirely according to what we have said many times.
But if there is a bone splinter in the bone which pierces then it is necessary that you equalize it if it is possible for you. And if not, cut over it and remove it; and take care of it as we have previously mentioned until it heals.
And when this fracture of the hip is bound up besides adding ligatures to it as we have said in aiding and the arm.
Truly however in its restoration thus fractured infirmity does not happen. And if it is restored alone besides which the other leg is applied to it then it is necessary that he who owns it limps always.
And know that the hip is bound up for fifty days or a little more or less; all of it depends according to diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the knee cap.
Know that a fracture of the knee cap rarely happens but attrition happens to it many times.
But if a fracture should happen to it then it is not anything except either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And it is established over all of that with sense.
And its restoration is such that it equals what has been disrupted from its parts with fingers, until it is assembled and joined according to what is possible from equality and facility and construction.
