Then put a plaster from sesame flour, or mill, with white of egg; and put a round star on top of it if it needs that; and bind it securely with a fitting bandage for that.
Then consider all the arrangements which we have narrated in other fractures: such as a warm poultice, and things similar to it; so that you oppose every accident with what suits it until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called the Tibia; and the other thin, and is called the Fibula.
And it happens to both of these kinds of fractures what happens to the two bones of the arm; and because of that its restoration is done equally as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is one.
If both bones are broken together, the leg is turned in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is turned to the front part.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it lower down; and it is among those things that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and equality, and binding of splints according to that equally.
However it is necessary, if the leg fracture is a foul one, having many shards: that the extension be smaller and gentler in its alleviation by you.
And indeed in the leg there is something from the operation above the arm; and it is that when you equalize the splints and complete all your operation, then take two bands from pine wood which is used in the making of sun-dials which are put under tables; and they are made from spatulae palms; and choose from them those which have a small thickness, and are neither too thin: and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee down.
Then wrap a cloth around each one with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg from one side and the other from the other side; and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie two bandages in three places from the two ends and the middle. For when constriction is prohibited, the leg inclines to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a cradle be made of wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it so that it preserves it from motion.
And indeed it is very necessary that you do this when there is a fracture with a proper wound.
Then consider the leg every day, and strive with the utmost care against a collection of pus, or swelling, or any other things. If therefore anything from those things should happen, then oppose it with what is necessary, until it heals, if God wills.
On the fracture of foot bones and toes.
A fracture does not truly happen to the heel bone.
However, a fracture sometimes happens to the foot bones and rarely to the toes; it does not happen to them except by friction in many positions.
If a fracture happens to the foot bones, and you see those bones rise up over each other, then let the sick person place their foot on prepared ground as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot on that which rises from those bones; then press or tread it down, and equalize it until it returns to its place.
Then put on a plaster and a wad over it; and put a small board under the sole of the foot, which has two flat heads.
Then bind it with a decent bandage after you wrap it with cloths and other things that it needs.
Therefore when three or four days have passed, then untie the ligature. For you will find the bones equalized, whether they are broken or dislocated. Therefore the operation on them is as you see.
But if some fingers are broken, then restore them and equalize them according to what I have told you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint with your finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be slightly wider; then place under the foot this board which I have told you about, and bind it with a proper constriction.
But if what is broken from the bones are two, or three, or more, then place a splint of its own size over each finger, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board under the foot; and let it stand on one head of the board outside the belly of the foot, so that it holds with good retention.
And it behooves you not to forget, but to consider all that I have told you in the remaining fractures from the accidents we mentioned; and oppose every accident with what is necessary.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the breastbone, and a man's clavicle.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, then have her sit square. Then incline her slightly to the back of herself, and let it be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with cotton until she fills it; and let it be made in the vulva like a sphere.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that cotton will come out until it becomes before the vulva like a sphere, and the broken bone will return.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when he wants to urinate, remove the bandage with ease, then let him urinate; and insert it again in the same way that you first introduced it; then let him return to his first pillow.
Let him do this for seven days or so, for the mouth itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a bladder of a goat, and fasten upon its opening a reed tube, and introduce the whole bladder into his vulva. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder is inflated inside the vulva, for the fracture will reunite.
Then fill it with cotton and let it remain for several days, as we have related, until it is healed.
And when the breastbone of a man or woman is broken, then apply in its restoration and in its setting what we have related about the bone of the jaw.
And do not conceal the rectum in these neighboring fractures which rarely occur, especially if someone has some study or use for it.
And understand this book of mine well, for the fracture itself indicates to you in many positions the straight way in its restoration and setting; therefore understand.
But when the penis of a man is broken, take the gullet of a goose, and insert the penis into it. Then place upon it a bandage of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and let it remain for three days or so, and it will be healed, if God wills.
On the restoration of bone fractures when there is a wound.
It is fitting for someone who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the thigh bone, or similar to them, that you hurry and bleed him immediately; if the conditions for bloodletting are confirmed to you, as we have previously stated.
If a wound bleeds, you must hurry to cut it off so that you sprinkle powdered rue upon it. If you do not have rue at hand, use something else.
Then begin to set the fracture on that same day and do not delay it unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then postpone the restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third or fourth day at all, for you would cause bad things to happen to him.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin exposed; then you must try to reduce it and equalize it with your hands with little force and extension.
But if its extension and equalization are not prepared by your hands; then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven or eight fingers; and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And for this reason it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of them according to the quantity he needs for its treatment in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round; and let there be a small thickness so that it is not doubled upon compression on it during the hour of the operation; and let it have sharp extremities, with an edge at its ends. And let its upper part be thick from its middle to its lower parts.
And this is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that the sharp curved extremity of it be placed upon the extremity of the prominent bone; and press it with it together, until when the bone returns and equalizes by some equalization.
Therefore try to equalize the extremities of the fracture to each other.
If the extremities of it are slender and do not receive good acceptance by the instrument; then it is necessary that you cut the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If you are unable to reduce them entirely with what we have said, then cut it with the appropriate instruments which we have described in the chapter on Incisions; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
Then scrape away what remains in the bone from roughness and fine scales.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced and the patient experiences intense, agonizing pain after its reduction, know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do it again. For you confer a great benefit on the patient when you complete the restoration that you are doing to the bone.
Then soak a cloth in black astringent wine. Especially if it is summer. And do not place anything on the wound that contains wax or oil so as not to cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer stars at the hour of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound uncovered; so that you may cut a hole with forceps according to the amount of the wound.
And be careful not to tighten the wound with a fracture. For foolish physicians have often done this and caused death, corruption, or gangrene, that is, suppuration, in their patients. And let your bandage be soft, gentle, different from the bandages of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large and you fear some of the bad things that we have mentioned, and you find pain suspended in the place: then it is not necessary to place stars on it. And make compresses from hard cloths in place of the stars and bind it with them.
But when, after one or two days, you see the wound beginning to fester: then remove the cloth that you placed on it with wine. Then apply linen cloths and ointments with which we are accustomed to healing wounds, such as the tetrapharmacon ointment and those similar to it.
And it behooves you to loosen the ligature. And consider the wound every day in the morning and evening until it consolidates and heals.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared so that pus flows from it to the lower parts with ease.
And if many days pass by the wound and it does not consolidate nor is pus separated from it, then know that there are small bone fragments there. Therefore it is necessary that you inquire into the wound with touch. How many indeed of those fragments are separated, remove them and extract them. And those which are not separated and prick the limb and cause pain to occur, try to cut into them and remove them in every way possible for you to do so.
And if it should happen that the wound develops a suppuration or an ulceration, that is, eschar, or another type of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary that you oppose to each one of them with what suits it from its treatment, of which there was previous mention in its chapter.
And from that on which my speech must stand and to which you should make your mind present when a bone is broken and protrudes above the limb, such as the hip bone, and the femur, and those similar to them from large limbs: it is, that you not presume its attraction, for very often death results from it, but rather let it be until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty days or thirty.
Then therefore care for the wound if it seems to you, and if not, let it be.
On the treatment of Nodulation in the wake of a fracture.
Often this swelling occurs in the course of healing a fracture. Especially that which is near the joints: and it spoils the shape of the limb from it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural operation.
Therefore consider then, if the swelling is recent: administer to it medicines which have astringency, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and those similar to them.
So that you take some of these, or all of them: and prepare them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar: and place them upon the swelling in a compress, and bind them well over it.
And leave the binding, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and repeat another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind over it a sheet of lead somewhat slantwise. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
But if the swelling has already turned to stone, and is tight, and urgent necessity quickly arises to remove it: then make an incision upon its upper part, and cut off the excess that protrudes: or shave it with certain razors until it is removed: and attend to it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment when restoration occurs, and the limb remains after that slender, not according to its nature.
When a fracture of a bone is restored, and the limb remains after that slender, weak: then it does not happen except for many causes. One of which is the multitude of loosening of ligaments and their ligation not as it ought to be: or because of excess tightness of ligatures of the limbs until nourishment is prevented from flowing to the limb: or because of a multitude of unnecessary embrocations at an unfitting time: or because of a scarcity of blood in the body of the sick person, and its weakness.
And its treatment is to feed it soft food and make its body tender until blood multiplies within it. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness upon it.
Then place pitch on the limb so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it. And continually embrocate it with warm water until nourishment flows and it returns to its natural shape.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are rejoined crooked.
When it happens that crookedness and prominence of the broken bone, or a knot, occurs in a limb already rejoined after its healing, and the shape of the limb is marred, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural operation, then you ought not to heed him who thinks it should be broken again.
And there have already been many stupid doctors and setters who did that in our land.
And this operation is truly very blameworthy, leading to death or great fear, so that it may be mortal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary that it be embrocated with warm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as the leaves and root of the marshmallow plant, melilot, and those similar to them. And let a plaster be made for it with a softening plaster, such as well-made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of the marshmallow root and shake it with chicken fat or sesame oil and apply a plaster with it.
Or take plump figs and grind them with dove's dung and similar things which are called in medicine those that lessen consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is loosened with continual, old, and gentle rubbing done with wet hands. And motion of the limb should be given to all parts in every hour.
If, however, the tortuosity has already preceded and become strong, and has been converted into a stone, and necessity has been provoked to cure it with iron: then it is necessary that it be cut above it, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and what overflows from the knot or bone be cut down with fine incisors; and facility be administered in it with solicitude and zeal.
On Speech in Dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of any joint from its place. Therefore it is prohibited from its motion and the figure of the limb is spoiled. And it causes intense pains and vehement injuries to befall the weak one.
Therefore when dislocation happens to anyone, then it is necessary to hurry to its restoration and not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed, and perhaps with it, the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore it is not necessary for it to be moved or extended in the hour of its inflammation. For very often it causes a spasm and painful injuries to happen.
But when that happens, then it is necessary for you to hasten to the phlebotomy of the weak one. Then let him be dismissed until the inflammation subsides.
Then let the limb be embrocated with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced with ease.
And let the whole limb be cared for along with that whose remembrance will come into its place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even according to what preceded in the fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Rarely are the two jaws dislocated unless rarely.
And dislocation of both occurs according to one of two methods. For they are either removed from their places by a small removal, and softened for a short time. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation, until they are softened to the part of the chest, until the saliva of the sick person flows, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
But when its dislocation is small, then it returns in several positions by itself with little effort.
But if it is a complete, total dislocation, then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is for the attendant to hold the head of the sick person: and for the physician to insert the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the jaw of the dislocation between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or he should insert his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is on both sides. And let the remaining fingers of his hands be outside, with which he equalizes.
And instruct the sick person to cleanse his jaws, and open them for eating in every direction. And the physician equalizes the jaw, and drives the jaw until it returns to its place.
But if its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are together: then administer vaporization with warm water, and oil, until an easy reduction of both is made: and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we said.
When therefore they return and are equalized, and the mouth of the sick person is closed, and they are not softened: then put poultices of cloths on them, with cerate that is made from wax: and rose oil.
Then it is bound with ease with soft bandages.
And let the sleep of the sick person be on his back: and let his head stand between two pillows, so that he does not move it to the right and left: nor does he attempt, or is forced, to chew anything: rather let his food be given soft drinks, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is set: then let him eat what he wants, and let it be administered with ease.
He should not work on the operation of his own mouth at eating and drinking, and kissing, until the mandible is healed.
If the reduction of the two mandibles becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some hour and do not return to their places, then fevers and frequent sweats often occur, and perhaps the bowels of the sick person are loosened and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore when you see this, know that it is ruin and very often he to whom this happens dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the clavicle and extremity of the humerus.
The clavicle indeed does not dislocate from the inner side on account of its continuity with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates to the outside and that is declared to the senses.
And its restoration is for the sick person to lie on his back and extend his arms. Then constrict the place with your palm in a strong constriction, for it will return itself.
Then put a plaster on it and compresses and bind it.
The extremities indeed which follow the elbow and are continued with it, do not dislocate except rarely. And if it dislocates on some day then it is necessary that it be reduced and equalized according to what we have said and what is prepared for you.
Then put a plaster on it and compresses and binding. And enjoin the sick person to adhere to quiet and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the humerus is reduced when it is separated even from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus does not dislocate except according to three ways.
One of them is to dislocate it towards the side of the tarsus below. The second is to dislocate it towards the side of the chest. And perhaps it may be dislocated to the upper part of the humerus; and that happens rarely.
And it does not dislocate backwards because of the spatula. And it does not dislocate forwards because of the nerves.
And indeed it most often dislocates and comes out below towards the tarsus; especially in those who have little flesh. Because it goes out quickly in them and goes in quickly.
But in those who have much flesh, it is the opposite of that, that is because it goes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people a blow or a fall and the humerus becomes inflamed with hot inflammation and it is thought because of that, that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore it is necessary for you to test it until you stand upon the certainty of it and then proceed in its treatment.
And dislocation is known when it is below towards the tarsus; and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a sound humerus. For you find a manifest difference between them and you find in the head of the humerus a concavity and under the tarsus at the touch the head of the humerus as if it were an egg itself; and the weak one cannot reduce his hand to his ear nor move it with all motions.
And similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards then you find a manifest touch and it is not concealed.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easily made when it is recent or the weak one is a boy.
And its reduction is to raise the minister's hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under his humerus, and elevate the joint with strength upwards to its place; and let the assistant raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then let him put it lower, for he quickly returns.
But if he does not return through that which we said, and there are many days from the beginning of the dislocation; then it is necessary that the infirm person bathe in hot water, and an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, such as if the roots of althea, and fenugreek, and melilot are decocted in water, and it is administered.
Then let the infirm person be laid on his back; and let a sphere and wool be placed under his humerus, which is equal between softness and firmness.
Then let the physician place his heel on the sphere, and elevate the head of the humerus with force, and draw the hand of the infirm person downwards.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method; and it is that you make a man who is taller than the infirm person be present, and let him stand on the side, and insert his shoulder under the humerus of the infirm person; and elevate the humerus of him upwards until the infirm person is suspended in the air. And let another assistant draw the hand of the infirm person downwards to the belly. But if the infirm person is light; then it is necessary that he be suspended with something else, so that it weighs him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced also according to another method; and it is that a long piece of wood be fixed into the ground, whose head is round in shape; such as the handle of a mortar; which is neither thick nor thin. Then let it be placed under the infirm person after soft cloths are placed on the head of the wood; and let the infirm person stand according to the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be extended downwards on one side, and his body should be extended on the other side with force; for the juncture returns to its place with speed.
But if its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this care: and it is, that you should take a piece of wood whose length is the measure of two arms, and whose width is the measure of four fingers, and whose thickness is the measure of two fingers: to which there should be a round head, so that its entry into the depth of the wound may be eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths should be tied over the round head so that the wood does not injure the weak flesh.
Then let it be placed under the weak limb so that the hand or arm may be extended upon the wood up to the lower parts; and let the wood be tied upon the support and the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then let the arm be placed upon the step of a ladder by its width, and let the hand be extended downwards, and let the rest of the body be left hanging from the other side. For the juncture enters immediately.
When therefore its entry is completed according to whichever way is possible; then it is necessary that you should place under the limb a sphere of equal size from wool. Then put a plaster prepared from the powder of millstone with frankincense and white of egg over the whole shoulder as it revolves from above.
Then bind under the limb a decent bandage; and let the bandage be wound over the plaster from above; and attach his hand to his own neck and let it go, and do not move his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the weak one until the limb is strengthened; for it is quicker towards its healing.
Then a wider bandage should be put on after seven days or five, and he should be raised with motion. If therefore it is hardened and not softened, then it is already healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture that happens to it, or because of some other cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three holes be applied to it, according to what was said previously in the chapter on cautery.
And if this is all done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not confirmed, and the plaster and bandage are put back on it several times, and it does not become firm, and it falls, and it cannot lift itself to the upper parts: then you should know that its nerves which are at the head of the shoulder have already been cut, or stretched, or softened: and you should know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
But the reduction of a dislocation which occurs towards the chest and nipple, or to the back, is done with a push and extension with the hands: until it returns, and the rest of the bandage and care are applied to it, until it is healed.
And if it should happen after healing that there is heaviness in the limb, and slowness in its motion, then let the weak person bathe many times, until that heaviness is eased, and it returns to its first nature.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty also: and indeed it dislocates to all sides, and especially to the front, or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: for it falls under sight, and under touch, according to whichever figure it dislocates. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the sound one, it is declared to you manifestly. For the joint is hollowed, and the arm cannot bend, nor touch its shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately, before hot abscess occurs to him.
For if a hot abscess should occur to him, its reduction becomes difficult and perhaps it does not heal at all, especially when it is toward the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocations and more severe in pain for them, and death often comes with it.
And its restoration when it is from those which are possible to reduce, is that his hand be extended with two hands and his arm be spread out, and the hands of the doctor be above the elbow and below it, and he himself presses the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the base of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or truly the dislocation is forward: then it returns thus, that he doubles his hand with his own hand until he strikes with the base of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
But if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer strong, powerful extension and it is that two assistants extend the arm, and two other assistants hold it also so that it does not move during the extension.
Then let the arm be revolved to every part after it is enveloped with a long doubled cloth over his hand, or a wide bandage.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil so that it may be among those which assist in lubricating the joint with ease. Then he presses the joint with a strong thrust until it returns.
And after its reduction it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it, in which there is astringency and drying with white of egg, and let it be bound with a decent bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the neck of the sick man, and let it rest for several days.
Then let it be unbound. If therefore the union is established in its place, then untie the ligature from it, and let it go.
And if you see that the union is not well tightened, then repeat the plaster and the ligature, and let it go for several days until it tightens. Then untie it.
But if gravity occurs to it after reduction of the union, and the disposition is in motion: then administer moistening in a bath, and subtle friction, and compression, until it is aligned, or place upon the union iliati, that is, the fat tail of a ram. Then it is ligated, and let it go upon it day and night. Then remove it, and introduce it into a bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the union with temperate friction. Then repeat upon it iliati juice once, and a second time, and a third time with entry into the bath, until it is softened.
And if you wish, place upon it cow dung, moist, warmed with butter, and bind above it.
Do that several times. For it is softened, and returns to its own disposition.
On the treatment of dislocation by Albucasis.
Albucasis often dislocates the hand, that is, the union which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Contrary to the other unions. However it is necessary that swiftness be in the reduction of its dislocation in the hour in which it is dislocated, before an abscess forms there: or hot inflammation occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in it is that you place Albucasis's hand of the injured side upon a table, and let an assistant extend his hand: and let the doctor place his palm upon the prominence of the union, and push it until it returns.
However, it is necessary that it be considered, if the palm has already receded to the belly of the hand: then let the patient place the back of his hand upon the table at extension and reduction; and if the dislocation proceeds to the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be intrinsically upon the table, that is, let the inner part of his hand be placed upon the table so that the hand of the physician may fall upon the prominence of the joint.
Therefore if it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster poultice; and dismiss him, and do not touch it further.
For if he does not tolerate it, nor can you effect its reduction after days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains according to its crookedness; and it does not harm the patient anything except that his hand is softened, and he cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are cut or rubbed.
There is therefore no remedy in this except to burn it with a cautery near its location. And perhaps it does not bring about anything.
Therefore when Almahassem does not return, then place upon it a plaster which we have described. Then let it be bound, and dismissed for five days; afterwards let it be loosened and dismissed, and let the hand be exercised.
But if its motion is deprived, and heaviness occurs therein, then soften it with warm water and frequent rubbing until it is softened.
Concerning the Treatment of Dislocations of the Fingers.
When a finger is dislocated from it to any part, or a finger to the back of the palm, or its belly: then extend the finger and press the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it toward the part to which it is dislocated, and dismiss him for two days.
Then suspend him and extend it until the straight part is equal to its length that day.
Therefore when it is night bind it according to the very account and do not cease to loosen it in the day and exercise it with motion and bind it at night.
Do that for several days until it tightens.
And do likewise with it if it is dislocated toward the belly of the hand and bind it to the same part and perform your first operation on it until it is healed.
And do likewise with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the Treatment of Dislocation of the Backbone.
When it happens to someone that the backbone or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure in it because death hastens for the weak one.
And the sign of it is that the excretion of the weak one comes out without volition and he cannot hold it and many times some members are loosened from him or two legs or two arms of him or one of them or a vertebra is removed from its place.
Very often it is removed, because very often the removal happens to four parts.
That therefore which is removed toward the back is called a hump.
The cure for it, however, is that you consider then if the hump occurs from boyhood then there is no cure in it nor healing at all from it.
In that which occurs from a fall or a blow or similar things, the ancients already said modes of treatment in lengthy speech. Most of which does not return with benefit.
I, however, have already shortened it from that which excuses little of it because of its proximity to my intention and by my exposition in which I exposed it and formed its instrument in a different way from that which they showed and exposed.
Therefore, I say that in hunchback which occurs from the front part, there is neither skill nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which happens on both sides also.
And it is not cured from them except that which occurs in the back properly with that which I relate: and it is, that the weak one be extended upon his face upon an equal platform near the wall: and let a wet straw mat be spread under him so that his chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be placed fixed in a hole in the ground, at the extremity of the platform, around his head: and another piece of wood around his feet at the other extremity of the platform: and let an assistant hold the wood, and let it not be fastened in the hole. And let another assistant hold the other one.
Then wind a soft, strong bandage over the head of the weak one, and under his nipples: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood which is by his head: and bind it to it.
Then bind another bandage above his hips, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages together, and bind them to the other wood which is by his feet.
Then let all the assistants extend toward the wood a bandage, and let the two woods not be removed from their places in which they are fixed: nevertheless let them not be fastened, as we said.
And the doctor place his hands upon the spine with force until it returns: or he may place upon it a board. Then he annexes his feet upon the board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arm-spans. And make a hole in the wall of which we said that it is near the weak one, a place into which the extremity of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board over the spine. And let the doctor put his feet on the other extremity. And bind it well until the spine is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a tourniquet which is enclosed with your hand and it is as follows: fix at the head of the sick person on the last step two pieces of wood, each the length of one arm; and at the head of him on the last step two pieces of wood.
Let the length between each piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place where the tourniquet passes; and let all the wood be firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all.
And insert a round piece of wood; and this is the tourniquet in which the ligature revolves in two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity there is a hole in which wood is fixed, the length of which is a palm with that which revolves; and similarly in the two other pieces of wood.
Then tighten the ligatures which you bound on the chest of the sick person with the tourniquet which is at his head; and the ligatures which you bound on his legs with the tourniquet which is at his feet.
Then let an assistant stand by each tourniquet who turns his hand with a handle with which the tourniquet revolves; and the doctor aligns the spine as we have said.
This is the form of the tourniquet which follows.
Then after the spine returns and the place is equalized, it is necessary that you apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then a compress. Afterwards place on the plaster an splint of wood, whose width is three fingers or thereabouts. And its length such a quantity that it takes in the place of the gibbosity and above the sound part of the spine.
Then it is bound with the ligature which we have said. And the weak person shall use delicate nourishment until he is healed.
And if something of a protuberance should remain in the place at the end of healing, then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, with the application of the plate which we have described for a long time. And now a lead plate is applied to that place.
And sometimes a prominence occurs at the last vertebra of the back and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that is the bone which now protrudes. Therefore it is not necessary for an assumption to be made regarding this with this medicine, for it might perhaps cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated jaw.
Know that dislocations of the jaw and shoulder do not occur unless there is a dislocation: nor does what occurs to other joints from a small removal and concavity happen to them.
And the joint of the jaw dislocates according to four parts. Which is because it dislocates anteriorly and posteriorly.
And most often it dislocates inwardly, and rarely dislocates anteriorly and posteriorly.
And the treatment for its dislocation inwards is as follows: when you join the leg of the healthy person with the injured person's leg it is longer; and his knee is more prominent than that of the healthy person, and he cannot bend his foot at the groin; and the place following the groin is swollen with a hot poultice, because the head of the femur has already arrived there.
And the sign of him to whom a dislocation outward occurs is that these accidents are the opposite of those accidents.
And the sign of him to whom a dislocation forward occurs is that he stretches out his healthy leg according to its complement; but he cannot bend it without pain which is in the knee: and if he attempts to walk, he cannot step forward on it; and his urine is retained, and his groin becomes swollen, and at walking there is a stepping by him on his heel.
The sign of him to whom dislocation happens posteriorly is that he does not extend his knee, nor can he bend it forward before bending his groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and his groin is softened, and the head of his femur is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction, however, of this species of dislocation is that you consider then, if the dislocation is old, which has already grown old with him having it. Then if you administer the reduction of it, and it remains according to its own arrangement: then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, one ought not presume a complete recovery for him.
But in him whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four species of dislocation: then hurry to this, so that you wish to join it, and extend it inward and outward, and move it rightward and leftward: perhaps for it will return, and does not need other care.
But if it does not return this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong attendant, and let him extend his leg from the lower part: either with his hands or with a bandage which is tied above his leg above the knee; and another attendant extends his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under his tibialis. Then let him tie with a soft bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the end of the other bandage.
And let his extension be either from the front part of the groin to the part of the furcula, or from the back to the part of the dorsum.
And let the extension of all of them be as one, until the weak person is raised with his body from the ground, and remains hanging.
And this, indeed, species of extension is a common species of the four species.
Therefore if the dislocation returns with what we said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every species that I say about its proper cure.
In its reduction indeed when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the weak one lie supine upon his sound side. Then let him place a bandage upon the root of the thigh, in that which is between the head of the thigh and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the collarbone.
Then let another attendant grasp his strong arms and reduce the thick place of the weak thigh, and extend it outward with a vehement extension. Now indeed it returns to its place.
And this kind is easier than the other kinds of treatment by which this limb is reduced.
But if it is excused over you and does not obey you to enter with this kind of treatment altogether: then it is necessary that two feet of the weak one be bound together with a strong, soft bandage upon the two heels and upon the two knees; and let the length of each be of the quantity of four fingers from its fellow: and let the weak leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the weak one be suspended upon the head of a beam which is in the house; and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arm spans.
Then direct a strong youth to brace the head of the thigh; and let another youth be suspended with the weak one: and let another youth drive, bracing the thigh with strength, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
But in its reduction indeed when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the weak one lie upon a bench, according to what we have related about one who has a hump. And let a bandage be wound upon his weak leg properly and upon his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then a board should be placed in the middle of the footstool, fixed well; upon which wet cloths should be wrapped so as not to hurt the weak person; and let the board be between his thighs, so that he is not drawn down to the lower parts during extension.
Then let every attendant extend to his own part, and the physician equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to reversion, it will be good.
And if not, place upon the board itself, and press it equally as we said in the case of humpbackedness. However, it is necessary that the weak person lie on his sound side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the leg of the weak person be extended quickly; and this is according to the same account on the footstool; and let the physician place the palm of his right hand on the groin of the weak person. Then let him press it with his other hand, and with that pressure let him place it extended downward toward the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the rear: then it is not necessary that the weak person be extended downward, and let him be raised above the ground; nay, it is necessary that he be placed on something hard, as it must be done also when his ankle is dislocated outward, according to what we said about his lying on two ankles; and it is upon his face, and with extended ligaments; according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also upon the place to which the joint goes, when reduction is completed as it should be.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you; and it is that you extend the two legs of the weak person; and when you see them equal, and the weak person draws up his leg and extends it without excuse: then know that the limb has now returned as it should; then join the two thighs together and put on a plaster and bind with bandages, that is, a wheel so that the ankle does not move to any of its parts; and let the weak person rest quietly for three days or four.
Then loosen the bandage and apply a plaster and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in condition, then you will know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore discharge the weak one to walk.
And if you see anything of softening; then return and apply a plaster to it and bind it according to your first binding; and let it remain for three days as well.
Then loosen it and make him delay walking on both for several days until it is well strengthened.
Concerning the dislocation of the knee.
The knee is dislocated according to three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is to say, backwards.
And it does not dislocate forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you order the weak person to apply his leg to his thigh. But if it does not adhere with the thigh; then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all ways of its dislocation is that you make the weak person sit upright so that he now extends his leg if he can upon it and adheres your back to his face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms and put them straight between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with force. And let another attendant extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of restoration is that the leg adheres with the thigh easily, not laboriously.
Then plaster it and make the leg adhere with the thigh. Then bind it together with a splint for three days or four. Then loosen them.
And let the weak one not walk for several days, until he is healed.
But if its reduction is refused to you, then administer strong extension with ligaments, of which narration preceded in the treatment of a sprain, until it returns.
On the Treatment of Dislocation of the Calcaneus.
The calcaneus is sometimes removed with slight movement, and sometimes dislocated according to the complement. And its dislocation occurs, either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the calcaneus swollen, protruding to the side to which it has dislocated.
But the treatment of its removal is easy reduction: and it is to extend it with ease with the hands, and equalize it, until it returns.
But in the treatment of it when it dislocates according to the complement, it is necessary that you make the weak one sit upright: and let a strong attendant hold him very firmly from the posterior part of his back, in the middle of him.
Then hold you with your right hand his foot from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot in the place of the heel.
Then draw the foot toward you with your right hand. Then with the left twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the chest of the foot with the right toward the leg without labor.
Do that twice, as we have described.
Then push the chest of the foot toward the leg in the third time, and you draw the heel.
Therefore if it returns in one time or two according to this manner, and you see the foot equalized, it is good: and if not, then repeat the operation upon it: for it will return.
But if its reduction is prevented from you with what we have described: then make the weak one lie on his back on the ground, and fix a wedge firmly in the ground, so that it falls between his thighs: and you now wrapped a cloth over him, so that it does not injure the weak one.
Then let the assistant take hold of his thigh; afterwards let another assistant extend the foot, either with his hands or with a bandage which he binds above the ankle. Then let every assistant extend himself against the extension of his companion; and let a bolster stand between the two thighs of the sick man, holding him so that his body is not drawn down towards the extension.
Then let the physician equalize the dislocation with his hands, and let another assistant hold the sound leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore when the dislocation returns and it is shown to you that the health of its return has been established, then apply a plaster and bandage, and bind it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you are careful or fearful of the nerves which are above the heel behind, so that the bandage is not tight upon them and injures them.
Then dismiss him for two days or three. If the bandage becomes loosened, tighten it; then untie it on the third or fourth day; and forbid the sick man from walking for forty days. But if he should try to walk before that time, there will be no safety lest the dislocation return upon him and become corrupted so that it does not receive a cure afterward.
But if a hot abscess should occur to him, then it is necessary that you administer it for the quieting of his ailment according to our previous narration in another place concerning its cure and an application, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes and their treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated from them be equalized with a little extension without labor; and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some of the knuckles on the back of the foot, then it is necessary that the sick man place his foot upon a level spot of ground or upon a board; and he stands thus as though walking.
Then you stand and place your foot on that which protrudes from those articulations. Then press that with your foot with force until it returns and you see it now leveled and no longer appearing prominent in the place.
Then place under the belly of his foot a board which will receive the whole foot, to which there are two heads. Then bind it with a decent bandage for three days. Then unbind it and make him rest from walking for many days until it is tightened and secured from displacement.
Concerning the types of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When something of this kind occurs and you attempt its treatment and restoration, death often follows; and therefore it is not necessary that one presume upon the cure of such a thing unless he is prudent in the art from long practice, merciful, pious, slow, neither bold nor presumptuous; and so as to use in the beginning medicines which only allay hot abscesses; and dismiss the sick man to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for safety from perdition with the gentleness of the illness, and in which there appears something of hope. Therefore attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If therefore the limb returns as we desire, then administer a regimen which allays abscesses; and care for the wound with what suits it from drying ointments.
But if the dislocation is with a fracture and separate bone splinters occur, then attempt their removal and do in that what we said in simple illnesses from what preceded in the places of those.
And be careful according to your ability and guard yourself from entering the way of fear; according to what my foregoing preaching has said to you.
For that is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more saving for your body among them in your time.
